Summary 0:
EU rules on marketing communications for collective investment undertakings and UCITS (Regulation (EU) No 346/2013)
EU rules for marketing communications and information on cross-border marketing activities for collective investments in UCITS
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 345/2013 — marketing communications to investors in AIFs, EuVECA managers and EuSEF managers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the marketing communications* and information to be sent to investors of collective investment funds (AIFs) and UCITs.
It aims to improve investor protection by ensuring that marketing communications addressed to investors are identifiable as such and describe the risks and rewards of purchasing units or shares of an AIF or units of a UCITS in an equally prominent manner, and that all information included in marketing communications is fair, clear and not misleading.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
a UCITS authorised in accordance with Article 5 of Directive 2009/65/EC;
a management company* designated by the European Commission for the management of UCITS;
e.g. a management company of a social entrepreneurship fund.
A UCITS management company may be externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form.
EU countries’ competent authorities must ensure that:
all marketing communications, including on-line communications, are clearly identifiable as AIFMs.
they must be clearly identifiable;
the fees and charges are published on the websites of the competent authorities’ websites;
they are publicly disclosed to investors;
in order to enhance transparency.
the communication must contain specific information about the AIF’s’ assets and liabilities.
The Commission must publish a report on reverse solicitation and demand on the own initiative of an investor, specifying the extent of that form of subscription to funds, its geographical distribution including in non-EU countries, and its impact on the passporting regime.
In addition, it must publish on its website:
the number of investors who have subscribed to funds and the number of funds that have been subscribed to the funds, and
the extent of the geographical distribution of funds, as well as the impact on passporting.
Directive 2011/61/EU on pre-marketing (see summary) lays down the rules for this type of marketing communication.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 August 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Investment Bank (EIB) is one of the largest investors in collective investments* in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘UCITS’ on the EIB's website.
* KEY TERMS
* UCITS: a collective investment fund (i.e. a fund that is managed and managed by a company).
* AIF: a fund which is managed by an individual or a group of individuals or companies.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Delegated regulation (EU), Euratom No 344/2013 of 3 August 2013 laying down rules concerning marketing communications (OJ L 183, 4.8.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom No 345 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 1:
Ensuring the effective enforcement of EU harmonisation legislation on consumer products (Regulation (EU) 2016/852)
Ensuing the effective enforcing of EU legislation on Consumer products (Council Regulation (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 852/2016 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the making available of products on the Union market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the general safety requirements for all consumer products in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the legislation is correctly enforced.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all manufactured products other than food, feed, medicinal products for human and veterinary use, living plants and animals, products of human origin and products of plants and animal relating directly to their future reproduction.
It does not apply to:
live animals;
animal feed;
medicinal products;
living plants;
products of plants or animals.
EU harmonisation
It applies to the following products:
food, feed and medicinal products intended for human or veterinary use;
live plants, animals and products directly related to their reproduction;
plants and animals intended for use as food or feed.
Importers, importers, distributors and authorised representatives must ensure that:
the products they place on the market comply with the legislation;
they act responsibly and in full accordance with the legal requirements applicable when placing or making products available on the EU market;
the authorities in charge of the control on products entering the market have effective investigative and enforcement powers.
In addition, the regulation sets out the responsibilities of market surveillance authorities and the authorities responsible for the control of the EU’s e-commerce market.
Enforcement of the legislation
EU countries are responsible for enforcing the legislation. They must:
ensure that products that are subject to the legislation are not placed on the European market without prior authorisation from the competent authorities;
take all necessary measures to prevent, detect, investigate and penalise those who fail to comply;
implement effective sanctions for those who knowingly infringe the legislation, such as fines and confiscation of their assets;
establish a system for the exchange of information on dangerous products through the Rapid Information Exchange System (RAPEX);
establish and maintain a register of authorised representatives in the EU.
Evaluation of the regulation
In 2016, the European Commission published a report on the implementation of the Regulation. It found that the regulation had been largely effective in preventing, detecting and penalising non-compliant and unsafe products. However, it noted that there were still some areas where it could be improved. The report called on the Commission to take further action.
The Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures, including:
introducing measures to improve the transparency of the RAPEX;
ensuring that EU countries’ competent authorities have access to the information they need to identify non-compliance;
developing a common set of investigative powers, allowing for enhanced cooperation between the authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘The harmonisation and enforcement of the law on consumer goods (European Commission).’
KEY TERMS
* Importers: any person or organisation that is responsible for placing, making available or putting into circulation a product.
* Distributor: any entity that is involved in the supply chain of a product, including the manufacturer, importer, authorised representative and authorised representative.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 2:
Online retransmission of television and radio programmes — rules on the exercise of copyright and related rights (Directive 2014/26/EU)
Online retranmission of televisual programmes — rights on the internet
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/24/EU on the licensing of rights for online retransmissions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the legal framework for broadcasting organisations’ rights to online services that are ancillary to their own broadcasts of television or radio programmes.
It sets out the rules for the licensing and management of the rights to these services.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
online services that provide access to programmes broadcast by a broadcasting organisation;
online retranscasts of television programmes originating in other EU countries;
ancillary online services which give access to material that enriches or otherwise expands the content of the broadcasts.
In order to make their online services available across borders, broadcasting organisations need to have the required rights to works and other protected subject matter for all the relevant territories of the EU, which further increases the complexity of the clearance of such rights.
The legislation applies to all broadcasting organisations that provide online services. It does not apply to the broadcasting organisation’s own productions, which are understood as productions carried out by the organisation with the use of its own resources, but excluding productions commissioned by the broadcasting organisations to producers that are independent from the organisation and co-productions.
the rights in phonograms or performances.
Rights to the works or other protected subjects used in the programmes, for the online services covered by the directive.
Licensing of rights
The law applies to broadcasting organisations and operators of online services:
operators of broadcasting organisations providing online services;
the collective management organisations of distributors of retransmitters’ services; and
collective management organisations (collective managing organisations) which manage the collective management rights of distributors.
When determining reasonable licensing terms, including the license fee, for a retransmitting in accordance with Directive 2014/27/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council, the economic value of the right to use the rights in trade and the value allocated to the means of the transmission should be taken into account when determining the amount of payment for the rights subject to the country of origin principle, such as methods based on the revenues generated by the online service.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise the rules governing the broadcasting sector, including:
the directive on the rights of broadcasters;
rules on collective management of rights;
measures to improve cooperation between collective management bodies and distributors;
changes to the rules on licensing rights for retransmisses.
For more information, see:
‘Directive on the broadcasting of online programmes’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 3:
The.eu Top Level Domain Name Registry (TLD) (until 31 December 2020)
The.EU Top Level DNS Registry (TFD)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 on the establishment of the.eu TLD
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the TLD’s registry, which is responsible for the organisation, administration and management of the top-level domain (tLD) of the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules and procedures for the establishment, management and operation of the registry.
KEY POINTS
The TLD is an essential component of the hierarchical structure of the domain name system (DNS) which ensure an interoperable system of unique identifiers, available throughout the world, on any application and any network.
The regulation establishes a registry for the.EU TLD. The registry is managed by the European Commission and the European Internet Agency (Interpol).
The Commission designates a registry to manage the.eU TLD on the basis of a non-discriminatory selection procedure, taking account of the relevant principles adopted by the Governmental Advisory Committee (GAC).
A.eu Multistakeholder Advisory Group is set up with the role of advising the Commission in order to strengthen and widen input into the good governance of the Registry.
Registry responsibilities
The registry must:
ensure the timely identification of abusive registrations of domain names and, where necessary, cooperate with competent authorities and other public bodies relevant to cybersecurity and information security which are specifically involved in the fight against such registrations, such as national computer emergency response teams (CERTs);
be audited at its own expense at least every 2 years by an independent body with the purpose of confirming, by means of a conformity assessment report, that the registry complies with the requirements laid down in this regulation.
It must ensure that:
the registrants are:
an EU citizen, independently of their place of residence;
a natural person who is not a EU citizen and who is a resident of an EU country;
an undertaking established in the EU; and
an organisation that is established without prejudice to the application of national law.
If a registrant is found by a court to be defamatory, racist or contrary to public policy or public security under EU law, that domain name shall be blocked by the registry and subsequently transferred to another party.
A registrant may also be revoked, and where necessary subsequently transferred, following an appropriate ADR or judicial procedure, where that name is identical or confusingly similar to a name in respect of which a right is established by EU or national law and where it:
has been registered by its holder without rights or legitimate interest in the name; or
is being registered or is being used in bad faith.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures to modernise and modernise the EU TLD, including:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2020/852 — the establishment by the Commission of a new TLD for the EU (see summary);
Regulating (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 — the creation of a TLD name to be assigned to the European Parliament and to the Council (See summary); and
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2019/854 — the designation of a second TLD to the EU.
For more information, see:
‘The new.eu top level domain’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Top level domain name: a top level suffix that is used to designate, manage and operate the top level of the internet. It consists of a set of prefixes, suffixes and suffixes that are used to identify the top levels of the Internet. The suffixes are: prefixes (e.g. ‘1’, ‘2’ and ‘3’); suffixes (i.e. suffixes).
* TLD: a suffix or suffix that has been assigned by the International Commission for the Registration of Top Level Directives (ICANN).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 23 November 2013 on the Establishment of a Top Level Registry (OJ L 347, 24.11.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 552 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 4:
Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) 2021-2027 — financial support for trans-European transport infrastructure projects
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the European Connecting European Facility (the ‘CEF’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU) Cohesion Fund to support projects in the field of transport, energy and digital infrastructure.
It aims to accelerate investment in these fields, while increasing legal certainty and respecting the principle of technological neutrality.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The CEF is designed to support the development of trans-border transport infrastructure (TEN-T) and energy infrastructure. It aims to improve the free movement of persons, goods, capital and services between the EU’s 28 Member States. It also aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (GHG) and improve the efficiency of transport networks.
Eligible projects
The regulation sets out a list of projects that are eligible for funding from the CEF. These include:
trans-border energy infrastructure projects (e.g. smart grids, smart grids and smart grids),
transport infrastructure projects,
smart grids,
energy efficiency projects, and
energy storage projects.
Projects eligible for CEF funding
The Regulation sets out the categories of projects eligible for financing:
energy infrastructure projects: projects that aim to reduce GHG emissions from transport infrastructure;
transportation infrastructure projects that contribute to reducing CO2 emissions;
carbon efficiency projects;
the development of smart grids;
smart grid projects
energy-related projects
connected and automated mobility.
The European Commission has adopted guidelines for the implementation of the Connecting Europe Fund. These guidelines cover the following:
cross-border projects in transport infrastructure,
transit infrastructure and infrastructure projects in energy;
connecting and autonomous mobility projects
transmission infrastructure projects and initiatives in the energy and transport sectors.
In addition, the Commission has also adopted guidelines on how to identify projects that could be eligible for EU funding.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
Transit infrastructure (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trans-European Network: a network of high-voltage, high-speed rail, road, air and sea transport networks that connect all the Member States of the European Union.
* Cross-border infrastructure: a transport infrastructure network that links all the countries in the EU, including remote and outermost regions.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 of 21 December 2018 establishing the European Cohesion Facility 2021–2027.

Summary 5:
Terrorist content: hosting service providers’ liability (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Terrorist material: hosting site providers‘ liability (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the hosting of terrorist content
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the detection and removal of terrorist material on the internet.
It applies to all providers of relevant services offered in the EU, irrespective of the country of their main establishment.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to providers of information society services which store and disseminate to the public information and material provided by a user of the service on request.
Providers of services, such as cloud infrastructure, which are provided at the request of parties other than the content providers and only indirectly benefit the latter, should not be covered by this regulation.
Hosting service providers must:
ensure that the competent authority of an EU country issues a removal order to a hosting service provider within 12 hours of receiving a request for such an order;
provide a written record of the order, including the accuracy of the date and the time of sending and receipt thereof.
The competent authority must ensure that the order is carried out in accordance with the law of the EU country in which it was issued.
In addition, the competent authorities must take into account:
the nature and wording of the statements;
the context in which the statements were made and their potential to lead to harmful consequences in respect of the security and safety of persons; and
the content of the content.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure the protection of the personal data of the users of the hosting service.
A hosting service service provider may be subject to liability for terrorist content if it:
has a substantial connection to the EU;
is used by a significant number of users in one or more EU countries, or its activities are targeted towards one EU country.
However, this regulation does not affect the powers of national authorities and courts to establish the liability of hosting services providers where the conditions set out in Directive 2000/31/EC for liability exemption are not met.
Where a host service provider fails to comply with the regulation’s requirements, it may be liable for criminal offences, including:
failure to comply in the event of a criminal offence; or
fail to cooperate in the investigation and prosecution of criminal offences.
Definitions of ‘terrorist content’ and ‘hosting service provider’
The term ‘terrorism’ is used in the regulation to refer to:
terrorist material;
information society services;
cloud infrastructure;
internet service providers;
web hosting services.
Exemptions from the regulation include:
providers of domain name systems (DNS), payment or distributed denial of service (DdoS) protection services, as well as providers of content-related services, which do not fall outside the scope of this regulation;
service providers that do not directly benefit from the content of a terrorist material, but have a direct or indirect effect on the dissemination of such material.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Terrorist’ on the European Commission's website.
For more information, see:
‘Terrorism on the EU's website’.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation on the implementation of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2017 of 23 December 2017 on the sharing of information on terrorist content (OJ L 127, 24.12.2017, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 552 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 (FPR&I 2021)
Framework Program for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/2027 establishing the framework programme for research and innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the European Union (EU) to invest in research and development (R&D) in line with the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (the '2030 Agenda'), the SDGs and the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote the creation, better diffusion and transfer of high-quality and excellent knowledge and technologies in the EU;
contribute to full engagement of the talent pool, facilitate collaborative links and strengthen the impact of R&I in developing, supporting and implementing the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The framework programme covers the period 2021–2027.
The programme’s main objectives are:
to:
increase the number of researchers and technological innovators in Europe;
improve the quality of research and technological innovation;
to contribute to the creation of sustainable jobs and growth in Europe.
Programmes
The Framework Programme is divided into 4 thematic areas:
research and innovation (R & I);
science and technology (S&T);
culture and creative sectors (C & C);
industrial competitiveness (ICT);
environmental and sustainable development (SDGs).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has committed to investing at least €1.5 billion in R&D over the period 2020–2025.
For more information, see:
‘Horizon Europe’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Research and innovation: the transfer of knowledge, skills and technologies between the public and private sectors.
ACT
Council Resolution of 21 December 2021 establishing the Framework Programme for research & innovation 2021-2020.
REFERENCES

Summary 7:
Funding for the European Union’s space programme (EU-SAP)
Funding For the EU’SAP
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 laying down the rules for the implementation of the EU Space Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures for the financing of the programme.
It aims to support the creation, growth, and development of the entire space industry in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers:
research and innovation in the field of space;
development of infrastructure in the space sector;
the use and uptake of the data, information and services provided, as well as the development of downstream applications based on those data.
Objectives
To achieve the objectives of freedom of action, independence and security, it is essential that the EU benefits from an autonomous access to space and is able to use it safely.
To this end, the programme aims to:
increase capacity in designing, building and operating its own systems;
encourage the participation of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), start-ups and innovative businesses in the development and operation of space-related infrastructure;
promote cooperation between the EU and non-EU countries in the area of space research and development;
support international cooperation and to collaborate in relevant sectoral bodies of the United Nations (UN).
In order to achieve these objectives, the European Commission must:
establish and implement the budget through the procurement, prizes and indirect implementation procedures;
provide for checks in particular on the financial actors involved in the programme;
ensure that the programme is financed by the EU budget.
In addition, the Commission must promote synergies with other related EU programmes and financial instruments, which would allow, where possible, use of access to risk finance, innovation partnerships, and cumulative or blended funding.
Infrastructure dedicated to the programme could require additional research and innovation, which could be supported under Horizon Europe, aiming for coherence with activities in this domain by ESA.
Exemptions
The European Commission may exclude from the scope of the regulation:
the participation of the European Space Agency (ESA);
the European Union Agency for Research and Innovation (R&I); and
the EU Agency for Fundamental Rights (FRA).
Financing
The Programme is financed in line with the general EU budget for the period 2019-2023.
The Commission may, where appropriate, use the resources of other EU programmes, such as Horizon Europe and the European Investment Bank (EIB), to supplement the budget of the Space Programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘The European Union's space programme’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егарскабраца

Successive amendments to Regulation (E) No 2596/2012 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Programme (EDI) 2021-2027 — financial framework for the period 2020-2028
European Digital Investment Programme (EIDP) 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2093 establishing the European Digital Innovation programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s first multiannual digital innovation programme for a period of 7 years from 2020 to 2027.
It aims to support the development and deployment of advanced digital technologies by industry, in particular by SMEs and by other entities that employ up to 3 000 people (mid-caps), by public organisations and by the academic community.
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to:
promote the adoption of advanced technologies in the fields of information and communication technology (ICT) and artificial intelligence (AI) by industry;
facilitate the sharing of best practices and best practices in the field of digital innovation;
support the development of innovative financial instruments and mechanisms that involve the EU budget, with respect to their contribution towards achieving EU objectives, both as regards initial expectations and the end results.
The objectives of the programme are:
to improve the EU's digital capacities and to address market failures or sub-optimal investment situations, in a proportionate manner, without duplicating or crowding out private financing, and should provide clear European added value;
to increase investment and develop stronger synergies between EU and non-EU countries’ policies in the areas covered by the programme.
To achieve these objectives, the programme is funded by the European Union budget.
to support the creation of European digital innovation hubs (e.
the creation of a network of EIDP digital innovation centres in each EU Member State.
A central role in the implementation of the Programme is attributed to European digital Innovation Hubs, which should stimulate the broad adoption of innovative technologies in industry. The Commission must take the utmost account of the opinion of each Member State before selecting a European digitalisation hub in the territory of that Member State, in line with the rules of the Digitising European Industry Initiative (see summary).
The Commission must also take into account the views of the Member States before selecting the European Innovation Hub.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

It entered into force on 1 January 2021.

Summary 9:
Sustainable internal market: EU SME programme (2013-2020)
Sustainable Internal market: U2S Programme (2013 - 2020)
This European Union (EU) programme aims to improve the competitiveness, capacity building and sustainability of enterprises, especially small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
ACT
Council Decision 2013/852/EU of 25 May 2013 establishing the European Union Small and Medium-sized Enterprises Programme.
SUMMARY
The European Union SME Programme is one of the EU’s major achievements and its best asset in an increasingly global world, as well as being a core element in achieving the green and digital transformation towards a sustainable economy, in response to the increasing pressures of climate change.
The programme is based on principles of fairness, transparency and mutual trust. It aims to promote the correct and full implementation by the Member States of the Union legal framework for anti-money laundering and countering terrorism financing and the development of future policies to address new challenges in those fields.
In addition, the programme is designed to improve SMEs’ access to finance and to provide them with a more flexible, transparent, simplified and agile framework to finance activities aiming to achieve a well-functioning sustainable internal market.
Objectives
The main objectives of the programme are to:
foster the competitiveness and capacity building of enterprises;
improve the sustainability of SMEs, especially SMEs operating in the tourism sector;
facilitate the access to financing for SMEs;
promote SMEs to the European single market;
strengthen cooperation between Member States and their authorities and courts;
ensure the proper implementation of the legislation underpinning the proper functioning of the internal market; and
improve cooperation between the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council.
Evaluation and implementation
To achieve its objectives, the Programme will assess whether the existing legislation is fit for purpose and, when new risks emerge, identify potential areas for action. It will also assess whether it is possible to adapt the programme to the needs of the modern economy and to the changing needs of consumers, businesses and employees.
It will also examine the possibility of extending the scope of the Programme to cover new sectors, such as e-commerce and the digital economy.
Financing
The Programme will provide financial support to SMEs in the following areas:
the creation of a European Single Market Information System (SOMIS);
the development of the European Single Digital Gateway (ESG); and
the establishment of the Your Europe Advice, SOLVIT, the Internal Market Information system and the Single Market Scoreboard. The programme will also provide support for the development, production and dissemination of European statistics pursuant to Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 on the European Statistical Programme (ESP).
Sustainability
The aim of this programme is to promote:
a sustainable and inclusive internal market for all citizens and businesses;
a high level of protection for consumers and businesses when goods and services are imported from economic operators based in non-EU countries, including when they are sold online;
access to finance for enterprises, particularly SMEs engaged in services;
encouraging SMEs and social partners to take advantage of the opportunities offered by the programme;
fostering the growth and diversification of the SME sector, especially through the creation of new SMEs with a view to diversifying their sources of income;
supporting SMEs that are experiencing difficulties in accessing finance;
enhancing the capacity of micro-enterprises;
providing support to micro-entrepreneurs and SMEs which are facing difficulties in access to financial resources;
raising awareness of the importance of financial instruments and the need to diversify their financial resources.

Summary 10:
Firearms and essential components — traceability and protection of firearms and their essential components (Directive 2014/34/EU)
Firearms — traceable and protection against fire hazards
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2014/35/EU on the tracing of firearms, essential components and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure the traceability of all firearms* and the protection of the EU’s external borders.
It codifies and repeals Directive 1999/87/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
firearms classified in the following categories:
category A: rifles, shotguns, pistols, revolvers and assault rifles;
category B: shotguns, assault rifles, pistols and rifles with a calibre of.22 or smaller, unless they have been converted from automatic firearms.
General rules
EU countries may authorise individuals to acquire and possess firearms in category A for historical, cultural, scientific, technical, educational or heritage purposes, provided that such museums and collectors demonstrate, prior to being granted such an authorisation, that they have taken the necessary measures to address any risks to public security or public order, including by way of proper storage.
Dealers and brokers may not be prevented from handling firearms in categories A, B and C, except in cases where their handling is necessary for the purposes of deactivation or conversion, or where otherwise permitted under this directive.
The activities of a dealer include not only the manufacturing but also the modification or conversion of a firearm, such as the shortening of a complete firearm, leading to a change in their category or subcategory.
In addition, the activities of dealers and brokers must not be considered to be activities that only a dealer would be permitted to undertake.
All firearms or their components must be marked with a clear, permanent and unique marking and registered in the data-filing systems of the Member States.
National authorities must be able to trace the firearms and the essential components after the destruction of the firearms or essential components concerned.
To facilitate their free movement, all firearms must be stamped with a clearly visible, permanent, unique and easily identifiable identification number.
Member States must also ensure that:
all firearms and ammunition classified in category B are marked and registered with the national data-processing systems;
they are registered with national authorities for a period of 30 years after their destruction;
the data are retained for the purpose of administrative and criminal proceedings for up to 30 years.
Authorisations are valid for a maximum period of 10 years after the death of the holder of the firearm or essential component concerned. They may be renewed for an additional period of 5 years if they are necessary for customs proceedings or for the enforcement of criminal law.
Exceptions to the above rules include:
museums and collectors may be allowed to acquire, possess and dispose of firearms in the category A when they are:
necessary for historical or cultural purposes;
in the context of voluntary military training provided under national law;
for the preservation, restoration or restoration of historical, scientific or technical, technical or heritage objects;
when they are part of a collection or exhibition;
on the basis of a request from a museum or collector.
Ammunition is not covered by the directive unless it has been converted into a semi-automatic firearm by the action of a combustible propellant, and the Commission adopts technical specifications in order to ensure that they cannot be so converted.
Imports and exports of firearms are covered by Directive 2014/36/EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
    дарскабарка башка  гракта  
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
  бългандранка
For more information, see:
‘Firearms Directive’ on the European Commission's website.

Council Directive 2013/33/EU of 21 December 2013 on the traceable traceability, protection and security of firearms (codified version) (OJ L 183, 29.12.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 11:
Maritime National Single Window (EMSWe)
Maritime Network of Single Windows (EMSW)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of a maritime national single window
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a single maritime reporting system for port calls.
It aims to reduce the administrative burden for shipping companies by simplifying and harmonising the reporting procedures.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the establishment and operation of the maritime maritime single window.
EU countries are responsible for the operation of their maritime National Single Windows.
The European Commission is responsible for deploying, maintaining and employing the harmonised interface module and the related harmonised elements of the national single windows.
National authorities must ensure that:
the information provided by shipping companies is in a language that is easily understood by the public;
the data sets can be reported to each other in the same way;
national authorities must provide clear instructions on the processes and technical requirements related to the use of the single window;
information and support are provided to shipping companies via easily accessible and user-friendly national websites with common ‘look and feel’ standards.
Reporting obligations
The national authorities must:
ensure that the information provided through the maritime single windows is:
inform the European Commission of the information that is required by port calls;
provide the Commission with a list of the port services listed in Article 1(2) of the European Parliament and of the Council’s Regulation on the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) (see summary).
The Commission may amend the data set on the basis of reporting obligations contained in their national legislation and requirements.
In addition, the Commission may:
request additional data elements to be added to the maritime NMSW data set;
request the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and the Committee of the Regions to adopt measures to improve the efficiency of the reporting system;
assist the Commission in the preparation of implementing measures for the implementation of the regulation.
Implementing powers
The implementing powers conferred on the Commission are:
to lay down the functional and technical specifications, quality control mechanisms and procedures for deploying the integrated reporting interface module;
to establish, maintain and employ the harmonisation interface module.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
The maritime transport sector is the backbone of trade and communications within and beyond the single market. For the facilitation of maritime transport, and in order to further reduce the burden on shipping companies, the information procedures for fulfilment of the shipping companies’ reporting obligations imposed by EU legal acts, by international legal acts and by national law of EU countries should be further simplified and harmonised and should be technology-neutral.
For more information, see:
‘Maritime Single Window’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Maritime National Window: an electronic reporting system.
* Single Window: a national reporting system that is open to all shipping companies in the European Union (EU).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, 2019/1825 of 12 August 2018 laying down a framework for establishing a maritime maritime national window and laying down implementing powers for its operation.
Successive amendments to Regulation (U, 2018, 2019 and 2021 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 12:
Electronic road tolls — interoperability and the obligation to pay
Electial road toll systems — interoperable and the responsibility of the driver
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/34/EU on the interoperability of electronic road toll* systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
 
It aims to ensure that all road users in the European Union (EU) are able to pay their road toll by means of an electronic vehicle identification number (OBE).
It recasts Directive 2004/36/EC, which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to users of light-duty vehicles equipped with 5,8 GHz microwave technology only.
It does not apply to:
passenger vehicles;
commercial vehicles; or
vehicles not equipped with OBE.
EETS providers are not liable for tolls that their clients have not paid, even if the latter are equipped with an OBE that has been declared to the toll charger as invalidated.
Toll chargers must not request or require from EETS providers any specific technical solutions which could jeopardise interoperability with other EETS domains and with the existing interoperability constituents of the EETS provider.
All OBE user rebates or discounts on tolls offered by a EU country or by a toll charger should be transparent, publicly announced and available under the same conditions to the clients of the providers of EETS.
EU countries are required to compile and publish all important data regarding EETS in publicly available national registers.
A mediation procedure should be established with a view to settling disputes between toll chargers and EET providers during contractual negotiations and in their contractual relationships.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EETS’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* OBE: a radio frequency identification number that allows a vehicle to be identified in the event of a failure to pay a road fee. It can be used to identify the vehicle owner or the driver of the vehicle. It is also used as a means of identifying non-resident offenders to EETS systems and to process their personal data.

Summary 13:
Joint deployment of immigration liaison officers to non-EU countries (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Joint deploying of immigration liaisons to non EU countries (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Council Decision 2019/820/EU on the recast of Council Regulation (EC) No 377/2004 as regards the deployment of liaison officers in the field of immigration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It recasts and repeals Council Decision 2004/926/EC, which had been amended several times.
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries, the European Commission and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Border Guard Agency) in the area of immigration.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to:
ensure the effective implementation of all aspects of EU policies on immigration;
promote operational cooperation and information sharing between Member States, as well as to respond to needs at EU level, as defined by the Steering Board of the European Union (TFEU);
improve the effectiveness of the network of EU liaison officers deployed to third countries to implement EU priorities more effectively;
optimise the utilisation of the work of EU agencies and other EU instruments or structures in order to implement the EU migration policy more effectively.
The regulation repeals and replaces Council Decision 2000/365/EC (see summary).
It does not apply to the United Kingdom (1) and Ireland (1 October 2018).
In addition, it repeals:
Decision 2008/149/JHA of the Council on the joint deployment of EU officers to the Schengen area of cooperation;
Regulation 2008/148/2008/149 of the Commission on the establishment of an information exchange platform for the use of the information collected by the liaison officers;
Commission Decision 2008/147/Joint Deployment Regulation (JHA) on the organisation and organisation of the Network of Immigration Liaison Officers (OJL) (JOLA).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of this regulation. It is assisted by the Directorate-General for Migration, Refugees and Migration Affairs.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECAST APPLACE APPLY?
It has applied since 18 February 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
The objective of EU immigration policy is to replace irregular and uncontrolled migratory flows with safe and well-managed pathways, through a comprehensive approach aimed at ensuring, at all stages, the efficient management of migration flows in accordance with Article 2 of Title V of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU.
For more information, see:
EU migration policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: a liaison officer whose role is to facilitate the exchange of information on migration-related issues between the national authorities of the Member States and the competent authorities of non-Member States.
* Border guard officer: an officer whose main task is to ensure that border controls are in place and that the border is secured.

Summary 14:
Biometric identity cards and residence cards for non-EU citizens and their family members who are not nationals of an EU country
Biometric identities and residence permits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/852 — minimum security standards for identity cards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes minimum security and format standards for EU citizens’ identity cards.
It aims to enhance security and to facilitate the exercise of the rights of free movement by EU citizens and family members.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
issue and renew identity cards or passports to their nationals in accordance with national laws;
ensure that these are secure;
verify the authenticity of the documents when EU citizens exercise their right to free movement.
The regulation applies to:
identity cards issued by EU countries to EU citizens who are permanent residents residing in another EU country;
residence cards issued to family members of a EU citizen who are non-nationals;
travel documents issued to non-European Union citizens who do not have the necessary travel documents;
visits and transit documents issued by the Schengen countries.
In addition, EU countries may require EU citizens to register with the relevant authorities.
Issuing and renewal of identity cards, residence cards and travel documents
EU citizens are to be issued and renew their identity cards by their country of residence.
Member States must ensure that:
the identity card is valid for at least 6 months;
the residence card is issued for more than 3 months; and
the travel documents are issued for a maximum of 6 months.
the passport is issued a minimum of 3 years.
Where difficulties are encountered in the collection of biometric identifiers, Member States should ensure that appropriate procedures are in place to respect the dignity of the person concerned.
National authorities must also ensure that, in cases where a verification of the biometric data does not confirm that the document or the identity of its holder is authentic, a compulsory manual check is carried out by qualified staff.
Residence cards must include specific information to ensure that they are identified as such in all Member States.
Family members of EU citizens must be given every reasonable opportunity to prove by other means that they have the right to stay in the EU and are covered by the EU’s freedom of movement. This includes:
being able to prove their identity;
being accompanied by a valid travel document;
having a valid residence permit issued by a Member State;
a valid identity card issued by an EU Member State.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
EU identity cards (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Identity card: a card issued to a person who is a permanent resident of the European Union (EU).
* Passport: a passport issued to an EU citizen.
* Residence card: the same document issued to the same person but issued by another Member State to a family member who is not a resident.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management
Integration of European information systems (EES, VIS, ETIAS, Eurodac, SIS and ECRIS-TCN)
The aim of this Communication is to improve the interoperability of the EU’s information systems.
ACT
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation of the Roadmap to enhance information exchange and information management including interoperability solutions in the Justice and Home Affairs area of 6 June 2016.
SUMMARY
The Communication initiated a process towards achieving interoperability between the EU Information Systems for Security, Border and Migration (ESP), a shared biometric matching service (shared BMS), a common identity repository (CIR) and a multiple-identity detector (MID) as interoperability components.
WHAT DOES THIS COMMUNICATION DO?
The communication establishes a set of interoperability objectives for the European Union (EU) Information System for Security and Border Management (VIS) and the Schengen Information System (Eurodac) for the purposes of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences.
KEY POINTS
The interoperability objective is to:
improve and develop existing EU systems, address information gaps and move towards their interoperability, as well as proposals for compulsory information sharing at EU level, accompanied by the necessary data protection safeguards;
ensure the correct identification of persons present in those systems;
improve the security and border and immigration management of EU citizens;
provide border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities with the necessary information to prevent, detect and investigate terrorist offences;
facilitate the exchange of information between border guards and border control authorities;
strengthen border guards’ and customs officers’ access to travel document data;
increase the speed and efficiency of border control procedures;
reduce administrative burdens for border guards.
In addition, the communication sets out the following objectives:
to improve the security of persons and travel documents held in the EU information system;
to streamline access for the purpose of preventing or detecting terrorist offences and other serious crimes to the EES, the VIS, the ETIAS and EurodACSI, the Europol database of Stolen and Lost Travel Documents (SLTD database), and
to establish the validity of a travel document.
FROM WHEN DOES THE COMMUNITY APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission adopted a roadmap in June 2016 on the strategic priorities for the EU work programme 2017. The roadmap identified interoperability as one of the key objectives of the work programme.
For more information, see:
European information system interoperability (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interoperability: the ability of an information system to be linked to other information systems in order to provide the same information.
* Single identity repository: a database of stolen and lost travel documents.

Summary 16:
Renewable energy: energy from renewable wind energy and energy from biofuels
Resolution of the European Parliament and of the Council on the recast of Directive 2009/28/EC on the promotion of the use of electricity from renewable sources
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Directive (EU) 2018/1999
1.6.2018
-
OJ L 127 of 1.6, 2018
RELATED ACTS
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 amending Directives 2009/29/EC and 2014/34/EU on renewable energy and Directive 2014/35/EU.
Directives 2014/36/EU and 2014-35/EC establishing a framework for renewable energy.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVES?
The renewable energy target for 2030 and the EU’s contribution to that target, including their baseline shares in relation to their national overall targets for 2020, are among the elements which have an overarching importance for the EU's energy and environmental policy.
The directive sets binding national targets on the share of renewable energy in energy consumption and in the transport sector to be met by 2020.
It recasts and repeals the 2009 directive.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that renewable energy is included in the calculation methods used to calculate energy consumption* and in energy markets;
make sure that it is not over-subsidised by feed-in tariffs (i.e.g. feedstock tariffs) and that it does not distort the internal energy market;
allow renewable energy producers to participate in cross-border tendering procedures.
In order to support renewable energy projects in the EU, the directive establishes a financial framework aiming to facilitate investments in renewable energy developments in those Member States, including through financial instruments.
Tender procedures
Tenders must be open to all producers of electricity generated from renewable renewable sources on a non-discriminatory basis. To increase the effectiveness of tendering procedure to minimise overall support costs, tenders should, in principle, exclude small-scale installations from tenders opened to them by other Member States.
National support schemes
Member States must ensure that:
they are open to renewable energy installations located on their territory to tenders open to them;
they take into account the participation of those affected by the projects, in particular local populations;
the cost-benefit ratio, in accordance with EU law relating to the electricity market, is calculated according to the overall cost of deploying renewable energy;
there is no preferential treatment of renewable wind power installations compared to other types of energy sources.
Cooperation
The European Commission and the European Atomic Energy Agency (EAA) are responsible for the implementation of the renewable energy directive. The EAA plays a key role in promoting renewable energy development in the European Union (EU).
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE ENTER INTO FORCE?
It entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has set binding renewable energy targets for 2030.
For more information, see:
‘Renewables and climate change’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
    баранскабрациальна гарка каканда народна (Directive 2009/27/EC)

Summary 17:
Regulation (EU) 2016/679 on the regulation of electronic communications networks and services (e.g. radio spectrum) (recast) (OJ L 304, 29.11.2016, pp. 1-11)
Regulation(EU) 2018/679 — the regulation on the harmonisation of the rules on the use of the radio spectrum by electronic communication networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to harmonise the rules governing radio spectrum* for the electronic communications* market. It aims to:
ensure that the EU’s radio spectrum policy and management is consistent with those of the European Union (EU);
provide a level playing field for all market players;
improve the protection of consumers;
promote a true internal market by tackling regulatory fragmentation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all electronic communications services. It does not cover:
the content of services delivered over e-communications networks, such as broadcasting content, financial services and certain information society services, and is without prejudice to measures taken at EU or national level in respect of such services, in accordance with EU law, in order to promote cultural and linguistic diversity and to ensure the defence of media pluralism;
the provision of web-based and not communications-related content.
The scope of the directive does not include:
access to the internet, and services not covered by this directive;
services that enable communication.
the use of radio spectrum, including the emerging self-use of radio frequencies by new types of networks consisting exclusively of autonomous systems of mobile radio equipment that is connected via wireless links without a central management or centralised network operator.
requirements for radio spectrum management.
conditions for the application of the regulation
the right of the end-user to be paid for a service.
Rights and obligations of service providers and end-users
The regulation applies to the following types of services:
radio-frequency communication services;
internet-based services; and services that enable the exchange of information;
telecommunications-related services.
EU countries must ensure that the regulation does not interfere with the functioning of the internal market and that it does not affect the links existing between them, in particular in the areas of media and cultural diversity, cultural diversity and consumer protection.
In addition, the directive sets out the rights and obligations that service providers are responsible for:
collecting, processing and storing personal data;
ensuring that end-to-end interoperability is achieved;
protecting consumers; and
promoting the free movement of goods and services between the EU and non-EU countries.
Repeal
Directive 2014/53/EU repeals Directive 2002/19/EC and its subsequent amendments. It repeals Directives 2002/20/EC, 2002/21/EC (the ‘recast’) and 2002/22/EC.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 29 November 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission adopted the directive on the basis of Article 52(1) of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU.
For more information, see:
Radio Spectrum (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Radio spectrum: the electromagnetic spectrum that is used to transmit, receive and process radio signals.
* Electronic communications services: any electronic communication service that enables communication. The term ‘service’ includes:
communication services, including radio-frequency (RF) communication services, e-mail services, internet-based internet services, mobile communications services, social media services, financial and information society service, and certain services not included in the scope of this directive.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
  Егандарскаца нарка блака  хакта  баксанка (Council Directive 2014/54/EU) of 29 November 2014 on the recast of the Directive 2001/58/EC relating to the harmonization of the laws of the Member States relating to radio spectrum and to the electronic Communications Network and Services (Recast).
Successive amendments and corrections to the Directive have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption of new heavy-duty vehicles
Monitoring & reporting of heavy duty vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2400 — monitoring and reporting requirements for the monitoring and monitoring of the CO2 emission from new heavy vehicles registered in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the requirements for monitoring and the reporting of the carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions* from and the fuel consumption* of new light commercial vehicles (‘heavy duty vehicles’).
It aims to ensure that the EU’s greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions target is achieved by 2020.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles of categories M1, M2, N1 and N2 with a reference mass that exceeds 2 610 kg and which do not fall within the scope of Regulation (EC) No 715/2007 of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary);
light commercial vehicles of category M3 and N3;
heavy-duty trucks of categories O3 and O4.
EU countries must monitor and report to the European Commission every 2 years the total CO2 emitted and the total fuel consumption by heavy duty vehicle categories.
From 1 January 2019, and for each subsequent calendar year, EU countries must report the data specified in Part A of the regulation to the Commission.
The Commission must:
monitor and report the results of the on-road verification tests carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities;
inform the Commission of the number of vehicles that have been tested;
verify that the data are accurate and that they are consistent with the EU ETS emission allowance targets;
report to the EU of any errors detected;
make available to the public the aerodynamic performance of the vehicles and the air drag value ranges of the relevant components, separate technical units and systems.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that:
the simulation tool operation of the simulation tool is carried out in accordance with the requirements laid down in the annexes to the regulation;
the vehicle is tested on a regular basis;
all vehicles are tested on the same roads in the same calendar year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
From 31 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
Heavy duty vehicles are one of the largest sources of greenhouse gas emissions in the European Union (EU), accounting for over 40 % of total GHG emissions.
For more information, see:
Heavy-duty vehicle emissions (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CO2: the total amount of greenhouse gases emitted by a vehicle in the form of its CO2 and its fuel consumption.
* Fuel consumption: the amount of fuel consumed by the vehicle in relation to its total weight.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 3/2017 of 30 November 2017 laying down the rules for the measurement and reporting obligations of manufacturers for the emission and fuel emissions from and of new vehicles of the light commercial vehicle category M1 (O3) (O2) and N1 (N2) (See summary).
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Commission Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2018/852 of 27 October 2018 laying down rules for monitoring the emission allowances of heavy commercial vehicles and for the verification and correction of the monitored data.
Commission Decision (EU/2018/853 of 27 November 2018 on the harmonisation of the rules on the verification of the emission allowance trading system for the EU (EU ETS) and for certain heavy commercial vehicle categories falling within its scope.

Summary 19:
Protection of the EU’s gas supply in the event of natural gas disruptions
Protecting the EU's gas supply
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — protection of the European Union's gas system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all the necessary measures are taken to safeguard an uninterrupted supply of gas throughout the EU, in particular to protected customers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of the national authorities responsible for the preparation and implementation of the gas supply and the emergency plans.
Preventive action plans and emergency plans
National authorities must:
establish and implement the preventive action plans;
take account of the safe operation of the natural gas system at regional and national levels;
coordinate their actions with other EU countries and the European Commission;
identify the risks to the security of the internal energy market.
Emergency plans
The national authorities must draw up and implement emergency plans to:
ensure the uninterrupted supply to protected consumers;
prepare for and respond to any natural gas supply disruption;
ensuring the protection of public health, safety and security of essential services;
provide information to the public and the media;
assist the Commission in the preparation of the emergency plan;
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the measures to be taken.
Risk-based approach
The European Commission is responsible for preparing and implementing the plans. The Commission is involved in the planning and implementation.
The Commission must consults with the European Network of Transmission System Operators for Gas (ENTSOG) on the risk assessment and risk assessment.
Regional coordination system for gas flows
The Regional Coordination System for Gas, established by ENTSOG and composed of standing expert groups, should be involved in carrying out simulations.
In addition, the Commission should:
conduct a risk-based risk assessment of gas flows in the EU;
cooperate with the regional coordinators;
draw up regional contingency plans and prepare contingency plans for gas supply disruptions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMESSAGE APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  Ебласкабраканский гарода

‘Protected customers’ on the European Agency for Safety and Security at European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
*Protected customer: a natural gas customer who has been guaranteed uninterrupted gas supply by an EU country and is entitled to the same treatment as other customers. It is therefore possible for EU countries to treat, in accordance with that framework, healthcare, essential social care, emergency and security services as solidarity protected customers, including where those services are performed by a public administration.

Summary 20:
Energy labelling of energy-related products (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Energy labeling of energy–related products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 852/2019/EU on energy labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules for the labelling and information sheet* of energy* and non-energy* related products in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the transparency of the EU’s energy market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy-related electrical and electronic products;
space and water heating products, unless they are imported from a non-EU country;
means of transport for persons or persons travelling by means of public transport.
It does not apply to second-hand products.
Labels and information sheets
EU Member States have the power to adopt delegated acts setting out specific product-specific requirements for energy-specific labelling. These must be adopted by the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council. The Commission adopts the delegated acts on the basis of a proposal from the Commission.
The Commission must publish, in the Official Journal, transitional measurement and calculation methods in relation to the product specific requirements. It must also publish, at least once a year, a list of the products covered by the regulation.
Products covered by delegated acts must bear the following information on the label:
the energy efficiency of the product;
the percentage of products sold that are in the top 2 classes of products in each category
the number of products that fall within the top two classes
the total number of units sold in each class
the average energy consumption of each class of product.
Suppliers must provide a printed label together with each unit of a product. The label must indicate the absolute energy consumption in addition to the label scale, in order to allow customers to predict the direct impact of their choices on their energy bills.
In exceptional cases, where technology is expected to develop more rapidly, no products should fall within these 2 classes at the moment of introduction of the newly rescaled label. However, those products should nonetheless be shown on a label in grey.
A supplier must ensure that products that do not fall into one of these 2 categories are placed on the market and that they comply with the delegated act. The supplier must also provide a product information sheet for each of the 2 categories of products. The information sheet must include:
a description of the energy efficiency* of each category of product and the total energy consumption;
a list of all the products that are covered by this delegated act;
an indicative list of further products for which an energy label could be established.
Disputes between suppliers and the Commission must be settled by the Commission, in cooperation with market surveillance authorities and suppliers, through the implementation of the public and compliance parts of the system on market surveillance.
EU countries have the right to introduce new national schemes for the energy label and the information sheet. They must also maintain or introduce new schemes for these schemes.
If a delegated act is not justified under the EU safeguard procedure, the Commission may adopt a national measure to determine whether it is justified or not and for establishing detailed requirements concerning the operational details concerning the product database.

Summary 21:
Protection of personal data in the European Union (EU) — rules on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of their personal data (Regulation (EC) No 596/2004)
Protection in the EU: rules on protecting natural persons
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2004 on data protection
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all EU citizens have the same level of protection when their data are processed.
It sets out the principles of, and rules on protection of, the rights and freedoms of, natural persons.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the processing of data by a natural person in the course of a purely personal or household activity and thus with no connection to a professional or commercial activity;
processing of data subjects in the context of the activities of an establishment of a controller or a processor in an EU country;
processing of data subject who are in the Union by a non-EU country’s controller or processor.
Rights and freedoms
The protection afforded by this regulation should apply to natural persons, whatever their nationality or place of residence. It does not apply to persons who do not live in the country in which the processing takes place.
Processing must be carried out in accordance with this regulation, regardless of whether the processing itself takes place within the EU.
Consent must be given by a freely given, specific, informed and unambiguous indication of consent, including by an oral statement.
The consent must be accompanied by a written statement. This statement must include a description of the purpose of the processing and the reasons for the processing, as well as the conditions under which it is carried out.
Procedures must be in line with the principles laid down in Articles 8 and 9 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU (the ‘Charter’) and Article 16(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU).
EU countries must ensure that:
data subjects who are not established in a EU country are subject to this regulation when it is related to the monitoring of the behaviour of such data subjects, so as to monitor their behaviour in so far as their behaviour takes place inside the EU;
the protection of the personal data of persons who are outside the EU is not restricted or prohibited for reasons connected with their protection of their data;
personal data can be transferred to third countries and international organisations without any restrictions, as long as it complies with their data-protection obligations.
Liability of intermediary service providers
Directive 2000/31/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council lays down the liability rules of intermediaries, in particular of the liability requirements of internet service providers, for the purposes of processing personal data. This regulation also provides a margin of manoeuvre for EU countries to specify its rules, including for processing of special categories of data (‘sensitive data’).
The processing of sensitive data is subject to the same rules as for other types of data, such as those relating to the security of the data and to public security, so that it is within the scope of this regulation.
Implementation of the regulation
The European Commission has adopted implementing acts to implement the regulation. These include:
Implementing Decision (EU), 2016/849/EU on the implementation of Implementing Regulation (EC), No 598/2004, and Implementing Decision 2014/13/EU of the Commission on the application of implementing Decision 2005/11/EC, as amended by Implementing Decisions 2014/14/EU and 2014/15/EU;
Impositioning Decision 2015/852/EU, 2016/922/EU as amended and implementing Implementing Directive 2014/12/EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Personal data: any information concerning an identified or identifiable natural person.
* Processing: the collection, storage, processing, storage and transfer of personal information by a person or a company.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Delegated regulation (EU: 2014/1038/EU) of 23 December 2014 on the harmonisation of the rules of the Protection of the Rights and Freedoms of Natural Persons in respect of the Processing of their Personal Data (OJ L 127, 29.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, EC) No 555/2004 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 22:
Harmonised indices of consumer prices (HICP, HICP-CT, OOH price index) and the house price index (HPI)
Harmonomically-based consumer prices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1176/2011 laying down a common framework for the development, production and dissemination of harmonised indices* of consumer price*
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for developing, producing and disseminating harmonised index* of the harmonised consumer price index*, harmonised price index-CT*, and harmonised household final monetary consumption expenditure* (OHCP)* at EU and national level.
KEY POINTS
The HCP is based on observed prices, which include taxes on products. It is the basis of the European Commission’s macroeconomic imbalance procedure. The HCP-CT and the OOH-CT are based on the price changes and weights of products included in the household final money consumption expenditure.
The European Commission and the European Central Bank (ECB) use these indices in their assessment of price stability in the EU.
HICPs and HICPs-CTs are compiled from statistical units as defined in Council Regulation (EEC) No 696/93.
A ‘Laspeyres-type index’ is defined as:
the price of a product is denoted by p, the price reference period is 0, and the comparison period is t.
An ‘index reference period’ means the period for which the index is set to 100 index points;
a ‘price reference period,’ which is the period to which the average price of all products is set, is set at 0.
National authorities must verify the sources and methods used by EU countries to calculate the indices and should monitor the implementation of the legal framework by Member States.
EU countries must provide Eurostat with all the necessary data, such as transactions, and electronic records of the data they have collected, every 3 years.
Eurostat is empowered to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 10 for the amendment of Annex I of the regulation. These implementing acts concern:
sampling and representativity;
collection and treatment of prices;
replacements and quality adjustment;
index compilation;
revisions;
special indices;
treatment of products in specific areas.
In addition, the Commission must:
monitor the compliance of the Member States with the implementing acts;
make recommendations to the European Statistical System Committee on the application of the Regulation;
assess the suitability of the index for integration into the HCP coverage.
By 31 December 2018, Eurostat must prepare a report which shall address whether the harmonisation requirements for the harmonising indices referred to in Article 4 of this regulation have been met.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
The harmonised EU indices are used in the context of the macroeconomic balance procedure, as established by Article 140 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).
For more information, see:
‘The harmonisation of EU indices’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Laspeyre type index: an index based on prices of goods and services.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (UE) 2011/1176 of 20 June 2011 laying down the common framework of the development and production of harmonized indices of the consumer price, harmonised prices of the goods and the services, harmonized price indices and the House price index at Union and national levels (OJ L 183, 31.6.2011, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, No 1175/2011 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
