Summary 23:
Anti-dumping: rules for the application of the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement (ADAA) and the rules applicable to imports from non-EU countries (Codified Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Anti-Dumped: rules applicable for imports from third countries (codified Regulation)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The regulation codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 852/94, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1994.
The aim of the regulation is to ensure that the EU’s trade policy is consistent with those of its major trading partners.
It sets out the conditions under which provisional duties may be imposed, including conditions concerning provisional duties.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The legislation applies to imports originating from a market economy third country (non-EU country) to the EU.
A product is considered to be dumped if its export price is less than a comparable price for a comparable product, in the ordinary course of trade, as established for the exporting country.
Expiry procedure
The Commission may initiate an investigation into a product if:
the price of the product is below the normal value of the comparable product in the market economy of the importing country;
the prices are artificially low;
there is significant barter trade, or when there are non-commercial processing arrangements.
Procedure
The investigation may be launched within 60 days of the initiation of the investigation.
the Commission may decide to suspend the investigation if it considers that the investigation is necessary to supplement the findings.
Imports may be subject to registration.
If the Commission considers that a product is being dumped, it may impose provisional duties on the import of the same product.
Dumping may be suspended if the price of a similar product is:
below the average normal value for the like product;
not higher than the average price for the similar product for the same period of time;
is being sold in quantities which do not provide for the recovery of all costs within a reasonable time.
In the event of a breach or withdrawal of undertakings, the Commission may impose a provisional duty of up to 9 months. The Commission may also decide to impose additional duties on imports.
Approval of provisional measures
Provisional measures may be adopted by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the Commission. They must be adopted no earlier than 60 days from initiation and no later than 9 months thereafter. They may include:
an adjustment to costs applicable during the investigation period;
an increase in the average costs applicable, under the abovementioned allocation rules, at the end of such a phase, and shall be included at that level, for the period concerned;
a reduction in the weighted average costs referred to in the second subparagraph of paragraph 4.
An advisory procedure must be used for the adoption of the provisional measures given the effects of such measures and their sequential logic in relation to the adoption definitive measures.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Directive 2014/34/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 May 2014 on the application by the European Commission of Regulation (EC) No 982/94 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU of the Agreement on the Protection of the Internal Market against the Illicit Trade of Goods and Services between the Member States and the European Economic Area (OJ L 347, 29.6.2014, pp. 1-8)
Directives 2014/35/EU and 2014/36/EU have been amended several times, most recently by Directive (EU, Euratom) 2019/856.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Dumping’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 24:
Countervailable subsidies: rules for the application of the EU’s anti-subsidy rules
Countervailing subsidies: ways of applying them
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules for applying the rules for implementing the EU Anti-Subsidy Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules governing the application by the European Commission of the rules on countervailable (or ‘subsidies’) subsidies.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 552/97.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all imports of goods from non-EU countries into the EU.
A product is considered to be subsidised if it benefits from a ‘countervailable’ subsidy, defined in Articles 3 and 4 of the regulation.
Subsidies may be subject to countervailing measures only if they are specific, as defined in paragraphs 2, 3, 4 and 4.
The amount of the subsidy must be calculated in terms of the benefit conferred on the recipient which is found to exist during the investigation period for subsidisation. It must not be less than the total value of the total subsidy.
Imports under investigation may be made subject to registration upon importation in order to enable measures to be subsequently applied against such imports.
In addition, the regulation sets out the procedures for the rejection of complaints or the initiation of proceedings.
Procedures for the submission of complaints and the termination of proceedings are laid down by the Commission.
Complaints must be made within 12 months of the beginning of the investigation. They must include:
the name and address of the recipient;
the nature and nature of the beneficiary;
any application fee or other costs necessarily incurred to qualify for, or to obtain, the subsidy;
export taxes, duties or other charges levied on the export of the product to the EU specifically intended to offset the subsidy. The Commission may, where appropriate, refuse to accept a complaint.
If the Commission considers that a complaint has not been properly investigated, it may, under certain conditions, decide to initiate proceedings against the recipient.
Defendants must provide the Commission with all the information necessary to prove that they have not benefited from the subsidy and that they are not liable for any damage caused by it.
Where the Commission finds that the recipient has benefited from a subsidy that is not justified by the facts of the case, it must take appropriate measures. These measures must be effective, proportionate, dissuasive and dissuasive.
EU countries must ensure that the information they provide to the Commission and to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC) is not used as a basis for the decision-making process in the EU and that it is not disclosed to any third party.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU Anti Subsidies Agreement (SAA) is the cornerstone of the system of rules on subsidies which the Commission has adopted since its entry into force.
For more information, see:
‘Countervial subsidies’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Delegation 2017/884 of 23 June 2017 laying down rules for application of Article 3 of the SubsidIES Agreement (OJ L 183, 29.6.2017, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (U) 2017 /852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1082/2014 of the European Central Bank of 11 May 2014 laying down the principles and procedures for applying Article 3(2) of the SAA and repealing Council Regulation (EC) no 1081/2004 (O J L 183 of 30.5.2014, p. 1)

Summary 25:
Financial Facility for the European Economic and Social Fund (FESF) 2020-2021 — recovery and resilience plans for Member States (Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046)
Fundamental principles of the Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the EU’s financial assistance to Member States to implement their economic and social recovery plans for the period 2020-21.
It aims to provide financial support for the implementation of these plans.
KEY POINTS
The Facility is the cornerstone of the European Semester for economic policy coordination (European Semester), which is the framework to identify national reform priorities and monitor their implementation. It aims to support Member States’ efforts to:
recover from the COVID-19 crisis;
improve the resilience of their economies;
reduce inequalities and divergences in the European Union (EU).
The Regulation sets out the specific objectives and targets for the Facility.
The specific objective of the facility is to provide the Member States with financial support with a view to achieving the milestones and targets of reforms and investments set out in their recovery and resiliency plans. These plans must be submitted to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission by 30 June 2022.
To achieve the objectives, the plans must:
address structural weaknesses of Member State economies, and that strengthen the resilience, increase productivity and lead to higher competitiveness of Member States;
promote entrepreneurship, social economy, the development of sustainable infrastructure and transport, and industrialisation and reindustrialisation;
mitigate the impact of the crisis on the economy;
contribute to the transition towards a low-carbon economy.
make the EU more resilient and less dependent by diversifying key supply chains and thereby strengthening the strategic autonomy of the Union alongside an open economy. The plan must also include measures that are relevant for the green and digital transitions.
In addition, the plan must include:
measures to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and the backwardness of the least favoured regions;
reforms and investments that address structural weaknesses in the economy of the most disadvantaged Member States.
Implementation of the plan
The Member States must submit a recovery plan to the Council, to the Parliament and to the Commission every 3 years. The plans must include the following elements:
the objectives of the recovery plan;
the measures to be taken by the Member State concerned to achieve the recovery objectives;
a description of how the plan will be implemented;
measures that will contribute to the achievement of the objectives.
Member States must also:
ensure that the plan is based on sound economic and financial management;
identify the beneficiaries of the financial support;
set out how they will implement the plan and how the financial assistance will be used;
provide the Commission with a list of the beneficiaries and the measures that will be made available to them;
make a report on the progress of the plans and how they are being implemented.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 June 2018 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID–19 crisis had a devastating impact on the EU economy and had a significant impact on growth and jobs. The crisis had severe economic and economic consequences for the most deprived regions of the EU. It led to the creation of a large number of new unemployed people, which had to be reintegrated into the labour market.
For more information, see:
‘Cohesion Fund’ (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EU) No 1046/2018/Euratom of 30 May 2018 establishing the framework of the Financial and Social Facility (OJ L 183, 30.6.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratome) No 1080/2018 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  i.e. Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1182/2014 of the Council of 30 April 2014 establishing the European Social Fund and repealing Regulation (O J L 183/2014, 30 May 2014, p. 1)

Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for EU countries’ efforts to implement reforms
Transitional support for reforms
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1939 establishing a technical support instrument with a view to continuing and enhancing support for Member States in the implementation of reforms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
This regulation establishes the technical support instruments (TSI) for Member State efforts to promote the EU’s economic, social and territorial cohesion by supporting Member States efforts to achieve reforms.
It aims to support the efforts of Member States to:
reform their institutional and administrative capacity and judicial framework;
improve their capacity to effectively address country-specific recommendations and actions related to EU law;
promote social and economic governance processes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out a financial envelope for the instrument, which is to constitute the prime reference amount, within the meaning of the Interinstitutional Agreement of 16 December 2020 between the European Parliament, the Council of the European Union and the European Commission on budgetary discipline, on cooperation in budgetary matters and on sound financial management, as well as on new own resources, including a roadmap towards the introduction of new EU-wide own resources.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. The European Parliament and the Council are responsible for drawing up the cooperation and support plans for the provision of technical support to the Member States.
In addition, the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAFO) has the power to carry out administrative investigations, including on-the-spot checks and inspections, to establish whether there has been fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the EU.
Member States must:
apply for grants of up to 100% of the eligible costs;
ensure that grants are used exclusively for the benefit of the Member State concerned;
implement reforms undertaken at the initiative of the national authorities, including those in the context of economic governance, in particular those effectively addressing the country specific recommendations or actions relating to the implementing of EU law, and reforms in relation to economic adjustment programmes.
EU countries must also:
provide the Commission with information on the progress they have made in implementing reforms;
cooperate with each other and with the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) and the Committee of the Regions (CERC).
The Commission must submit a report to the EU Parliament and to the Council every 3 years on the effectiveness of the TSI.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The TSI is part of the framework for the European Semester for economic policy coordination, the framework that identifies challenges and national reform priorities and monitors the progress towards achieving those priorities.
For more information, see:

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights
Rail passengers' rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 — passenger rights on rail passenger services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the rights of rail passengers to travel by rail in the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC), which had been in force since 2007.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
passengers taking international and domestic journeys;
passenger transport tickets sold by railway undertakings, ticket vendors and tour operators;
travel information to persons with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility.
It does not apply to rail services offered strictly for historic or touristic use.
EU countries may grant exemptions from the application of the regulation to certain services offered for historic and touristic purposes.
The European Commission must consult the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEA) before implementing the regulation.
In addition, the Commission must carry out appropriate consultations during its preparatory work, including at expert level, and that those consultations must be conducted in accordance with the principles laid down in the Interinstitutional Agreement on Better Law on Better Regulation (EU) 2015.
Passengers must be clearly informed whether tickets sold in a single commercial transaction constitute a through-ticket and whether they are valid for more than one journey.
European Union countries must ensure that:
rail ticket vendors, ticket sellers and travel information providers provide passengers with access to such travel information and give them the possibility to make and cancel reservations in order to facilitate rail travel;
the possibility of making and cancelling rail travel arrangements;
railways and platforms selling passenger transport tickets are open to all passengers;
there is no discrimination in the sale of rail tickets on online platforms.
Railway undertakings and station managers must:
provide passengers with information concerning the accessibility of rail services, access conditions of rolling stock and the facilities on board;
ensure that rail services are free of any discrimination on the basis of age, gender, disability, age, sex, ethnic origin, religion, sexual orientation, age of the travelling party, nationality, language, age or disability;
establish joint customer services and complaint-handling mechanisms;
set up national single points of contact to coordinate information and assistance;
make and cancel rail travel reservations.
Exemptions from the regulation
The Commission may, in exceptional cases, grant exemptions to certain railway services, such as those operated by the same railway undertaking. These exemptions are valid until 31 December 2023. The Commission must, however, consult the EU countries before granting these exemptions.
Amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 1307/2013
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendments to the regulation have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 June 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 June 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
 * Passenger transport: any passenger travelling by rail, whether international or domestic.
* Passenger transport ticket: a ticket issued by a railway undertaking for the purpose of travelling on a rail transport service.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебранскаблака граксандрациан на  харка (Regulation No 1370/2007)
-
Successive amendments to Regulation No 1372/2007 (OJ L 127, 30.5.2007, pp. 1-8)

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance for the Western Balkans
Pre–accession aid
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 establishing a dedicated instrument for pre‐accession* assistance to the beneficiaries listed in Annex I for the 2021-2027 period (IPA III) in support of enlargement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union instrument for providing pre–Accession assistance to beneficiaries of the EU’s external action for the period 2021–2027.
It aims to support the transition towards accession for the benefit of the beneficiaries, drawing on the experience of the Member States.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the main objectives of the IPA III.
The aim of the regulation is to:
provide the beneficiaries with financial support to enable them to prepare for the EU enlargement process;
ensure that the beneficiaries are better prepared to address global challenges, such as sustainable development and climate change, and to align with the EU's efforts to address those issues;
support the development of a social market economy in line with the key principles and rights set out in the European Pillar of Social Rights;
promote human rights, democracy and the rule of law in the region;
strengthen gender equality and the empowerment of women and girls in EU external action and increase efforts to reach the minimum standards of performance indicated by the EU Gender Action Plans.
In addition, the regulation also provides for the implementation of the United Nations 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development adopted in September 2015 as a universal agenda to which the EU and its Member States are fully committed and which all beneficiaries have endorsed.
Scope
The IPA III covers the period between 2021 and 2027. The regulation applies to the period from 2021 to 2027 and covers:
the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027;
the duration of the period 2020-2023.
Beneficiaries
The beneficiaries covered by the regulation are:
1.the Western Balkans (Annex I);
2.the countries of the Western Balkan Association of European Neighbourhood States;
3.the Member States of the European Economic Area;
4.
Funding
The Regulation establishes a budget of €2.8 billion for the duration of this period. The budget includes:
a contribution of €1.5 billion from the European Union budget for 2021 to 2021 and €1 billion for 2023;
a grant of €500 million from the EU budget for 2027 to 2023 to the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
a loan of €250 million for the same period from the ERDF to the EU Fund for the Development of Europe (EU Fund for Europe).
The European Commission is responsible for the management of the budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND

Summary 29:
The EU’s development cooperation instrument
The EU's development cooperation instruments
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2016/852 — the European Development Cooperation Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE INITIATION?
The decision establishes the EU's external development cooperation (EDC) instrument.
The objective of the EDC is to support the sustainable economic, social and environmental development of developing countries, with the primary aim of eradicating poverty and achieving sustainable development by 2030. It aims to contribute to achieving this objective by providing 0.7 % of gross national income (GNI) as ODA to social inclusion and human development, including basic social services, such as health, education, nutrition, water, sanitation and hygiene, and social protection, particularly to the most marginalised.
KEY POINTS
The EDC aims to:
support the implementation of the 2030 Agenda and the Addis Ababa Action Agenda;
contribute to the achievement of the European Union's (EU's) common foreign and security policy objectives;
promote democracy, human rights and upholding the rule of law, the stabilisation and strengthening of neighbouring countries and strengthening their resilience, particularly by promoting political, economic and social reforms;
strengthen cooperation between the EU and the African Union (ACP) and the European Neighbourhood Policy (see summary).
The EDC is the cornerstone of the EU development cooperation policy. It contributes to the EU strategy for Africa, which is based on a common approach to the region and the world. It is also the basis for the development cooperation with the United Nations (UN), the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund (IMF).
In addition, the EDC also contributes to: the development of sustainable development in Africa;the stabilisation of the region;the fight against poverty and the sustainable development of the world's least developed countries;the strengthening of international peace, preventing conflicts and strengthening international security.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The instrument entered into force on 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Development cooperation instrument is one of the main instruments of EU external action. It complements the EU external development policy, which aims to promote sustainable development and to contribute towards achieving the EU 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development.
For more information, see:
Development cooperation instruments (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Summary 30:
Whistleblowers’ protection: new rules on EU law breaches and breaches of the EU’s environmental acquis (Directive (EU) 2017/1371)
WhistleBlowers‘ protection: New rules on the protection of whistleblowers’
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2018/1372/EU — the introduction of a whistleblower protection framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that whistleblowers are protected when reporting breaches of EU law that are harmful to the public interest.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all matters not regulated under EU law, including:
the supply of products or the provision of services;
the protection of the financial interests of the European Union (EU);
the implementation of the Treaty establishing the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom Treaty).
It does not apply to:
state aid;
state procurement;
public procurement of defence-related products.
WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2019.
EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
It had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
EU law breaches are a major obstacle to the proper functioning of the internal market and the free movement of goods and services within the EU. This is why it is essential to ensure effective enforcement of EU rules. The directive is part of a package of measures designed to modernise and modernise EU law.
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the enforcement of existing EU rules, in particular in the area of environmental protection.
For more information, see:
EU rules on environmental protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Whistleblower: a person who has been the subject of a breach of the law of an EU country and who has the right to disclose it to the competent authorities.

Summary 31:
European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EUROSURG)
European Border & Coast Guard Association
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — European Border and Cart Guard Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the European Border & Cart Guard Association (EurOSUR) as the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the Member States of the European Union (EBRG).
KEY POINTS
The agency is responsible for the management of operational cooperation at the external borders of the EU countries. Since taking up its responsibilities on 1 May 2005, it has been successful in assisting Member States with implementing the operational aspects of external border management through joint operations and rapid border interventions, risk analysis, information exchange, relations with non-EU countries and the return of irregular migrants.
The regulation establishes a framework for the exchange of information and the operational cooperation between Member States’ national authorities and with the agency. It also establishes a standing corps to provide the necessary capabilities for border surveillance and the carrying out of border checks.
In addition, the regulation sets out a common integrated risk analysis model, to be applied by the agency itself and by Member States.
It also defines the role of Frontex, the European border and coast guard’s liaison officers, in the implementation of the regulation.
FRONtex
The European border control and return agency (Frontex) has been renamed to the EBRG since 1 May 2006. Since its creation, the agency has been responsible for:
the management of the Schengen area;
monitoring of migratory flows towards and within the EU;
the coordination of border surveillance, border checks and border checks;
coordination with the European Commission and with other EU agencies;
exchange of information, including with the Commission;
border surveillance and cross-border border checks with the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR);
the implementation of border control measures;
reintegration of migrants into the labour market;
cooperation with the UNHCR and other international organisations.
EURG has a number of tasks, including:
coordinating border surveillance;
organising border surveillance operations;
carrying out border checks, including border surveillance with the UNHCR;
assisting Member States in their efforts to protect their external borders;
contributing to the effective and sustainable return of migrants;
ensuring the protection of the internal security of the Union;
providing information to the Commission and Member States on border security and border management;
developing and implementing a multiannual strategic policy for European integrated border management, including the development of a European border management strategy.
Risk analysis and situational awareness
The Agency carries out a vulnerability assessment based on objective criteria to assess the capacity and readiness of Member States to face challenges at their borders and to contribute to the standing corps and technical equipment pool. The reports of the liaison officers should form part of the vulnerability assessment.
Where there is a specific and disproportionate challenge at the borders, Member States should immediately refer the Rapid Response Teams (RRTs) to the agency for immediate response. Member States must also inform the EAS and Rapid Response Coordination Centre (RCCs) of any requests for assistance from the Commission. The RCTs are responsible for coordinating the rapid response teams and for the rapid deployment of the ECRs.
Member States should also make their national coordination centres responsible for exchanging information and for cooperation with regard to other components of European integrated Border Management.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
 The European Border Guard Agency was established in May 2005. Since then, its tasks have been expanded with full continuity in all its activities and procedures.
For more information, see:
External border management (European Commission).

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 32:
Information and communications technology (ICT)
Information and Communications Technology (ICT)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1148 on cybersecurity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Network and Information Security Agency (ENISA) as the EU’s central point of information and communication technology policy in the field of cybersecurity.
It sets out the tasks and responsibilities of ENISA.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes ENISA as the European Union centre for information and knowledge on cybersecurity. It is responsible for:
ensuring the security of the ICT products, ICT services and ICT processes of the EU;
providing advice and expertise to EU countries and the public sector on cybersecurity;
facilitating the exchange of best practices between EU countries;
supporting operational cooperation among EU countries, where requested by one or more of them, in the handling of incidents from a technical perspective;
coordinating vulnerability disclosure policies.
ENISA is responsible, in particular, for the development and updating of cybersecurity strategies at EU level and, upon request, at EU country level, and should promote the dissemination of such strategies and follow the progress of their implementation.
In addition, the regulation establishes the role of the European Cybercrime Centre (CERT), which assists ENISA in its work. CERT provides advice on cybersecurity to the European Commission, Europol, Eurojust and Eurojust, as well as to other EU agencies and bodies.
FROM WHEN DOES ENISA ENTER INTO FORCE?
  
It entered into force on 1 January 2016.
 
The new regulation applies from 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The ENISA has been operating since 1 January 2015.

Summary 33:
Statistical integrity through respect for the principles of the European Statistics Code of Practice: Gross National Income (GNI) (ESA 2010) (European Statistical System Committee (ESSC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 on the European System of Accounts (ESA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European system of accounts for the calculation of gross national income*.
It aims to ensure the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the EU’s statistics.
It repeals Council Decision 2007/436/EC, Euratom.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with an inventory of the sources and methods used to produce GNI aggregates and their components in accordance with ESA 2010. To facilitate comparable analyses of compliance, the Commission must draw up an inventory guide in close cooperation with the expert group referred to in Article 4.2 of Council Regulation (EC) No 1287/2003.
The Commission must verify the sources, their uses and the methods in the inventory by carrying out verification visits to the national authorities responsible for the reporting of national accounts, as well as to ensure that GNI data are comparable, reliable and exhaustive.
National authorities must send the Commission a report on the quality of the data they transmit to Eurostat every 2 years. The data for previous years must be communicated at the same time.
Eurostat may request the assistance of the national accounts experts representing national statistical authorities of other EU countries.
An expert group is set up by the Commission to assist the Commission in the exercise of its implementing powers.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLANATION APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 April 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Statistics code of practice, as reviewed and updated by the European statistical system committee on 16 November 2017, and adopted by the ESSC, is based on the principle of statistical integrity.
For more information, see:
‘The European Statistical System’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Gross national income: the total primary income receivable by resident institutional units: compensation of employees, taxes on production and imports less subsidies, property income (receivable less payable), gross operating surplus and gross mixed income.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of new vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the EU (Regulation (EU) 2016/1628)
EU type–approval requirements after the withdrawal from the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/1622 — type–applicability of new vehicle and system approvals following the EU’s decision to withdraw from the UK
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the granting of EU type‑approval to new vehicles and systems, which are intended to be placed on the market in the EU after the UK withdraws from the Union.
It aims to ensure equal treatment of manufacturers affected by the withdrawal.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
new vehicles* and systems*, systems*, components*, separate technical unit* of the same type* that have been approved in the UK;
vehicles and systems* already on the EU market and already placed on other EU countries’ markets;
different types of vehicles that have already been approved by EU countries other than the UK.
This regulation applies from 30 March 2019, unless the European Council, in agreement with the UK, unanimously decides to extend the period of application of the regulation until 30 March 2023.
Type-approvals are granted on the basis of test reports already presented for the purposes of obtaining the EU type‐approval for the same vehicle.
Manufacturers must notify the competent EU authority that is to approve the new vehicle or system before the date on which the regulation becomes applicable.
The competent EU authorities must take over the responsibilities of the UK authority for the following:
recalls, repair and maintenance information and in-service conformity checks;
instructions for the safety of the vehicle and its systems;
the recall of parts and spare parts;
recall and maintenance requirements for the vehicle, system, components, separate technicalunit and separate unit;
requests for the conformity assessment of the vehicles and components.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers continue to have the greatest possible freedom to choose the competent authority.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
From 30 March 2017.
BACKGROUND
The withdrawal agreement between the EU and the UK contains arrangements allowing for the application of certain provisions of EU law to and in the country beyond the date that the Treaties cease to apply to and to the EU.
For more information, see:
The United Kingdom and the EU — withdrawal agreement (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle of a type intended for sale, registration, sale or entry into service in the Member States.
* System, component or separate unit: any part of a vehicle intended for use in a vehicle other than its engine.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on the transfer of personal data to other EU bodies, authorities of third countries and international organisations (Regulation (EC) No 552/2014)
European Police Organisation (EuroPOL) — Rules on the Transfer of Personal Data to other European Union bodies, third countries, authorities and organisations
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/371/JHA establishing the European Police Office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision sets out the rules governing the Europol’s accession to the European Union (EU) and the management board of Europol.
The regulation lays down the rules for Europol to be governed by a regulation to be adopted in accordance with the ordinary legislative procedure.
KEY POINTS
Europol is the European police office responsible for preventing and combating serious crime in the EU. It is responsible for the protection of the personal data of EU citizens.
To achieve its mission, Europol:
supports and strengthens EU countries’ actions and their cooperation to prevent and combat serious crime affecting two or more EU countries;
exchanges all relevant information, with the exception personal data, with other EU and non-EU bodies and authorities;
cooperates closely with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and other international organisations;
is able to request EU countries to initiate, conduct or coordinate criminal investigations in specific cases where cross-border cooperation would add value.
EU countries must provide Europol with a copy of bilateral and multilateral exchanges of information on crime falling within its mandate, as well as part of its work.
Exchanges of information
The management board and the European Commission are responsible for supervising the management of the organisation. The management board is chaired by the Commission and the Commission is represented on the Management Board by the European Directorate-General for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (EDPS).
The Management Board:
is composed of representatives of the Commission, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Council of Europe;
has the power to authorise, under certain conditions, the transfers of data;
may decide to suspend or restrict access to personal data for specific reasons;
ensures that personal data are processed only for the purpose for which they were originally transferred;
establishes a system of access rights for individuals who have the right to have their personal data processed;
sets out the rights of individuals who are subject to access to the data it processes.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Council Decision 2009/391JHA has applied since 1 July 2009.
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom and Euratom Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU have applied since 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The Europol is a European police organisation, established under Article 88 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).
For more information, see:
About Europol (European External Action Service).
KEY TERMS
* Personal data: the data that Europol receives from EU countries.
* EU countries: the information they provide to Europol that they have provided to it.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
  Еъларсканна нарка гарода баргаланскограка   глакова  Пандаранка, г.

Summary 36:
Financial assistance for the COVID-19 outbreak
Financial assistance to the EU countries affected by the outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 — financial assistance to Member States in difficulties or seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond their control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a mechanism for the European Union (EU) to provide financial assistance for Member States experiencing severe economic disturbances caused by an exceptional occurrence.
It aims to provide the affected Member States with sufficient financial means under favourable terms to enable them to deal with the impact of the outbreak on their labour market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following Member States:
France, Germany, Italy, Spain and the United Kingdom (1).
It does not apply to the Member States of the European Economic Area (EEA).
EU financial assistance
Member States may request EU financial assistance under the Instrument (‘financial assistance’) where:
their actual and possibly also planned public expenditure has suddenly and severely increased as of 1 February 2020 due to national measures directly related to short-time work schemes and similar measures to address the socio-economic effects of the exceptional occurrence caused by the crisis;
their expenditure has been significantly increased in support of their national loans or similar measures, and, where applicable, in support for relevant health-related measures.
The European Commission must assess every 6 months whether the exceptional circumstances causing the severe economic disturbance in Member States still exist and report to the Council. The Council may grant financial assistance if the Council decides to do so.
If the following:
the Member State must provide the European Central Bank of the EU (ECBanca) with a guarantee of up to €1.5 billion;
the European Commission is empowered to borrow on the capital markets or with financial institutions on behalf of the Union at the most appropriate time so as to optimise the cost of funding and preserve its reputation as the EU’s issuer in the markets.
In addition to public health impacts with substantial fatal outcomes, COVID‐19 has had a massive and disruptive impact on the economic systems of EU countries, caused societal disruptions and increased public expenditure in a growing number of Member States.
Administration of the loans
The Commission must establish the arrangements for the administration of these loans with the ECB. The ECB is responsible for administering the loans. The loans are guaranteed by Member States and are irrevocable, unconditional and on demand, while additional safeguards are in place to enhance the robustness of the system.
Loan amount
The total amount of loans granted to the three Member States representing the largest share of the loan granted shall not exceed 60 per cent of the maximum amount referred to in Article 5.5 of the regulation. The maximum amount for each Member State may not exceed €1 billion. The total amount for the 3 Member States is not less than €2 billion.
Borrowing and lending operations under the regulation
The Regulation sets out prudential rules, including on the possibility of rolling over the borrowings contracted by the EU.
EU countries may not use the loans to finance their national short-term work schemes, as well as, where appropriate, of the relevant measures, that may be financed by the European Health Insurance Fund (EHIF).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 February 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID–19 outbreak was the first outbreak of its kind in the EU since the outbreak of the Ebola virus in 2003.
For more information, see:
Short-term working schemes (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Short-time working scheme: a scheme that allows workers to work part-time in order to supplement their income. Similar schemes exist for income replacement for the self-employed.
* Health-related measure: measures to prevent, control or reduce the incidence of communicable diseases.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1046/2018/EU of 21 December 2018 establishing a framework for the granting of financial assistance pursuant to Article 220(5) of Regulation (EU), Euratom (OJ L 304, 28.12.2018, pp. 1-10)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No1046/2010 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of central counterparties (CCPs) (Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 on the recovery and resolution framework for the resolution of Central Counterparties (CPSs)
Recovery & resolution of CCPs (Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 552/2012 and (EU), No 553/2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 649/2012 — the recovery & resolution framework of Central counterparties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
The regulation establishes a framework to ensure that central counterparty (CCP)* (i.e. the financial institution that acts as the buyer to every seller and the seller to every buyer) is able to recover from financial distress and to maintain the critical functions of a CCP which is failing or likely to fail while winding up the remaining activities through normal insolvency proceedings.
KEY POINTS
The aim of the regulation is to:
ensure that:
CCPs set out measures to recover in the event of financial distress;
the resolution of the CCP’s failure is carried out in accordance with the law of the country in which the CCP is established;
there is a clear separation of responsibilities between the competent authorities and resolution authorities.
EU countries must ensure that competent authorities have adequate tools at their disposal to handle situations involving CCP failures.
The resolution of a failing CCP should be carried out by competent authorities. The resolution authority should be designated as the resolution authority. The authorities should provide appropriate justification for their participation, based on their analysis of the negative impact that the resolution could have on their EU countries, and the resolution authorities’ ability to minimise the cost to taxpayers of a failed CCP.
In EU countries where a CCP is set up, adequate structural arrangements should be put in place to separate the resolution functions from other functions, in particular where the authority responsible for the prudential supervision of credit institutions or investment firms that are CCPs’ members, is designated as a resolution authority, to avoid any conflicts of interest and risk of regulatory forbearance.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMESSAGE APPLYORK ENTER INTO FORCELEBRATION
The Regulation entered into force on 1 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
  KEY TERMS
* Central counterpartie: a central counterpartie is a financial institution which acts as a buyer or seller between two or more counterparties.
* Clearing: the process whereby financial products and services are exchanged between counterparties in order to facilitate the flow of financial products, services and money between financial institutions and their clients.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 608/2012 of 23 June 2012 establishing a framework for recovery and resolving the failure of Central Clearing Partnerships (CCPSs).
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 38:
European Year for Railways 2021 - encouraging and supporting the growth of passengers and freight moving by rail (European Green Deal Decision) (European Commission)
European Year FORREWORK
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/2021 establishing a European Year for rail transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It aims to encourage and support the efforts of the Union, Member States, regional and local authorities, and other organisations to increase the share of passengers* and freight* moving by railway in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the objectives of the European Year 2021.
KEY POINTS
The year 2021 will be important for the EU rail policy, since it will represent the first full year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the EU, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the Union and on providing the European European Union Agency for Railway Safety (ERA) with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
The European Year will be coordinated by the European Commission and the European Network of Railways (ENR).
The Commission will regularly convene meetings of the national contact persons in order to coordinate the running of the EU year.
In addition, the Commission will consider initiating studies on the feasibility of creating a European label to promote goods and products transported by rail so as to encourage businesses to switch their transport to rail; anda feasibility study with a view to introducing a rail connectivity index, with the aim of categorising the level of integration achieved through the use of services on the rail network and showing the potential of rail to compete with other modes of transport.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision applies from 1 January 2021. It applies to the period 2021-2027.
BACKGROUND
The EU rail strategy is a new growth strategy that aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use. To achieve climate neutrality, a 90 % reduction in transport emissions is needed by 2050.

Summary 40:
Urban waste water for agricultural irrigation: rules for water reuse
Urban wastewater treatment plants
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the reuse of treated urban waste water
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes minimum requirements for the use of urban wastewater for agricultural purposes.
It sets out the conditions for the granting of a permit to the EU Member State of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to treated urban wastewater from urban waste-water treatment plants that is reused, in accordance with Directive 91/271/EEC (see summary).
The purpose of the regulation is to:
ensure that water is reused whenever it enters a collecting system and is subject to treatment in an urban wastewater treatment plant;
establish rules for the water reuse risk management plan;
set out the criteria for the approval of the permit, including the conditions and measures to be taken by the Member State to ensure that the water is not used for the abstraction of water intended for human consumption and relevant safeguard zones.
Permits
The Member State must issue a permit containing all the necessary conditions, measures and measures for the reuse, as well as the risk management plans, to the European Commission and the Member States. If all the conditions are met, the competent authority in the EU country must grant the permit.
The permit must contain the following information:
the water treatment process;
the conditions for reuse;
a description of the risks to the reuse poses to human health and the environment;
measures to protect the water bodies used for human and animal consumption and safeguard zones;
details of the measures to prevent the free movement of such products which have been irrigated with reclaimed water.
In addition, the permit must also contain information on the water treatment plant and the water management plan.
Risk management plan
The risk management must include the identification and management of risks in a proactive way, and should incorporate the concept of producing reclaimed water of a specific quality required for particular uses. The plan must also include:
a risk assessment of the potential health and environmental risks and potential obstacles to the movement of products which are irrigated using reclaimed water;
information on the risks and measures taken to avoid them;
an assessment of risks to human and environmental health and safety;
adequate monitoring of the water quality of the reclaimed water before it is discharged into fresh water or estuaries.
If the permit is not granted, the authority in question must inform the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council, and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) of the reasons for the decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
Water reuse is a market-driven action, based on the demands and needs of the agricultural sector, in particular in certain Member States that face water resource shortages.
For more information, see:
Urban water reuse (European Commission).

Summary 41:
EU blue card for highly qualified non-EU nationals who wish to move to another EU Member State
European blue card
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/801 on the European Union’s highly qualified employment visa
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the mobility of highly qualified third-country nationals wishing to work in the EU.
It sets out the conditions for the granting of an EU blue card to these individuals.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to EU citizens who have been granted international protection (known as the ‘Blue Card’) and who are highly qualified.
EU countries must ensure a level playing field between EU Blue Cards and national residence permits for the purpose of highly skilled employment, in terms of procedural and equal treatment rights, procedures and access to information.
To obtain an EU Blue Card, applicants must demonstrate that they have higher professional skills, attested by at least 5 years of professional experience at a level comparable to higher education qualifications and which is relevant to the profession or sector specified in the work contract or binding job offer.
The salary paid to the applicant must not be lower than the applicable salary threshold but may be higher, in line with market conditions, labour law, collective agreements and practices in the Member State concerned. However, the employer may decide to provide for a lower salary threshold to benefit third country nationals during a certain period following their graduation.
Applicants must be able to present a valid work contract and a valid for at least 3 years of experience in the same profession.
If the employer does not yet know his or her future address, EU countries should accept a temporary address, which could be the address of the employer.
Employers should not withdraw or refuse to renew an application if the salary threshold is temporarily not met due to illness, disability or parental leave.
A decision rejecting an application for an EU card does not affect the right of the third country national concerned to submit another application.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2016. EU Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The blue card is one of a number of legal instruments designed to facilitate the movement of skilled workers between the EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union blue card’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Highly qualified: a person with a high level of professional competence in the field of work to be carried out, which is inherently regarded as demanding such competence.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom) 2017/801 of 23 January 2016 on the EU‘s high-skilled employment visa’.

Summary 42:
Funding of the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund for the period 2014-2020 (Regulation (EU) No 516/2014) (see summary)
Funding for the 2014-2030 period (Council Decision 2014/516/EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 establishing the European Fund for asylum, migration and integration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes the EU’s main funding instrument for asylum and migration at EU level.
The regulation sets out the objectives of the Fund, which is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
KEY POINTS
Asylum and migration policy
The Fund supports the efforts of the EU and its Member States to enhance the Member States’ capacity to develop, monitor and evaluate their asylum policies in the light of their obligations under EU law. It also supports measures tailored to the needs of third-country nationals that are generally implemented in the early stages of integration, as well as horizontal measures supporting EU countries’ capacities in the field of integration.
In addition, the Fund supports measures for sustainable return policies, such as measures to facilitate and guarantee safe and dignified return and readmission and sustainable reintegration of returnees, including through the provision of cash or in-kind support.
It also contributes to global resettlement initiatives with a view to help countries in regions to which a large number of persons in need of international protection have been displaced by helping to alleviate the pressure on those countries, and to effectively contribute to global migration initiatives.
Scope
The fund is divided into 3 categories:
the European Asylum and Migration Fund (the ‘Fund’), which is financed by the ESF, the European Return Fund (Fund for the Integration of Third-Country Nationals) and other EU programmes, including those under shared EU programmes such as the Common European Asylum System (CEAS).
The European Integration Fund (EIF) is financed through the European External Action Service (EEAS) and is co-financed by the EU budget.
Strategy
The EIF is the main instrument for the implementation of EU migration policy. It aims to:
improve the capacity of Member States in the area of integration;
ensure compliance with legal migration rules;
facilitate the swift return of irregular migrants;
promote the integration of non-EU nationals in the EU;
contribute to the resettlement and relocation of applicants for international protection and beneficiaries who are granted international protection under the legal framework of the common European asylum system.
Implementation of the EIF
In order to optimise the added value from investments funded wholly or in part through the Union budget, synergies should be sought, in particular, between the Fund and other European Union programmes, notably the European Stability Mechanism (ESM), the Common Foreign and Security Policy (CFSP), the Erasmus+ and the Common Security and Defence Fund (CSDP).
Strategic objectives
The aim of the fund is to support actions serving internal EU policy on asylum and immigration in line with the objectives set out in the regulation. These include:
improving the capacities of EU countries to develop and monitor their asylum systems;
encouraging cooperation between Member States on migration and border management;
supporting efforts to ensure a comprehensive approach to the management of migration that is grounded on mutual trust, solidarity and fair sharing of responsibility among Member States and EU institutions;
helping to reduce the burden of migration on Member States;
providing support to countries in the region to which large numbers of persons who are displaced by the influx of asylum seekers.
Sustainable return policies
To achieve its objectives, the fund supports measures in third countries to facilitate the return of illegal migrants and to encourage compliance with migration rules, thereby safeguarding the integrity of the immigration systems. The Fund also supports:
effective readmission of illegally staying non-European Union nationals by third countries;
recipients of refugee status who have been returned to their country of origin;
reintegration and resettlement of those who have returned to the territory of a Member State;
the protection of stateless persons in the context of the Schengen area;
transitional measures for the protection of vulnerable persons who have left their country and who are in danger of being displaced.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive (EC) No 496/2014 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 27 September 2014 on the establishment of a European Asylum Fund and repealing Decision No 575/2007/EC (OJ L 127, 28.9.2014, pp. 1-8)
Regulation(EU) 2018-2022 of the same Parliament and Council of 16 May 2018 laying down the rules for the operation of



Summary 43:
Paternity and paternity leave: equal treatment for men and women
Paternity leave: equitable treatment for women and men
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/18/EU on equal treatment of fathers and mothers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the treatment of both men* and women when it comes to parental leave and leave for carers*.
It sets out the conditions for the granting of paternity and maternity leave.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all workers in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to employees in the private sector.
It does not affect the rights of workers who are entitled to maternity or paternity leave or to flexible working arrangements.
EU countries are free to decide whether to provide a payment or an allowance for paternity leave, in order to guarantee the effective take-up of the right by carers, in particular by men.
The period within which workers should be entitled to take parental leave should be linked to the age of the child. It should not be subject to a certain period of service.
Workers who exercise their right to take leave must be protected against discrimination or any less favourable treatment on that ground. This includes:
the right to return to the same or to an equivalent post after taking such leave and the right not to be subjected to any detriment in the terms and conditions of their contract of employment or employment relationship as a result of taking the leave;
being subject to any financial penalties for failing to take the leave, such as fines or the payment of compensation.
being able to limit the duration of the leave and any reduction in working hours or any remote working arrangements;
not being subject to undue burden on the employer.
In addition, EU countries are encouraged to ensure that paternity leave is granted irrespective of marital or family status, as defined by national law.
National authorities are also encouraged to take account of the needs of the person in need of care or support.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has consulted management and labour in a two-stage process with regard to challenges related to work-life balance, in accordance with Article 154 TFEU (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘Working life balance’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 44:
Protecting the EU’s financial interests: fraud against the common budget
Protection of the EU's financial interests
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 98/8/EC on the protection of the European Communities' financial interests (Financial Instruments Directive)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect the European Union (EU) financial interests against fraud.
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU financial interests.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
fraud affecting EU revenue, expenditure and assets at the expense of the general budget (the ‘EU budget’), including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
The definition of fraud falls within the scope of this directive, which should cover fraudulent conduct with respect to revenues, expenditure or assets at:
the general budget;
the EU budget of the Member States;
financial operations that are financed by the EU budget.
Penalties
EU countries must ensure that the imposition of criminal sanctions for criminal offences in accordance with this directive and of administrative measures and penalties does not lead to a breach of the Charter of Fundamental Rights and the principles of justice.
Sanctions
The Directive does not oblige Member States to provide for sanctions of imprisonment for the commission of offences that are not of a serious nature, in cases where intent is presumed under national law. However, Member States must provide for certain types and levels of sanctions when the criminal offences are committed. The maximum sentences should be at least 5 years from the time when the offence was committed.
Limitation periods
Member States should lay down rules concerning limitation periods necessary in order to enable them to counter illegal activities at the cost of the Union's financial interest.
Proper implementation of the Directive by Member States includes the processing of personal data by the competent national authorities, and the exchange of such data between Member States on the one hand, and between competent EU bodies on the other.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 26 July 1998 and had to become law in Member States by 26 July 1999.
BACKGROUND
  
The Convention drawn up on the basis of Article K.3.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on the Protection of European Communities’ financial interests of 26 July 1995 (3), including the Protocols thereto of 27 September 1996 (4), of 29 November 1996 and of 19 June 1997 (6).
For more information, see:
‘Fraud’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fraud: any act or omission which, contrary to the intended purpose, causes the loss of funds or assets to the EU or to the Member State concerned.
ACT
Council Directive 98/9/EC of 27 July 1998 on the prevention and combatting of fraud in the field of financial Instruments Directive

Summary 45:
Delegated Regulation (EU) No 260/2012 on the harmonisation of interchange fees for card-based payments (Delegating Regulation (EC) No 552/2012) (OJ L 347, 29.10.2012, pp. 1-8)
Delegation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2012 laying down the rules for the implementation of Delegated Regulations (EU and Euratom).
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
   
 
The regulation aims to harmonise the interchange fees* charged by payment card issuers and payment service providers for the use of debit cards.
The aim is to ensure that the fees for debit card transactions are set at an economically efficient level, whilst contributing to fair competition, innovation and market entry of new operators.
KEY POINTS
The interchange fees are charged by the payment card issuer (i.e. the bank issuing the card) and the payment service provider (the acquirer).
The issuer makes payment cards available to the payer, authorises transactions at terminals or their equivalent and may guarantee payment to the acquirer for transactions that are in conformity with the rules of the relevant scheme.
Payment service providers are the banks’ authorised acquirers. The regulation defines the terms ‘acquirer’, ‘bank’ and ‘intermediaries’.
Intermediaries providing part of the acquiring services but without direct contractual relationship with payees should nevertheless be covered in the definition of acquirer under this regulation.
In order to define the relevant interchange fee caps for domestic and cross-border transactions, it is appropriate to allow national competent authorities to collect information regarding the volume and value of all transactions within a payment card scheme or of the transactions pertaining to one or more payment card service providers. The authorities may require that the information sent by these entities is certified by an independent auditor.
It is also necessary to define a maximum share of domestic ‘universal cards’ payment transactions which are considered as being equivalent to credit card transactions. This is based on the number of merchants in each EU country.
FROM WHEN DO THE LEGISLATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2012/260/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2012 laying down rules on interchange fees and on the prohibition of territorial licensing for payment card schemes.
Directives 2012/280/EU and 2012/290/EU have applied since 1 January 2013.
Regulation 2013/30/EU has applied since 31 December 2014.
For more information, see:
‘Interchange fees’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 46:
European framework for the documentation of skills and qualifications acquired in formal, non-formal and informal settings, including through practical experiences, mobility and volunteering (‘Europass’)
European framework to support the transparency and understanding of skills (Europass)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 establishing a European framework to provide information on the documentation and assessment of skills acquired through working and learning experiences
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a framework to help individuals, employers, education and training providers and social partners to better communicate and present their knowledge and skills and to compare qualifications.
It aims to improve the visibility and comparability of the skills acquired in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the Europass framework, which aims to:
provide information on learning opportunities and qualifications offered in different education systems in Europe and on the comparison of national qualifications frameworks using the European Qualifications Framework (EQF);
improve the visibility of skills in the labour market;
improve access to information on employment and learning opportunities.
The framework is based on the European dimension of guidance provided through an online platform and supported by national services intended to help users to communicate, present and compare their skills.
Europass is designed to be a tool for:
documenting and describing personal information in a variety of formats, including curriculum vitae (CV) templates;
visits and working experiences;
assessment of skills;
the assessment and self-assessment;
validation opportunities and guidance;
information on validation opportunities;
exchange of information on qualifications and guidance.
In addition, the decision sets up a Europass portal to provide users with access to relevant information, such as:
the EQF;
competences and qualifications;
qualifications;
education and training outcomes of qualifications; and
the learning outcomes of education.
Context
The New Skills Agenda for Europe, adopted by the European Commission on 10 June 2016, invites Member States, social partners, industry and other stakeholders to work together on ten actions to improve quality and relevance of skills formation, to make skills more visible and comparable and to improve skills intelligence and especially information for better career choices. This decision establishes a European platform to support this objective.
For more information, see:
The Europass website.

Summary 47:
Statistics on the transport of goods by inland waterways (Codified Regulation)
Statistics on:
the transport of good by inland waters
codified Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 on the transmission of data subject to statistical confidentiality to the European Statistical Office (ESO).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission with:
data on the total volume of goods transported annually by inland waterways as national transport exceeding 1.000 000 tonnes;
data relating to passenger transport by inland inland waterways, including by cross-border transport services.
The Commission is responsible for:
monitoring and developing the common transport policy, as well as the transport elements of policies on the regions and on trans-European networks.
It is assisted by the European statistical system committee established by Regulation (Euratom).
The European Statistical System Committee (ESSC) is composed of representatives of EU countries’ national statistical institutes and other national authorities. The ESSC’s tasks include:
preparing and implementing implementing acts;
ensuring the quality of the data transmitted and the data collection methods used;
providing the Commission with statistical actions for which those delegated acts provide, using, where appropriate, a cost-effectiveness analysis, including an assessment of the burden on respondents and of the production costs as referred to in Article 14(3)(c) of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 8 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
Inland waterways transport is a key element of the EU transport policy and a key driver of economic and technical development.
For more information, see:
‘Inland waterway transport’ on the Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Inland waterways: vessels used solely for non-commercial purposes by port administrations and public authorities.
* Passenger transport: vessels carrying goods from one place to another.

Summary 48:
Statistics on rail transport
Statistics on railway transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — common rules for the production of rail transport statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish common rules on the production and transmission of railway transport statistics at EU level.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 91/2003.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
railway undertakings which operate entirely or mainly within industrial and similar installations, including harbours;
railways which mainly provide local tourist services, such as preserved historical steam railways.
EU countries may exclude from the scope of the regulation undertakings that operate solely or mainly in industrial or similar installations: railways which provide local passenger services, railways which operate exclusively in industrial installations, railways that provide local tourism services, railways which operate only in urban areas, and railway undertakings operating only in rural areas.
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the arrangements for the transmission of the statistics referred to in Article 4. Those implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure laid down in Article 11(2) of the implementing act. The Commission must consult the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC) before adopting implementing acts. The ESC is assisted by the European statistical system committee established by Regulation (ESS) No 223/2009.
Transmission of statistics
The Commission must transmit the statistics to Eurostat, the European Union’s statistical office, which is responsible for collecting and processing the statistics.
Eurostat, on request by the Commission, must supply information on the methods used in producing the statistics, including:
the methods used for calculating the statistics;
the number of railway vehicles used in the transport of passengers by rail; and
the types of passenger-kilometres carried out by rail vehicles.
Data transmission period
The data transmission period for annual data on rail passengers is from 13 December 2016 to 13 December 2023. The data transmitted are based on the number of rail passengers transported by rail in the course of combined transport operations.
Railway transport statistics are also used to prepare and monitor EU action in the field of transport safety.
