Summary 49:
Harmonisation of the visa requirements and the exemption from the visa requirement for non-EU nationals
Harmonomisation of:
visa requirements
exemption from visa requirements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 on the common policy on visas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the conditions for entry and transit of non-European Union (EU) nationals by EU countries’ external borders.
It does so by establishing a list of the countries whose nationals are subject to a requirement to be in possession of a visa for the crossing of EU countries' external borders (also referred to herein as ‘the visa requirement’) and those who are exempt from that requirement.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the other EU countries of the measures they take to ensure that the visa regime is administered openly and that the persons concerned are informed.
Nationals of the EU countries listed in Annex II of the regulation must be exempt from the requirement set out in Article 3(1) for stays of no more than 90 days in any 180-day period.
Non-EU countries are excluded from the list if:
they are not a candidate for membership of the Schengen area;
they do not hold the nationality of any EU country; or
they have not signed an agreement with an EU country on the free movement of persons.
The regulation does not apply to:
stateless persons and other persons who do not have nationality;
non-EU citizens who are not citizens of an EU Member State.
Exemptions from visa requirement
A Member State may exempt a number of categories of persons from a visa requirement, depending on the specific circumstances notified by one or several Member States or reported by the Commission, in accordance with public international law or custom. It may also suspend the exemption if there is a decrease in cooperation on readmission as well as a substantial increase in risks to the public policy or internal security of Member States. The suspension mechanism can be triggered by short reference periods and deadlines, allowing for a fast procedure, and the possible grounds of suspension include:
a deterioration in the security situation of the Member State concerned;
an increase in the risk of terrorism;
a decrease in the level of trust between the Member States concerned.
A temporary suspension of the exemption may also be triggered in the event of a deterioration of the security of the State concerned. The implementing act must determine the categories of nationals to which the suspension should apply, taking into account:
the number of nationals covered by the regulation;
the nature and duration of the temporary suspension; and
the reasons for suspending the exemption.
In addition, the Commission may decide to suspend the visa exemption for a limited period of time, in particular where a readmission agreement has been concluded between the third country concerned and the EU.
Member States must communicate to the Commission and to the other Member States the measures which they take pursuant to this regulation. They must also inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of all the measures that they have taken to implement the regulation.
If a Member State considers that a third country has not complied with its obligations under this regulation, it must inform the Commission of the reasons for its decision. The Commission may also decide to invoke the suspension mechanism in the case of a failure to cooperate with readmission.
Visa requirements
The Member States must:
ensure that:
all nationals of the country concerned are free of any visa requirement during their stay;
visas are issued in a language easily understood by the general public;
travel documents are issued for the same duration as those of the non-member country concerned, except for travel documents issued by the State of origin of the person concerned. In addition, Member States may issue travel documents for the purpose of verifying the validity of the travel document issued for a period of 90 days.
Visas must be issued in the form of a travel document and must be accompanied by a visa. The Member State issuing the visa must also provide the Commission with all the necessary information, such as the date and time of entry and exit, for the persons subject to the visa.
Authorisation of travel documents
The implementing act sets out the number of visas to be issued for each Member State during the period of stay. The number of valid visas must not exceed 5,000.
Each Member State must ensure that all the nationals of its territory are entitled to a visa under the same conditions as nationals of any other Member State, and that all visas are issued within the same period of validity.
Annex II to the regulation provides for the implementation of the reciprocal visa system between Member States and non-Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Visas



Summary 50:
Eurojust — rules of procedure and operational data protection — Eurojust
Eurojust – rules and procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 — rules and procedures of the European External Action Service (Eurojust)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out Eurojust’s legal framework.
It aims to ensure that Eurojust complies with the principles applicable to EU agencies and fully respects fundamental rights and freedoms, while maintaining its independence in the exercise of its operational functions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all cases falling under the competence of Eurojust that directly involve at least three EU countries and for which requests or decisions on judicial cooperation have been transmitted to at least two EU countries. It does not apply to investigations involving only one EU country but which have repercussions at EU level.
EU countries must inform Eurojust without undue delay of cases falling within its competence.
The European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and the European Police and Criminal Investigation Office (EPPO) are responsible for investigating and prosecuting frauds affecting the financial interests of the EU and Eurojust is responsible for assisting them in this area.
In addition, Eurojust may take a more proactive role in coordinating cases, such as by supporting the national authorities in their investigations and prosecutions. It may also assist with investigations involving EU countries that might not initially have been included in the case and discovering links between cases based on the information it receives from Europol, the European anti-fraud office (Europol), OLAF, the EPPOJHA and national authorities.
Cooperation between Eurojust and other EU agencies
It may also be requested by the European Commission or the European Union Agency for the prevention, investigation, detection or prosecution of criminal offences or the execution of criminal penalties, including the safeguarding against and combating terrorism.
Duties and powers of the Directorate-General for the Protection of the Internal Market (EDPS)
The EDPS has the power to order Eurojust to carry out rectification, restriction, restriction of processing or erasure of operational personal data which have been processed in breach of the data protection provisions contained in national case files. The EDPS must be granted powers allowing him or her to fulfil this duty effectively.
A President and two Vice-Presidents are elected by the College from among the national members for a term of office of four years. When a national member is elected President, the Member State concerned is able to second another suitably qualified person to the national desk and to apply for compensation from Eurojust's budget. The College is also responsible for providing strategic guidance to the Executive Board. The Commission is also its representative on the executive board, to ensure non-operational supervision of the body and to provide it with strategic guidance.
Rights and freedoms
The rules and obligations of the EDPS are set out in the regulation. These include:
the protection of personal data;
the right to access and to have access to operational data for archiving purposes in the public interest or statistical purposes;
protection of the confidentiality of operational data in the event of breaches of data protection rules;
data protection rules on admissibility as evidence in criminal pre-trial and court proceedings;
rules on contractual and non-contractual liability applicable to European institutions, bodies, offices and agencies.
Transfers of operational operational data to third countries and international organisations
Personal data can be transferred by Eurojust only if necessary for preventing and combating crime that falls within its tasks. It must be transferred to a third country or to an international organisation only if:
it falls within the scope of its tasks;
it is necessary to prevent and combat crime in a way that is proportionate to the nature of the crime;
there is no need for the transfer to be carried out under the same conditions as that of a national police or judicial authority.
Data protection rules are laid down in the annex to the regulation, including:
data security;
privacy of data; and
the rights of the subjects of data transfers.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  Еганналарскаблака барка  гародна года
For more information, see:
‘Rules of procedure’ on the Eurojust website.
* KEY TERMS
* Operational personal data: operational data: data that is processed for the purpose of preventing, investigating, detecting or prosecuting a criminal offence.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2010/852/JHA of 20 December 2010 on the rules and procedural provisions of the Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on the European external action service (Euro-5) (O



Summary 51:
Money laundering: criminal law
money laundering: financial crime
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/1371 — criminal law against money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering by means of criminal law, enabling more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between EU countries’ authorities.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all criminal activities which constitute predicate offences for money laundering. It does not apply to money laundering as regards property derived from criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests, which is subject to specific rules laid down in Directive (EU’1371.
The definition of criminal activities is based on the Financial Action Task Force (FATF) Recommendations and instruments of other international organisations and bodies active in combating money laundering and terrorist financing.
EU countries must ensure that certain types of money laundering activities are also punishable when committed by the perpetrator of the criminal activity that generated the property (‘self-laundering’).
Prosecutions are not impeded by the fact that a criminal activity was committed in another EU country or in a non-EU country, subject to the conditions set out in this directive.
To ensure the successful investigation and prosecution of these offences, those responsible for investigating or prosecuting such offences should have the possibility to make use of effective investigative tools such as those which are used in combating organised crime or other serious crimes.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure the confiscation of property deriving from activities of a criminal nature, including in the absence of a conviction of a specific person or persons for those activities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   х ъ  
It applies from 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a signatory to the Council of Europe Convention on Laundering, Search, Seizure and Confiscation of the Proceeds from Crime and on the Financing of Terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Money laundering: the laundering of funds, instrumentalities and proceeds of crime to facilitate the transfer of funds between criminal organisations or individuals.
Penalty: a maximum term of imprisonment of at least 5 years for a first-time offender. The maximum term may be increased to 10 years for second-time offenders.

Summary 52:
Recovery plan for bluefin tuna
Resolution of the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas
RESOLUTION OF THE INTERNATIONAL CONVENTION FOR THE RECOMMENDATION OF THE DECISION AND OF THE REGULATION
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the implementation of the multiannual recovery plan for the sustainable management of bluefin tunas in the eastern Atlantic and Mediterranean ending in 2022.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE RESOLUTION AND REGULATIONS?
The regulation sets out the rules for the implementation by EU countries of the EU’s recovery plan (‘the recovery plan’).
The aim of the recovery plan is to achieve a biomass of bluefish equivalent to the maximum sustainable yield by 2022 with at least a 60 % probability of achieving that objective.
The Regulation (EU), which repeals Regulation (EC) No 404/2011, aims to achieve the same objective by 2023.
KEY POINTS
The recovery plan:
aims to:
reduce the impact of fishing on the marine environment;
ensure a sustainable yield;
increase the number of fish caught;
improve the quality of fishing gear and techniques used in traditional and artisanal fisheries, thereby contributing to a fair standard of living for local economies.
EU countries must submit an annual fishing plan to the European Commission specifying the quotas for each gear group and each year.
Each EU country must submit a fishing plan identifying the quotas allocated to each fishing vessel and gear group, including information on:
the number of traps registered in an EU country and of fishing vessels flying its flag that may fish for, retain on board, tranship, transport, or land bluefin fish;
the total number of vessels authorised to fish for and tranship the fish on board a fishing vessel to another fishing vessel.
Every year, EU countries submit their annual fishing plans to the Commission for the purpose of calculating the quotas. The plan must be submitted no later than 30 days before the start of the fishing season applicable to each such vessel. The Commission must transmit the plan to each EU country within 30 days of the end of its fishing season.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGISIONS APPLY?
   
Regulations (EC, EC, EC and EU countries) have applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is party to the Convention on the International Conservation of Tropical Tunas (ICCAT).
For more information, see:
‘Recovery Plan for the Sustainable Management of Bluefin Tunas’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bluefin tuna: the largest species of tuna in the world.
* Tuna: a species of fish that is the subject of an international conservation plan, adopted in 2006.

Summary 53:
Shipbuilding injurious pricing: combating ship-purchase transactions (IPI Code)
Shipbuilding injurious pricing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/755 — combating shipbuilding injuriously priced vessel sales
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the EU’s shipbuilding industry against the sale of ships below their normal value.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 182/2011, which had previously codified the Shipbuilding Injurious Pricing Code.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to ships from non-market-economy countries and, in particular, those to which the European Parliament and of the Council have concluded a Shipbuilding Agreement with non-EU countries.
The normal value of a ship is calculated on the basis of the cost of production in the country of origin plus a reasonable amount for selling, general and administrative costs and for profits. It does not include actual costs which the shipbuilder demonstrates are due to force majeure and are significantly above the cost increase which the company could reasonably have anticipated and taken into account when the material terms of sales were fixed.
In addition, the normal value does not take into account:
the cost of the vessel itself;
the costs related to its construction, including taxes and import duties; and
the profit of the ship builder.
A vessel is to be considered as being ‘injurious priced’ if the export price of a vessel sold is less than a comparable price for a like vessel, in the ordinary course of trade, when sold to a buyer of the exporting country.
a person or company controls a buyer when the buyer is legally or operationally in a position to exercise restraint or direction over the buyer, which is presumed at a 25% interest.
To maintain the balance of rights and obligations which the agreement establishes, action should be taken by the EU against any shipbuilder whose sale at less than normal value causes injury to the EU industry.
Where appropriate, an investigation may also be initiated upon a written complaint by the authorities of a contracting party to the agreement, in accordance with this regulation and under the conditions of the agreement.
Complaints
The Commission must take all necessary steps to ascertain, in large conglomerates and holdings of third countries, the validity of accounting charges when the cost price structure needs to be estimated. It must also take all the necessary measures to ensure that such charges are paid within the applicable time limit.
If a shipbuilder does not pay the charge within the time limit, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for its decision. The Commission may take countermeasures if this does not happen.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts to combat shipbuilding piracy. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014 on the protection of shipowners and ship-owners against ship-building piracy (see summary);
Directive 2014/34/EU on the prevention, control and enforcement of piracy and on the enforcement of anti-dumping measures in the field of maritime transport.
Directives 2014/35/EU and 2014/36/EU have been amended several times, most recently by Directive 2014/33/EU.
For more information, see:
‘Injurious Pays’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 54:
Cableway subsystems and safety components — EU declaration of conformity
Cableshields and cableway safety components: EU declaration thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the declaration of CE conformity for subsystems of cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the functioning of the EU’s internal market for subsystem* and safety* components for cableways.
It sets out the essential requirements and conformity assessment procedures for the design, manufacture and placing on the market of subsystems* and components*.
It does not apply to:
cable-operated ferries;
lifts, including cable-operated lifts, permanently serving specific levels of buildings and constructions and not operating between cableway stations.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies in its entirety to new cableways, to modifications of existing installations requiring a new authorisation and to installations which are new to the EU market when they are placed on it; that is to say, they are either new subsystems or safety components made by a manufacturer established in the EU or subsystems, whether new or second-hand, imported from a non-EU country.
The scope of the regulation does not include:
construction and maintenance of cableways;
the inspection of installations prior to their entry into service;
monitoring of installations during operation.
Implementing Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 lays down the rules for the authorisation of planned cableways and for the monitoring of installations before they are put into service.
Authorisation procedures
EU countries have to specify adequate procedures for authorising the construction, maintenance and operation of new and existing cableways as well as for monitoring installations during their operation. These procedures must be consistent with those laid down in Directive 2000/9/EC on the harmonisation of technical harmonisation and standards (see summary).
EU notification of conformity assessment bodies
EU notified bodies must notify the European Commission of the results of the conformity assessment carried out by them. The notification must be sent to the Commission within 3 months of the notification of the competent national authority. The Commission must then inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of its decision to adopt the regulation.
EU declaration of safety components
EU declarations of conformity must be available in a single EU declaration. This declaration is the visible consequence of a whole process comprising conformity assessment in a broad sense.
Manufacturers, importers and distributors must ensure that their products are in conformity with the regulation and that they only make available those which are in compliance with this regulation. They must also ensure that:
all their products bear the CE marking and documentation drawn up by manufacturers are available for inspection by the competent authorities;
they keep the information required under this regulation for the identification of other economic operators who have either supplied them with a subsystem or safety component or to whom they have supplied a subsystem and a safety component.
In the interests of competitiveness, it is crucial that notified bodies do not create unnecessary burdens for economic operators.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities (Directive (EU) 2018/1972)
Assessment of accessibility requirements for electronic communications services and related products for persons who have long-term physical, mental, intellectual or sensory impairments (United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It aims to improve the accessibility of e-commerce services for people with disabilities.
It harmonises the requirements for the placing on the market of these services in order to ensure their free movement in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
electronic communications services;
mobile device-based services including mobile applications developed or made available by operators of passenger transport services within the scope of this directive or on their behalf;
online ticketing services, electronic tickets and interactive self-service terminals.
Accessibility requirements must be met by:
the websites and mobile applications of the products or services covered by the directive;
the accessibility of audiovisual content;
technologies that allow users with disabilities to use their assistive technologies.
This directive does not apply to the sale of a product or service covered in its own right under this directive.
Exemptions
Certain elements of the accessibility requirements, in particular in relation to the provision of information as set out in this directive, are already covered by existing EU law in the field of passenger transportation.
EU law on banking and financial services aims to protect and provide information to consumers of those services across the EU, but does not include accessibility requirements.
The European Commission has adopted a report on the implementation of the directive. It concludes that the directive has not yet achieved its full potential.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 July 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Convention against the Discrimination of Persons With Disabilities (UN CRPD) requires EU countries to adopt additional national provisions on accessibility of goods and services.
For more information, see:
European Commission’s website on accessibility (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Electronic communications services: a service provided at a distance, through websites or mobile devices, by electronic means and at the individual request of a consumer, with a view to concluding a consumer contract.
* Mobile ticketing: an electronic ticketing service that allows passengers to check their travel status on a mobile device.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Directive 2018/72/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 May 2018 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to accessibility of products, services, information and communication technologies and repealing Council Directives (EC) No 552/2004, (EC, EC, Euratom) No 652/2003, (Euratom) 2006 and (EC), and amending Council Directive (EURO) No 1082/2004.
Successive amendments and corrections to Directive 2018/62/EU have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Importers of minerals and metals from conflict-affected and high-risk areas must comply with the OECD due diligence guidance for responsible supply chains of minerals from conflict affected and high risk areas (OECD Due Diligence Guidance for Responsible Supply Chains of Minerals from Conflict-Affected and High-Risk Areas)

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 184/2011 — obligations of EU importers of minerals, metals and by-products of conflict areas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the obligations of the EU’s importers to ensure that their supply chains are responsible for the safe and undistorted supply of minerals* from conflict areas.
It aims to prevent the financing of armed groups and security forces in resource-rich areas, as well as the exploitation of these resources.
KEY POINTS

Summary 57:
EU customs code
EU Customs code
SUMMaintaining and modernising the EU Customs Code
The European Union Customs Code (EU Customs Code) aims to simplify customs legislation, to allow the use of modern tools and technology and to promote further the uniform application of customs legislation and modernised approaches to customs control, thus helping to ensure the basis for efficient and simple customs clearance procedures.
ACT
Commission Decision 2014/852/EU of 31 December 2014 on the implementation of the Union customs code (the Code) [See amending act(s)].
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
The decision establishes a legal framework for the application of certain provisions of the EU customs legislation to trade in EU goods between parts of the customs territory to which the provisions of Council Directive 2006/112/EC of 28 November 2006 on the common system of value added tax apply and parts of that territory where those provisions do not apply, or to trade between parts where they do not.
The aim of the decision is to modernise customs legislation in line with the Commission Communication of 24 July 2003 entitled ‘A simple and paperless environment for customs and trade’.
KEY POINTS
The Commission adopted the decision on the basis of Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 February 2011 laying down the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by Member States of the Commission’s exercise of implementing powers.
This decision establishes the legal framework within which the European Customs Code can be implemented. It covers:
the exchange of risk information and analysis, common risk criteria and standards, control measures and priority control areas;
the determination of the tariff classification of goods;
measures temporarily prohibiting the import and export of goods with a comprehensive guarantee;
implementation of customs debt on import;
customs information and communication systems for customs operations.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts cover:
measures to ensure uniform application and modernisation of customs controls, including:
common risk management framework and an electronic system for its implementation;
rules on the establishment of customs information systems for the exchange and communication of information on customs operations;
procedures for the release of a customs debt.
In addition, the Commission adopted implementing acts relating to:
implementing measures to ensure that:
all customs transactions are to be handled electronically;
information and communication system for customs operation are to offer, in each Member State, the same facilities to economic operators;
a customs representative who complies with the criteria for the granting of the status of authorised economic operator for customs simplifications should be entitled to provide his or her services in a Member State other than the Member State where he or she is established.
Delegated acts
In order to ensure effective administrative simplification, the views of economic operators should be taken into account when the customs legislation is further modernised. The Commission should ensure that Member States’ experts and the business community are consulted in a transparent manner, and well in advance.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The code entered into force on 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU Customs code is one of a number of measures designed to simplify and modernise the customs system in order to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the internal market. It is the first comprehensive customs code to be adopted in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Customs Code’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 58:
Enforcement of intellectual property rights: small consignments and the destruction of infringements of rights
En enforcement of Intellectual property rights : small consignment and the disposal of infringed goods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 laying down the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by Member States of the European Commission’s exercise of implementing powers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the enforcement of the rights of manufacturers, importers and traders.
It aims to prevent the diversion of counterfeit medicines into the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
goods that are manufactured by a person duly authorised by a right-holder to manufacture a certain quantity of goods, in excess of the quantities agreed between that person and the right-holders, and which are manufactured as genuine goods and are therefore not subject to customs controls;
manufactured goods that are placed on the market for the first time in the European Economic Area without his or her consent, and these are considered to be counterfeit goods.
Importers and importers are liable to customs supervision or customs control, and to carry out adequate controls on such goods with a view to preventing operations in breach of the intellectual property laws of the EU countries.
The rules apply to the following types of goods:
the manufacture of goods for sale in the EU without the authorisation of the right holder;
the sale of goods intended for use in a non-EU country.
Customs authorities must:
carry out adequate customs controls and take proportionate identification measures as provided for in Article 13(1) and Article 72 of Regulation (EEC) No 2913/92 in accordance with risk analysis criteria;
identify the persons or companies responsible for the manufacture of the goods;
take all necessary measures to ensure that the goods are destroyed.
In the case of counterfeiting, the holder of the decision must reimburse all the costs incurred by the customs authorities in taking action to enforce his intellectual property right.
Member States must, in cooperation with the Commission, provide appropriate training for customs officials, in order to ensure the correct implementation of this regulation.
A central electronic database is established for the exchange of information relating to decisions on applications and to customs actions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2011. It repeals Regulation (EC) 1383/2003.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Illegal trade in counterfeit medicines’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Importers: any person, organisation or group of persons acting on behalf of a legitimate business.

Summary 59:
Customs 2020 programme: financial support for customs cooperation (Regulation (EU) 2021/241)
Customs2020 programme: Financial support for Customs cooperation ( Regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom, EC) No 883/2013 establishing a new programme, the Customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a customs programme for the period 2021-2023.
It aims to support customs cooperation at EU level.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers:
the internal market, competitiveness of enterprises, including small and medium-sized enterprises, the area of plants, animals, food and feed, and European statistics (Single Market Programme);
the Recovery and Resilience Facility established by the European Parliament and of the Council;
the Technical Support Instrument established by Regulation (Euratom).
Funding
The financial envelope of the programme covers necessary and duly justified expenses for managing the programme and evaluating its performance, provided that those activities are related to the general and specific objectives of the Programme. The programme is financed through grants, procurement, prizes, indirect implementation, and checks on the responsibility of financial actors.
Implementation
The Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by the Commission and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF), which has the power to carry out administrative investigations, including on-the-spot checks and inspections, with a view to establishing whether there has been fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the EU.
EU countries may participate in the programme if certain conditions are fulfilled.
In order to support the process of accession and association by third countries, the programme is open to the participation of acceding countries, candidate countries, potential candidates and countries covered by the EU’s European Neighbourhood policy.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The customs union is a significant example of successful EU integration, and is essential for the proper functioning of the internal market for the benefit of both businesses and citizens. However, it is a dynamic policy area facing new challenges, such as globalisation, new patterns in fraud and smuggling, and digitalisation. These challenges increase the demand for support to customs authorities and call for innovative solutions.
For more information, see:
‘Customs programme’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 60:
The European Social Fund (ESF+)
The European social fund (EaSI)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/1060 establishing the European Social Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE ESF?
The decision establishes the European social funds (ESFs).
The ESF+ is the European Union’s main instrument for tackling poverty and social exclusion. It aims to contribute to the eradication of poverty by supporting national schemes aiming to alleviate food and material deprivation and promote social integration of people at risk of poverty or social exclusion and the most deprived persons.
It also aims to improve the functioning of labour markets by supporting the modernisation of labour market institutions such as the public employment services, in order to improve their capacity to provide intensified targeted counselling and guidance during the job search and the transition to employment and to enhance the mobility of workers.
KEY POINTS
The EaSI strand is based on three pillars:
equal opportunities and access to the labour market;
fair working conditions;
social protection and inclusion.
The 20 key principles of the Pillar are structured in three categories: equal opportunities, access to labour market, fair working conditions and social protection; andinclusion.
To achieve its objectives, the ESF aims to:
facilitate reskilling and upskilling and employability, career transitions, geographic and sectoral mobility and supporting in particular low-skilled people, persons, persons with disabilities and poorly qualified adults;
promote self-employment and the social economy;
encourage the participation of the most at all levels of society;
support the social inclusion of disadvantaged groups;
improve the quality of education, training and lifelong learning;
ensure a gender balanced labour market.
In order to achieve the objectives of the EASI strand, the European Commission has adopted a number of recommendations, including:
Recommendation of 15 March 2018 on a European Framework for Quality and Effective Apprenticeships, lifelong guidance, skills anticipation in close cooperation with industry, up-to-date training materials and delivery methods, forecasting and graduate tracking, training of educators, validation of learning outcomes and recognition of qualifications and industry-based certifications;
Council Recommendation of 22 November 2016 on the European Semester for economic policy coordination (the ‘European Semester’) and on the ‘Next steps for a sustainable European future’ as a first step in mainstreaming the United Nations (UN) Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) and applying sustainable development as an essential guiding principle for all EU policies, including through its financing instruments.
EU countries are required to identify and coordinate priority investment projects to be supported by EU or national funding. The Commission may reimburse EU countries using simplified cost options including lump sums and grants.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
   храбранна шарка  такала  баккашка
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Council adopted the decision on behalf of the EU on 20 June 2017.
For more information, see:
‘A sustainable European Future: the EU response to the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Social Fund: a European Union instrument for combating poverty and promoting social inclusion. It is one of the main instruments of the European Pillar of Social Rights (PSR).
* Employment: a social policy instrument for improving employment and social inclusion, which aims to reduce poverty and improve social inclusion by investing in relevant skills, education and training.
* Mobility: the ability of workers to move from one job to another and to participate in the economy.

Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps (ESC) 2017
European solidarity Corps 2017
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1288/2013 on solidarity activities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a single entry point for solidarity activities in the European Union (EU).
It aims to encourage young people to take part in solidarity activities with victims of crises and disasters in third countries and to raise both the awareness and the visibility of humanitarian aid, and volunteering in general, as a lifelong activity among EU citizens.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to solidarity activities of volunteering teams in priority areas identified at EU level and solidarity activities for humanitarian aid operations in non-EU countries.
It does not apply to activities in support of the European Structural and Investment Funds and the EU programmes relating to migration, security, justice and citizenship, health and culture.
Participation
EU citizens may take part as part of a solidarity project if they meet the following conditions:
they are at least 18 years old;
they do not have to be resident in the country in question;
the organisation of the project is based in a non-formal and informal learning context.
Organisation
Organisations must ensure that the volunteering takes place within the local context and that volunteers interact with local humanitarian actors, the hosting community and civil society.
Coaches, mentors and experts can be involved in volunteering in order to guide and accompany volunteers and help to support the development and capacity-building components of the volunteering, thereby strengthening local networks and communities.
Financing
EU countries are required to provide financial support for solidarity projects, including:
financial guarantees, financial assistance and reimbursement of external experts;
a quality label, provided that it complies with appropriate conditions.
The European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) has the power to carry out administrative investigations, including on fraud, and inspections, in relation to the participation of EU citizens in the ESC.
Procedures
The ESC is funded by the European Social Fund (ESF), the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF). The ESF and the EAF are the main sources of funding for the EU’s solidarity activities.
EU Member States are responsible for the implementation of the EU solidarity programme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Solidarity Corps’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 62:
European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGF) – 2021-2027 (European Pillar of Social Rights) (European Social Fund)
European General Fund (EGF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1927/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council establishing the European General Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European general fund to help displaced workers who have lost their jobs as a result of major structural changes in world trade patterns due to globalisation.
It is part of the EU’s multiannual financial framework (MFF) for 2021–2027.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EGF is a flexible and special instrument outside the budgetary ceilings of the MFF, as set out in the Commission communication entitled ‘A Modern Budget for a Union that Protects, Empowers and Defends The Multiannual Financial Framework for 2021-2020’ of 2 May 2018 and the annex thereto. It is funded by the European Social Fund (ESF+) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
Beneficiaries of the EGF are:
displaced workers and self-employed persons whose activity has ceased or whose employment contract or relationship has ceased;
persons who have been affected by a major restructuring event that has a significant impact on the local or regional economy;
people who have left the labour market due to the impact of the restructuring event.
The regulation sets out the eligibility criteria for beneficiaries.
EU countries must ensure that:
the EGF beneficiaries are provided with equal access to the fund, irrespective of their type of employment contract, relationship or relationship;
they have access to financial support;
the fund is open to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation;
beneficiaries receive equal access, without discrimination, to the same level of assistance as other EU citizens;
all beneficiaries are covered by the same eligibility criteria as other beneficiaries of the ESF and the ERDF.
Eligibility criteria
The European Commission publishes a list of eligible beneficiaries. The list is updated every 2 years. The Commission publishes the list of beneficiaries in accordance with Regulation (EU) 2019/2023/EU.
In addition, the Commission publishes an annual report on the implementation of EGF rules and measures taken by EU countries. It also publishes a summary of the number of beneficiaries and the results of the financial transfers made to beneficiaries in each year, as well as a breakdown of the beneficiaries’ contributions.
Funding
In order to support beneficiaries effectively and rapidly, EU countries must do their utmost to submit complete applications when applying for a financial contribution from the fund. They must also submit a detailed background analysis of the event that triggered the financial contribution, which must be made available to the Commission and to the public. In addition, they must provide the Commission with a detailed description of the reasons for the redundancies where relevant and anticipate future labour market perspectives and required skills.
Displaced workers must be provided with adequate information on their rights and obligations, including:
their rights and responsibilities;
their access to employment contracts, employment contracts and employment relationships;
a description of their rights, obligations and obligations under the employment contract and employment relationship, including their rights under the social security system;
access to social protection, including the right to a pension and the right of participation in the pension system.
To ensure that beneficiaries are not excluded from receiving financial support, the European Commission adopts a number of measures, such as:
measures to increase the representation of the less represented gender, thus contributing towards the reduction of the gender pay gap and the pension gap;
measures aimed at improving the participation of women in the decision-making process;
means to improve the inclusion of women and men in the selection and selection of beneficiaries, including by:
ensuring that women are included in the national selection criteria;
providing information on employment contracts;
assisting the Commission to identify and assess the needs of displaced workers;
supporting the Commission in the preparation of the annual work programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force
It applies from 2 May 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European general fund (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебранскабланна гандрашка банка   Бастральна
Council Regulation (EC, No 27/2006) of 20 May 2006 setting up the European general framework for the period 2007 to 2013 (the ‘Fund’).
Successive amendments and corrections to the Regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 63:
Transparency register for organisations and self-employed individuals engaged in EU policy-making and policy implementation — agreement between the European Parliament and the European Commission
Transparency registration for organisations, self-employment individuals involved in EU policies and policies
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on the registration of interest representatives
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — signing, on behalf of the signatory institutions, of the agreement on the register of interest representation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement establishes the European Union’s transparency register (EU Transparency Register).
The aim of the EU Transparency Register is to facilitate the exchange of information between EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies on the activities carried out by individuals who wish to participate in the formulation or implementation of EU policy or legislation.
The decision approves the signing and provisional application for registration in the register by the EU institutions.
KEY POINTS
The register is open to all individuals and organisations wishing to take part in the decision-making processes of EU institutions and bodies.
Activities covered by the agreement include:
making submissions in response to direct and specific requests from any of the Union institutions, their representatives or staff, for factual information, data or expertise;
activities carried out in a strictly personal capacity and not in association with others;
spontaneous meetings, meetings of a purely private or social character and meetings taking place in the context of an administrative procedure established by the TEU or TFEU or the European Court of Auditors.
Applicants are eligible to be entered into the register if they carry out covered activities and observe the code of conduct set out in Annex I (‘code of conduct’). They must also:
cooperate sincerely and constructively in accordance with the procedures laid down in Annex III;
participate in the management board of the register, which is chaired by the Secretaries-General of the institutions, for a term of one year;
ensure that the information they provide to the register continues to be accurate;
respect the confidentiality of their registration information.
Organisations, bodies and agencies that are not EU institutions or bodies are not eligible to register.
In addition, the register does not apply to:
non-governmental organisations, bodies or agencies, other than the signatories;
national or subnational level engaging in interest representation activities;
individuals acting in a non-governmental capacity;
organisations and bodies not involved in the EU policy making process, or the decision making processes of the European institutions or other EU institutions;
other EU bodies or bodies not associated with the EU.
Registration procedure
The European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions adopt individual decisions concerning applicants and registrants, in line with this agreement. The register is managed by a management board, which comprises the secretaries-general and the head of the secretariat. The management board adopts the decisions concerning the registration and the operation of the registry. The secretariat is assisted by a head of unit.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Decisions (EU, Euratom and EU countries) have to incorporate the agreement into national law by 31 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU Transparency register is one of the most important instruments of transparency and openness at EU level. It enables stakeholders to present their views on decisions that may affect them and hence to contribute effectively to the evidence base on which policy proposals are made.
For more information, see:
European Union Transparency Register (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interest representation: any activity carried out on the initiative of an individual or organisation with the objective of influencing the formulation, implementation or adoption of EU policies or legislation, or of the decisionmaking processes.
* Self-employed person: any person who wishes to participate, in a purely personal capacity, in any way, in the process of making a contribution to EU policy.
ACT

Summary 64:
Budgetary discipline and cooperation between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission: the multiannual financial framework 2021-2027 (CFSP 2021-2020) (Agreement on budgetary discipline, cooperation in budgetary matters and sound financial management)
Budgets and budgetary discipline
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/2021/CFSP — Interinstitutional Agreement on the implementation of budgetary discipline between the EU institutions and the Commission on budgetary matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s annual budgetary procedure and cooperate between the institutions on budgetary issues as well as the Commission and the Council.
The decision adopts the agreement on behalf of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027 is the framework for the EU budget. It covers the period from 2021 to 2027. The agreement replaces the agreement of 2 December 2013, which entered into force on 16 December 2013.
In the framework of the MFF, the agreement covers all expenditure. The total amount of CFSP operating expenditure is divided between the articles of the CFSP budget chapter as suggested in the third paragraph of the agreement. The Commission will examine how to develop a methodology to measure the relevant expenditure at programme level. This methodology will be transparent, comprehensive, result-oriented and performance-based, and will identify relevant measures to be taken in case of insufficient progress towards achieving applicable targets. It will include annual consultation by the Commission, the Commission of the Parliament and of the Council in order to identify the necessary measures to achieve the relevant targets.
CFSP actions are divided into the following categories:
single major missions;
other missions (for crisis management operations, conflict prevention, resolution and stabilisation, and monitoring and implementation of peace and security processes);
non-proliferation and disarmament;
emergency measures;
preparatory and follow-up measures; and
European Union Special Representatives.
Each year the Commission will update the forecasts for payment appropriations for the period at least until 2027 at least every 3 years.
If appropriations relating to fisheries agreements, including the reserve, prove insufficient, it must provide the European parliament and the council with the necessary information on the causes of the situation and on measures which might be adopted under established procedures.
Where the conditions for mobilising the Brexit Adjustment Reserve set out in the relevant instrument are met, it will submit a proposal to the European European Parliament and to the Council for a transfer from that reserve to the relevant budget lines in accordance with the Financial Regulation.
When a proposal for a new MFF or for a substantial revision has been presented, the Institutions will seek to determine specific arrangements for cooperation and dialogue between them throughout the procedure leading to its adoption.

Economic and Social support for CoVID - 2019
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1046/2018/EU on the financing of economic and social measures in response to the crisis caused by COVID19
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out how the EU’s financial instruments are to be used to support the economic recovery of the Member States affected by the crisis.
It is part of the multiannual financial framework for the period 2014-2023.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the financial instruments and budgetary guarantees granted by the European Commission to Member States that have been particularly hard hit by the COVID crisis;
the budgetary guarantees provided by the Commission to the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Stability Mechanism (ESM);

The financial instruments provided under the regulation are:
non-repayable support, support through financial instruments or provisioning for budgetary guarantees and related expenditure by the EU;
support for technical and administrative assistance for implementation of the measures set out in the regulation;
funding for agriculture and development in rural areas in addressing the impact of the crisis; and
financial support for the implementation of EU programmes.
In addition to the financial instruments, the regulation also provides for the following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the European Union (EU).
Funding
the regulation sets out the amount of the financial resources available to the EU under this regulation:
EUR 384 400 million in 2018 prices, of which € 5.6 billion will be used for loans to EU Member States;
€ 5.5 billion will constitute external assigned revenue to EU programmes;
Eur 5.2 billion will contribute to the cost-sharing of the costs of implementing the measures under the Regulation;
EU programmes which are not covered by this regulation will not be covered by the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMATERNICAL REGULATIONS APPLY?

BACKGROUND
The COVID–19 crisis has caused significant disturbances to economic activity which are reflected in a steep decline in gross domestic product and a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities. Many businesses are experiencing liquidity shortages and their solvency is at risk, while the financial markets are very volatile.
To prevent further deterioration of the economy, employment and social cohesion and to boost a sustainable and resilient recovery of economic activity, an exceptional and coordinated programme is put in place, in a spirit of solidarity between Member States.

Summary 67:
Population and housing censuses: methodology, definitions and programme of the statistical data and the metadata (Regulation (EC) No 596/2008)
Population and Housing censuses : methodology, definition and programme thereof (Regulations (EC, EC, EU, Eurostat)
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU) No 550/2008 on population and housing statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of statistics by the European Commission (Eurostat) to the European Parliament and the Council on the population, social and economic characteristics of persons, families and households, as well as on housing at a national, regional and local level.
KEY POINTS
Each EU country must submit to Eurostat data on its population covering determined demographic, social, economic and gender characteristics of people, families, households and households.
Data submission
EU countries must submit data to the Commission:
total population,
locality,
household status*,
family status,
type of family nucleus,
size of family unit,
number of members,
private household quarters,
the size of private household dwellings,
location of place of usual residence,
occupancy of households, and
the number of occupants.
Eurostat must adopt the technical specifications of the topics as required by this regulation, in terms of their breakdowns, in accordance with the regulatory procedure referred to in Article 8(2) of Council Decision 1999/468/EC.
The Commission must adopt a programme of statistical data to be transmitted to the Council of the European Union for the transmission of the relevant data. The programme must include:
a combination of register-based censuses and sample surveys;
an appropriate surveys with rotating samples (rolling censuses);
appropriate surveys with rotations (rolling surveys).
The data must be transmitted by Eurostat using the appropriate technical format to be used for transmitting the required data, in line with the technical procedures set out in Article 9(3) of the regulation;
the chosen data sources and methodology meet the essential features of population* and housing* censuses, as defined in Article 2(i) of this regulation.
In applying the quality assessment dimensions laid down in paragraph 1 to the data covered by the regulation, the modalities and structure of the quality reports must be defined in accordance to the rules laid down by the statistical programme committee. The Commission must be assisted by the Statistical Programme Committee.
Repeal
The regulation repeals Council Regulation (EC), No 608/2006 and its subsequent amendments.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Population: the number of people in a given EU country.
* Household: the total number of persons living in a household, divided into households and divided into individual households, according to the categories of persons and families defined in the annexes to the regulation (e.g. number of households per 1000 inhabitants).
* Housing: the average number of rooms in a house of a family of a population of a given size.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure of the European Parliament
Rights of the Members of Parliament
Rules of procedure of the Member States
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2019/302/JHA — Rules of Procedure for the 9th parliamentary term
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE RULES OF PROCEDURE?
The decision adopts the rules of procedure for the ninth parliamentary term (2019-2023).
The rules are designed to ensure that Members of the Parliament exercise their mandate freely and independently, without being bound by any instructions and do not receive a binding mandate.
KEY POINTS
The European Parliament is the assembly elected pursuant to the Treaties, the Act of 20 September 1976 concerning the election of the members of the EU Parliament by direct universal suffrage and national legislation deriving from those Treaties.
Member States are responsible for appointing the members. The rules apply to all Member States, other than those which, under that Act, fall exclusively under the national provisions to which that Act refers.
Members are elected for a term of 5 years. The term of office of an outgoing Member of Parliament ends on the date communicated by the competent authorities of an EU Member State.
The president of the Council of Europe is responsible for verifying the credentials of individual Members who are replacing outgoing Members and may at any time rule on any dispute as to the validity of the mandate of any of its Members.
When a Member resigns, the President must inform the committee responsible of the date on which that resignation is to take effect, which may not be more than 3 months after notification. If the committee considers that the resignation is in compliance with the act, a vacancy is declared with effect from the date indicated by the resigning Member in the official record, and the President informs Parliament thereof.
In the event of a dispute between the President and the Committee responsible, the president may, without delay, rule on the validity or validity of any Member’s mandate. The committee may also adopt a proposal for the committee to verify the membership of individual members who are replaced.
All Members have the right to participate actively in the work of Parliament's committees and delegations in accordance with the provisions of these rules. Access to confidential information is subject to the rules laid down in interinstitutional agreements concluded by Parliament relating to the treatment of confidential information.
Exemptions from parliamentary duties
Members have immunity from all parliamentary duties. They are not obliged to give evidence in the course of their parliamentary duties, except in cases where it would be difficult for them to do so. They may, however, invoke their immunity if they consider that they have been informed in writing that they will not be able to comply with their duties or that they are not confident that they do not fulfil their duties, or if they have not been informed of the reasons for their immunity.

Act

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) — mission and objectives
European Data Processing Supervisor (EDPB) — objectives
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/1725 establishing the European Union’s independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data, are respected by the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes an independent authority, referred to as the European data protection supervisor.
The regulation sets out the responsibilities and powers of the EDPS.
KEY POINTS
The EDPS is responsible for:
ensuring that:
the fundamental rights, freedoms and fundamental rights of the natural persons who have the right to personal data are respected;
the protection of their personal data is respected by EU institutions and bodies;
data protection is respected in particular by the European Data Protection Board (EDPB);
the EDPB ensures the protection of the personal data of EU citizens.
Duties and powers
The role of the EU data protection supervisory authority is to:
supervise and monitor the implementation of the data protection rules and regulations of EU institutions;
ensure that the rights of individuals and their rights are respected in relation to processing of their data;
cooperate with the European Commission, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions and the European Court of Auditors.
the European Commission and the Court of Justice.
It is assisted by the Directorate-General for the Protection of the Internal Market (ECP) and the Committee for Fundamental Rights and Freedoms.
Role of the Director and the Director of Unit and Sectors
The director and the director of the unit and the head of the sector are responsible for the overall coordination of the office. The director assists the director to ensure consistency and overall coordination and on any other tasks delegated to him or her by the supervisor. The Director may also delegate, where appropriate and in accordance with the regulation, to the Director or to the Head of Unit or Head of Sector concerned, the power to adopt and sign other documents.
In addition, the director and head of unit or head of sector are assisted by:
an Authorising Officer;
an Administrative Officer; and
an Executive Officer.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
They have applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European data protection supervisors (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* European data Protection Supervisor: a European Union institution, body, office or agency responsible for protecting personal data.
* Data Protection Officer: a person whose personal data has been processed by an EU institution or body, body or agency.

Summary 70:

Recovery & Resiliency Facility 2021–2027: own resources
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2021/2027 establishing a new system of own resources based on value added tax (VAT)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes the EU’s own resources* for the period 2021-2020.
It aims to ensure that the EU has sufficient financial capacity in the event of an economic crisis.
KEY POINTS
The decision establishes a new category of own resource based on national contributions calculated on the basis of non-recycled plastic packaging waste.
The new own resources are:
—
the VAT base of 3.8 kilograms multiplied by the population in 2017 of the EU countries concerned. This is equal to 3.4 % of the gross national income (GNI) of each EU country, i.e. the value of all taxable supplies divided by the weighted average VAT rate calculated for the relevant calendar year as stipulated in Council Regulation (EEC) No 1553/89 (see summary);
— and
the weight of the weight of all non-precious metals in the EU.
VAT base of 1.8 kg of the VAT base for each country. This corresponds to 3,8 kg multiplied by 3.3.8 % of GNI.
This will be increased to 1.40 % of EU countries’ GNIs by 2021. This will allow the European Commission to borrow up to €142 million from the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF. This amount will be used to finance the recovery and resilience measures of the COVID-19 crisis. The amount will not exceed 1.3 % of all EU countries' GNIs.
In addition, the decision sets out the rules for the early repayment of loans from the ERDF.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Decision applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The COVID–19 crisis was the first major crisis to hit the EU since the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) in the early 1980s. Since then, the ERF has been used to help the EU cope with economic and financial difficulties.

‘The EU's own resources’ on the Commission's website.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1242 on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to encourage end-users to purchase more fuel-efficient tyres by providing them with harmonised information on the tyre parameters.
It sets out the requirements for tyre labels.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
new tyres;
re-treaded tyres*;
new heavy-duty vehicles;
vehicles intended for use on the roads of the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to tyres intended for road transport.
The regulations cover:
the rolling resistance parameter;
the tyre abrasion and mileage parameters;
traffic noise.
In addition, the regulation sets out minimum ice grip performance standards, which are based on the International Organisation for Standardisation (ISO) 19447 standard.
EU countries must ensure that the information on tyre labels and on technical promotional material on tyres at the point of sale is available to distributors as well as potential consumers.
Manufacturers must provide the European Commission with technical documentation on the relevant tyre parameters at the time and place of the purchasing decision. This technical documentation must be made publicly available in the public part of the product database.
Where technical information is too sensitive to include it in the category of technical documentation, market surveillance authorities must have access to that information when necessary in accordance with the duty of cooperation on suppliers or by way of additional parts of the technical documentation uploaded to the product databases.
the Commission must assess the feasibility of adding additional information to the tyre label. The Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the content and format of the regulation to adapt the annexes to technological progress and to adapt it to technological developments.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLYIELD?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.

The EU’s transport sector accounts for a third of the EU's energy consumption. To reduce the environmental impact of road transport, it is therefore appropriate to update the provisions on the label of tyres.

Summary 72:
Funding for border management and border control equipment
Funding of border management & visa policy
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 establishing the Instrument for Financial Support for Border Management and Visa Policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s first instrument for financial support for customs control equipment.
It aims to improve the performance of customs controls at the external borders of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes the Fund for Financial support for border-management and visa policy (FMSDP).
The aim of the FMSDP is to:
increase the effectiveness of the customs control system at EU external borders;
improve the efficiency of border controls;
contribute to preventing the diversion of flows of goods towards the weakest points in the system, often referred to as ‘import point shopping’;
ensure the protection of the financial and economic interests of the EU and its Member States.
The instrument supports the costs of equipment of which the primary aim or effect is integrated border management, but will also allow that equipment to be used for additional purposes, such as border controls and security.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of the instrument. The European Commission is responsible for administering the instrument and for its implementation.
In addition, the Commission is assisted by the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA) and the Directorate-General for Customs and Border Control.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
  
It entered into force on 1 April 2017.
1.4.2017.
BACKGROUND
Customs authorities of the Member States have been taking on an increasing number of responsibilities which are carried out at the EU's external borders and which often extend into the security field.

Summary 73:
Ensuring the liberalisation of the internal market for electricity (Directive 2014/36/EU)
Ensolving the liberalised internal market: Directive 2014/35/EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2014/34/EU on the implementation of the European Energy Union (EEU) objectives
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It aims to ensure that the EU’s internal electricity market is open to all, including producers of renewable energy, new energy service providers, energy storage and flexible demand.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that no undue barriers exist within the market entry, operation and exit of electricity suppliers.
The directive applies to all electricity suppliers, including:
energy producers;
energy service providers;
new energy providers.
It does not apply to public service undertakings, which are not covered by the directive.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that public service obligations in price setting for the supply of electricity are reasonable and proportionate;
provide adequate economic incentives, using, where appropriate, any existing national and EU tools.
To this end, the directive requires EU countries to:
make bills clearer and easier to understand;
make billing and billing information more easily accessible to consumers;
promote the participation of consumers in the electricity market;
encourage consumers to take full advantage of the opportunities of a liberalised electricity market.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It entered into force on 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
Electricity is one of the most important energy sources in the EU. It is the largest source of electricity for households and businesses.

Regulation (EU) 2021/695 establishing the European Partnerships for the development of high performance computing and quantum technologies in the framework of Horizon Europe
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The regulation establishes a joint undertaking (JUB) for the implementation of the strategic EU vision in the field of supercomputing, quantum computing and data capabilities.
The JUB aims to:
ensure that the EU enjoys world-class supercomputer capabilities;
provide and manage access to a federated and interconnected supercomputer and data infrastructure and its services;
promote the industrial and market take-up of high-performance computing technologies;
contribute to the creation of a European supercomputer infrastructure.
KEY POINTS
JUB objectives
The objectives of the JUB include:
promoting a common strategic Union vision in high-performing computing and in quantum computing;
ensuring that European supercomputers have the computing performance needed to maintain their research and industrial capacities at a leading edge;
encouraging the use and development of new technologies in this field;
creating a European network of supercomputer services and applications;
developing and managing access to the supercomputer resources;
supporting the development and use of new supercomputer-related services and products;
facilities;
enhancing the use of the capabilities of the European super-computing infrastructure;
fostering cooperation between the public and private sectors.
Jubilee strategic programme
Joint undertaking
The joint undertaking is the best instrument capable of implementing the EU vision of the EU in the area of High-Performance Computing and Q-Computing. It is composed of:
1.
the European Commission;
2. the European Parliament and the Council;
3.the European Economic and Social Committee;
4.the Committee of the Regions;
5.participating countries.
Participating countries
Each participating country must conclude one or more administrative agreements with the joint undertaking, setting out the coordination mechanism for the payment of and reporting on contributions to applicants established in that participating country.
In addition, each participating country is required to cooperate with the Partnership for Advanced Computing in Europe (PRACE), which provides and manages access to supercomputer facilities and services in Europe, as well as with the European Network of Supercomputing Facilities (ENSCF) and the Connecting Europe Facility (CSPF).
The Joint undertaking is funded by the European Commission under the multiannual financial framework for the years 2021 to 2027 (the ‘MFF 2021–2027’).
Financial contributions from members other than the EU should be at least equal to 50 % and should be able to reach up to 75 % of the aggregated joint undertaking budgetary commitments.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a new legal framework for Horizon Europe.
For more information, see:
High Performance Computing (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* High-performance computer: a supercomputer with the capability to process, analyse, process, process and process data rapidly and efficiently.
* Quantum computing: a system with the potential to be used for the generation, processing, storage and transmission of information and communication of information in a non-linear way.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EU, 2021/694 of 11 December 2019 establishing the Joint Undertakings for the Development of High Performance Computers and Quantum Computers in the context of the Horizon Europe programme for the period 2021 to 2021 (OJ L 304, 12.12.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2021 /694 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
