Summary 75:
Climate-neutrality by 2050: EU-wide 2050 climate-neutralisation objective (Regulation (EC) No 401/2009)
Climate - neutralisation by 2050
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU-Wide 2050 Climate-Nutrality Objective
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s objective of achieving net-zero greenhouse gas emissions in the EU by 2050 through a socially-fair and cost-efficient transition.
KEY POINTS
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change and to delivering on the implementation of the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’).
To achieve this objective, the EU must:
reduce its greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by 30% by 2020 compared to 1990 levels;
reduced its GHG emissions by 40% by 2050 compared with 1990 levels.
This objective is achieved through a transition to a safe, sustainable, affordable and secure energy system relying on the deployment of renewables, a well-functioning internal energy market and the improvement of energy efficiency, while reducing energy poverty.
The objective is also achieved through the transition towards a climate-resilient economy based on a low-carbon economy.
In order to achieve these objectives, the Commission is calling on all EU countries to:
ensure that their policies and actions are aligned with the EU 2020 climate action strategy;
take the necessary measures to achieve the EU 2050 climate neutralisation objective;
make climate finance flows consistent with a pathway towards low greenhouse gas emission targets and climate resilience.
To ensure that the EU achieves its 2050 climate neutrality objective, it calls on the EU countries and the European Commission to take the necessary actions.
It calls on EU countries, the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission to ensure that:
the EU is a global leader in climate action;
all EU countries are committed to achieving climate neutrality by 2050;
carbon sinks play an essential role in the transition to climate neutrality, and in particular the agriculture, forestry and land use sectors make an important contribution in that context.
EU countries are called upon to take all necessary measures, including:
putting in place the necessary infrastructure and systems in place to meet the climate neutrality target;
ensuring that the transition is financed in a way that ensures that it is sustainable and fair;
taking the necessary steps to ensure a balanced and fair transition, taking into account different national circumstances in terms of starting points.
A climate-adoption platform
The Commission has launched a European climate and health observatory under the European Climate Adaptation Platform (Climate-ADAPT), to better understand, anticipate and minimise the health threats caused by climate change.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘A Clean Planet for all’ is a European strategic long-term vision for a prosperous, modern, competitive and climate-nouveau economy. It sets out a vision for achieving a clean planet for all by 2050.
For more information, see:
‘Forging a climate resilient Europe’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 76:
Public health: EU4Health (2020-2023)
Public health : EU4H (2020 -2023).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 282/2014 establishing the EU 4Health programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the EU4health programme, which aims to promote and improve public health in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to:
promote and improve the quality of health throughout the lifetime of an individual by addressing health risk factors, and health determinants, which would also contribute to the attainment of the UN 2030 Agenda Sustainable Development Goals;
improve the interoperability of EU countries’ health systems through cooperation and the exchange of best practices;
reduce and prevent alcohol-related harm, with particular emphasis on protecting the young;
increase the number of joint actions.
The objectives of the programme are:
to promote disease prevention and healthy lifestyles;
to counter misinformation and disinformation as regards the prevention, cause and treatment of diseases, to address vaccine hesitancy and to support efforts to strengthen altruistic behaviour, such as organ and blood donations, in a manner that complements national campaigns on those matters.
In addition, the programme supports:
health promotion and disease prevention activities;
health education and awareness-raising campaigns;
promotion of the importance of preventive and curative measures;
the exchange of information and best practice in the field of public health;
coordination of actions between EU countries.
EU4Health is funded by the EU’s general budget and by the European Social Fund (ESF).
The budget is divided between:
the general budget for the programme and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
the ERDF for the ERDF; and
the European Investment Bank (EIB) for the EDF.
Specific areas of action
The Programme supports actions in relation to which there are advantages and efficiency gains from collaboration and cooperation at EU level, and actions that have an impact on the internal market. These include:
improving the quality and availability of medicines and medical devices;
encouraging the development of innovative products and technologies;
strengthening the supply chain of medicinal products;
providing information to the general public and to specific groups of people or professionals;
contributing to the prevention and control of communicable diseases;
supporting the implementation of the COVID-19 outbreak;
raising awareness of the need to prepare for and respond to serious cross-border threats to health.
Cooperation and cooperation
The European Union is committed to strengthening cooperation between its Member States in the area of health. To this end, the European Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures, including:
a communication on the EU's public health strategy;
a Communication on the European Green Deal (the ‘European Green Deal’);
an action plan for the 2020-2025 period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU 4 Health Programme is one of the three pillars of the European Pillar of Social Rights. The other two pillars are the European Health Fund (EHF) and the Instrument for Pre-accession Assistance III (IP III).
For more information, see:
‘EU 4 Health’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine products — rules on production, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications (Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011)
Aromatic wine products – rules on presentation and labelling
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1219/2014 on the definition, description and presentation of aromatised wines
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the production of aromatic wines.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1601/91 and replaces it with this regulation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
aromatic wines produced in the EU for export;
wine products with a geographical indication (e.g. a region, a specific place, or a country);
wines with an alcoholic strength by volume of more than 1.2 % vol.
The rules apply to the presentation of these wines, as well as the description of the wine and the labelling of these products.
Production and presentation
EU countries must ensure that:
the products are produced in accordance with the requirements, restrictions and descriptions laid down in Annexes I and II of the regulation;
they comply with the rules in the annexes to the regulation, except where they do not comply with Annexes III and IV.
they must not use more than one sales denomination, the use of only one of those sales denominations is authorised, except in the case where Annex II provides otherwise.
They must also ensure that the products are accompanied by the customary name of the product within the meaning of the regulations.
Labelling
The products must be accompanied by a description of their characteristics and by a list of the specific characteristics of the aromatic wine product.
If a product does not conform to the requirements of Annexes II and III, the product may be referred to as ‘dry’, ‘sweetened’ or ‘fruity’.
In addition to this, the regulation sets out rules on the presentation and presentation, supplementing Annexes IV and V, of the products covered by Annexes VII and VIII.

Summary 80:
Fishing opportunities for European eel in the EU waters of the International Commission for the Regulation on the Law of the Sea (ICES) 2020/973 and Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the management of fish stocks and fishery opportunities in the European Union (EU waters of ICES) [See amending acts].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the fishing opportunities available to EU fishing vessels for certain species of fish in the International Council of the Mediterranean and the North Atlantic.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
European eel
European seabass
Horse mackerel
European shark
Atlantic blue sharks.
It does not apply to certain fish stocks, such as:
fishing opportunities* for European sharks,
bycatch* for by-catch* in mixed fisheries.
The fishing opportunities are based on scientific advice from the Scientific, Technical and Economic Committee for Fisheries (STECF) and other advisory bodies, as well as any advice received from specific advisory councils.
EU Member States are responsible for determining the level of fishing opportunities for each fish stock or fishery.
Where a TAC relating to a stock is allocated to one Member State only, it is up to that Member State to determine the level.
If a Member State has no quota to cover their unavoidable by-catches, the Member State is responsible for the allocation of the TAC.
In addition, Member States must ensure that:
the number of fishing vessels participating in a mixed fishery does not exceed the number of vessels;
the total number of fish caught in a given year is not exceeded by more than the maximum sustainable yield (MSY);
there is no obligation to land all catches from those stocks, as this would lead to the phenomenon of ‘choke species’.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulations apply from 1 August 2020 and 28 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a multiannual plan for the EU’s western waters.
For more information, see:
Fishing Opportunities in the Western Waters (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* By-catch: fish caught during the fishing closure period, i.e. the period during which the fishing vessels are not allowed to fish.

EU macro–financial assistance
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/1046 — EU’s macro‐financial assistance for Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the EU to provide a maximum amount of €1.2 billion of economic and financial assistance to the Ukrainian authorities in order to support the economic stabilisation and substantive reform agenda of the country.

strengthen the economic resilience of the Ukrainian economy;
accompany Ukraine in its efforts to implement structural reforms;
support the structural reform agenda, supplementing resources made available under the International Monetary Fund (IMF) financial arrangement.
The assistance is subject to economic policy conditions, to be set out in a memorandum of understanding (MOU). The European Commission is empowered to negotiate these conditions with Ukraine under the supervision of the committee of representatives of the EU countries.

The financial assistance will be available from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2022.
BACKGROUND
In spring 2014, Ukraine embarked on an ambitious reform programme with the aim of stabilising its economy and improving the livelihoods of its citizens. The programme was suspended when the country was unable to access the international capital markets. The continuous build-up of uncertainty has resulted in a recent loss of confidence, negatively affecting the economic outlook and, since mid-January 2022, in the economic prospects.

Summary 82:
Mediterranean swordfish: management and control measures
MEDIUM FRAMEWORK FISHING REGULATION
Mediterranean swords fishing: management measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2107 — management measures and controls for Mediterranean swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
   
The regulation aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), as a whole, is able to achieve a biomass of swordfish corresponding to the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) by 2031, with at least a 60 % probability of achieving that objective.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009.
KEY POINTS
The EU is party to the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (‘the ICCAT Convention’). To that end, including avoiding the collapse of the stock, and after analysing the scientific advice of the Standing Committee on Research and Statistics (SCRS), ICCAT adopted Recommendation 16-05 establishing a multiannual recovery plan for the stock.
The Regulation applies to the allocation of quotas for the years 2018 and 2019.
EU countries must use transparent and objective criteria, including those of an environmental, social and economic nature, when allocating the fishing opportunities available to them, and shall also endeavour to distribute national quotas fairly among the various fleet segments, giving consideration to traditional and artisanal fishing, and to provide incentives to EU fishing vessels deploying selective fishing gear or using fishing techniques with reduced environmental impact.
Fishing effort
EU vessels must:
fishing in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EU countries’ annual fishing plans;
make provision for bycatch within their quota and inform the Commission thereof when transmitting its annual fishing plan to the European Commission;
ensure that vessels that exceed their allocated quota or their maximum level of permitted by-catches are not allowed to continue to fish;
keep a record of all swordfish caught and processed in the EU;
notify the Commission by 1 March of each year the measures taken to limit the number of fishing vessels flying EU flag and authorised flag and flying the flag of EU countries flying the EU flag.
FROM WHEN DO THESE REGULATES APPLY?
The regulations entered into force on 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission adopted the Regulation in December 2016.
For more information, see:
‘Mediterranean Swordfish Regulation’ on the Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Regulation (U.S. 2015/98 of 21 December 2015 on the allocation and management of the quota for the fishery of the Mediterranean Swordfish (Codification Regulation) (OJ L 347, 28.12.2015, pp. 1-2)

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework for the period 2014-2020
Multi-iannual Financial Framework
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2014/2014 establishing a multiannual framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the framework of the European Union (EU)’s (now the EU) annual financial framework (MFF) from 2014 to 2020.
It aims to ensure that the EU budget is sound and predictable.
KEY POINTS
The MFF covers the general budget of the EU and the EU countries’ contributions to it.
The regulation sets out:
the general budget;
the European Union Solidarity Fund, the Flexibility Instrument, the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund and the Contingency Margin, the specific flexibility to tackle youth unemployment and strengthen research and the global margin for commitments for growth and employment, in particular youth employment.
In addition, the MFF includes special instruments such as the Emergency Aid Reserve and the International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor (ITER).
Scope
The framework runs from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020. It covers:
a total budget of €7.7 billion for the 2014-20 period;
a maximum amount of €9.9 billion for 2015-2020;
€10 billion for 2018-2021.
Specific instruments
The European Union Strategic Fund (ESF)
The ESF is designed to provide financial assistance in the event of major disasters occurring on the territory of an EU Member State or of a candidate country. It is managed by the European Commission. The ESFlexibility instrument
The EEFF is a flexible instrument that allows the Commission to make technical adjustments to the annual MFF payment ceiling. The Commission may make these technical adjustments on the basis of a fixed deflator of 2 % per year.
Contingency margin
The contingency margin is a percentage of the gross domestic product (GDP) of the Member States that contribute to the overall EU budget. This is calculated by taking into account the contribution of each Member State to the total EU budget and the contribution from the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (AFRD) and direct payments.
EU Solidarity fund
This fund provides financial assistance to Member States in the case of natural or man-made disasters. It has a maximum annual contribution of €2.5 billion.
Emergency Aid reserve
The emergency aid reserve is intended to allow Member States to receive financial assistance during major disasters. The maximum amount is €1.2 billion. It can be used for the following purposes:
emergency aid for the European satellite navigation programmes (EGNOS and Galileo) from the EU general budget, and
the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA).
EU solidarity fund
The EU solidarity fund contributes to the EU's general budget. It contributes to:
defence and foreign and security policy;
transitional assistance for Member States and candidate countries;
economic and social development;
development assistance;
research and innovation;
environmental protection;
social protection.
Special instruments
In 2014, the Commission adopted a regulation setting out the following special instruments:
The Emergency Aid reserve:
€1.3 billion;
Flexible Instrument:
EUR 6 300 million (in 2011 prices);
European Globalisation adjustment fund:
1.8 billion; and
contingency margins:
0.5% of the overall budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘MFF’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1401/2014 of 25 November 2014 establishing a framework for a seven-year period 2014–2020 (OJ L 127, 30.11.2014, pp. 1–8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) no 1401 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 84:
European Maritime Fund for the Conservation of Atlantic Fisheries (EMFAF) — priorities and rules for providing EU funding (Regulation (EU) 2021/1060)
European Maritime fund for the conservation of Atlantic fisheries (EMF)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 552/2018 — EMFAF
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP) and the European Maritime Fund.
It sets out the priorities and the specific rules for the EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the European Parliament and of the Council of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The European Maritime and Fisheries Fund aims to support the Common Fisheries Policy, the EU's maritime policy and the Union's international commitments in the field of ocean governance.
The CFP aims to:
restore and maintain populations of harvested species above levels which can produce maximum sustainable yield (MSY);
prevent and reduce, as far as possible, unwanted catches;
minimise the negative impact of fishing activities on the marine ecosystem.
To achieve these objectives, the EMF contributes to achieving the environmental, economic, social and employment objectives of the CFP, as set out in Article 2 of the Partnership Agreement between the EU and its Member States.
Funding
The EMF is funded by the EU budget. It has a budget of €1.8 billion. It is divided into 3 main categories:
1.1 billion for the common fisheries and aquaculture policy;
2.3 billion for EU maritime policy; and3.2 billion for maritime maritime policy.
3.4 billion for other EU maritime policies.
Eligibility rules
Investment in the fishing fleet
Investments and compensation for the fleet must be strictly conditional on their consistency with the conservation objectives of CFP. They must not contribute to overcapacity or overfishing in the EU fishing fleet.
In addition, investments and compensation must be based on:
the number of vessels registered in EU waters;
the quality of the fishing gear and equipment used; and
the quantity of fish caught.
EU countries are responsible for the management of the fleet. The Commission is responsible for managing the funds and for monitoring the implementation of the EU fisheries policy. It may also suspend payments and impose financial corrections in cases of serious non-compliance with the rules of the fisheries policy by a Member State.
Cooperation with other EU programmes
The partnership agreement between the European Fisheries Fund and other Union programmes is described in the Partnership agreement, in accordance with Article 22(4) of the Regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Common Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund is one of the most important fisheries policy instruments in the world. It aims to ensure the long-term sustainability of the marine environment and the sustainable management of marine ecosystems.
For more information, see:
Common Fisheries Policy (European Commission).

Summary 85:
Fishing closure periods for tropical tunas
Fishing closures for tropical tuna
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2403 on the fishing closure for purse seine vessels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the long-term conservation and sustainable use of the fishery resources of the Pacific Ocean in the area covered by the Inter-American Tropical Tuna Commission (IATTC) Convention.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1005/2008.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to fishing vessels operating in the following areas:
the Pacific Ocean bounded by the following lines: the 50°S parallel from its intersection with meridian 150°W until the intersection of meridian 130°W;
the 50°W parallel from the intersection with 150°S until the 45°S; and
the 4°S perpendicular from its intersect with 150.
The IATTC Convention covers the whole of the area.
EU fishing vessels must:
not operate within 1 nautical mile of a data buoy, provided that those vessels do not interact with the data buoy or deploy fishing gear, as set out in paragraphs 1 and 2 of the regulation;
fishing vessels must comply with the rules of the International Commission for the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fishes (ICMFF) and with regard to the design and deployment of fish-aggregating devices (FADs);
operate within the limits laid down in Annexes I and II to Regulation (EU, EC) No 1224/2009 on the use of FADs;
not use any fishing gear that is not attached to the vessel’s propeller;
use no fishing gear other than those fitted on board the vessel;
keep a logbook and unloading records, the bigeye tuna statistical document, several reporting deadlines and the turtle mortality guidelines.
Implementation
Each EU country must notify the European Commission by 15 June each year of which of the two closure periods from 29 July to 8 October or from 9 November to 19 January, shall be applicable to their vessels.
If a vessel has already observed a closure period in the same year during which the cause of the forcemajeure occurred, it must observe a reduced closure period of 40 consecutive days the following year, to be notified to the Commission no later than 15 July of that year. The Commission must assess and, where applicable, forward the request for consideration by the International Advisory Committee for the Implementation of the Convention to the Secretariat for consideration.
In addition, EU countries must record, through the observer programmes, the number of catches and releases of bluefin tuna and whiteetipipipids, as well as the number and number of dead and frozen bluefin sharks.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 15 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on the International Conservation of Tropical Tunas (ICC-19-01) was adopted in 1982.
For more information, see:
International Convention on Tropical Tuna (ICT-19).
KEY TERMS
* Purse seine vessel: a fishing vessel of less than 500 gross tonnage.
* Tuna: any vessel that is used for the fishing of tropical tuna.

Summary 86:
Better law-making in the EU: Common Understanding between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission
Better law making in the European Union: Common Agreement with the EU institutions
SUMMARY OF:
Joint Political Declaration of 27 October 2011 on the improvement of the quality of EU legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The joint political declaration establishes a framework for cooperation between the three European Union (EU) institutions:
the European Parliament and the Council,
the Council of Europe, and
the Commission.
The aim of the joint declaration is to improve the quality and efficiency of the EU’s legislation, while avoiding overregulation and administrative burdens.
KEY POINTS
The three institutions agree that the analysis of the potential ‘European added value’ of any proposed EU action, as well as an assessment of the ‘cost of non-Europe’ in the absence of action at EU level, should be fully taken into account when setting the legislative agenda.
In order to achieve this, the three institutions commit to:
public and stakeholder consultations;
impact assessments;
ex-post evaluations;
reporting, monitoring and evaluation requirements in legislation;
transparency and accountability.
To achieve these objectives, they commit to, as appropriate, establish reporting, monitoring, evaluation requirements and transparency requirements in EU legislation, in particular on Member States.
They also commit to ensuring that the Commission adopts a proposal for a delegated or implementing act within the limits of the Treaties.
Public consultations
The Commission will, before adopting a proposal, conduct public consultations in an open and transparent way, ensuring the modalities and time-limits of those public consultations allow for the widest possible participation. Where the Commission decides not to submit a proposal in response to such a request, it will inform the institution concerned of the detailed reasons, and will provide, where appropriate, an analysis of possible alternatives and respond to any issues raised by the co-legislators in relation to analyses concerning 'European value' and concerning the "cost ofnon-Europe". The Commission will also explain in its explanatory memoranda how the measures proposed are justified in the light of the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality and how they are compatible with fundamental rights.
Ex-post evaluation
The European Parliament will, when they consider this to be appropriate and necessary for the legislative process, carry out impact assessments.
Each institution will inform each other in good time before adopting or revising their guidelines concerning their tools for Better Law-Making (public consultations, impact assessments and ex-post assessments).
In addition, the Commission will:
inform the other institutions of its plans and priorities for the new term of the Commission;
share views on the principal policy objectives and priorities of the three Institutions;
participate in debates on the implementation of the Joint declaration in the Parliament and/or the Council during the spring of the year in question.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
   
The agreement entered into force on 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world's largest economy and has the largest number of citizens. It is also the largest single market in the world, with a population of over 1.3 billion.
For more information, see:
Better Law-making (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
  Еганскантранка грака  глаксанда барка, годанца кактанна   бълфана варта нашка and гакшанина (Common Agreement between the Council of European Union, the Parliament, and the Commission on the Improvement of the Quality of EU Legislation)
Council Joint Political Declaration 2011/852/EC of 27 November 2011 on improving the quality, efficiency and effectiveness of the Member States’ legislation (OJ L 304, 1.12.2011, pp. 1-2)

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative registration and verification of statements of support
Citizen’s Initiative Registration and verification
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the registration of proposed citizens’ initiatives
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the registration, verification and certification of statements* of support from signatories* of proposed European Union (EU) citizens' initiatives.
It aims to ensure that citizens from different EU countries are subject to similar conditions for supporting such initiatives, regardless of the EU country from which they come.
KEY POINTS
Organisations must register their initiative with the European Commission, providing the information set out in Annex II, in particular on the subject matter and objectives of the proposed citizens' initiative.
Organisers must also ensure that the online collection system used for the collection of the statement of support complies with the requirements of the regulation.
The organisers must provide, for the register and where appropriate on their website, regularly updated information on the sources of support and funding.
EU countries must verify the conformity of online collection systems with the regulation before statements are collected.
When the Commission receives a proposal for a legal act of the Union for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to those conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union and on the Council under Article 241 TFEU, the organisers are entitled to present that initiative at a public hearing at EU level. If the initiative is supported by the requisite number of signatories which fulfils the other requirements of this regulation, it is entitled to be presented to the Commission.
If the Commission considers that the initiative does not meet the other conditions, it may withdraw the initiative. The organisers may, however, withdraw it at any time before the submission of the necessary statements.
In addition, the Commission may, upon request, provide citizens with information and informal advice about citizens‘ initiatives, notably as regards the registration criteria.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
Citizens have the right to:
make a proposal to implement the EU Treaties;
request the Commission to adopt a law for the implementation of these Treaties.
For more information, see:
European Citizens’ Initiative Registration (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Citizens' initiative: any initiative which aims to promote a European Union interest.
* Legal act: any act adopted by an EU country to implement one of the 2 Treaties, i.e. the Treaty of Lisbon or the Treaty establishing the European Economic Area (EEA).

Statistical data on the application of pesticides
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 on the statistical collection, transmission and processing of agricultural holding data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out detailed, harmonised and up-to-date statistics on sales and use of pesticide pesticides at EU level.
It aims to ensure that EU countries provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with accurate, comparable and reliable statistics on pesticides placed on the market in the EU.

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — Regulation (EEC) No 596/2004
Plant Protection products — Regulatory Regulation (EU) No 550/2005
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 529/2004 on plant protection products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules for the authorisation of plant protection* products.
It aims to ensure a high level of protection of both human and animal health and the environment.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to substances, including micro-organisms having general or specific action against harmful organisms on plants, which are classified as ‘plant protection products’. It does not apply to substances which are not used for plant protection, but which may be of value for such purposes.
EU countries must apply the precautionary principle when granting authorisations to plants. This principle states that substances or products produced or placed on the market do not have any harmful effect on human or animal health or any unacceptable effects on the environment, and that they do not pose a risk to the health or safety of plants or animals.
Authorisations are granted by EU countries on the basis of harmonised criteria. They are valid for a period of 5 years and may be extended after 5 years if necessary.
In addition to active substances, the regulation sets out rules on:
the authorisation procedure for minor uses;
the control and inspection arrangements with regard to the marketing and use of plants protection products;
compliance with feed and food law, animal health, animal welfare and animal welfare rules, including record-keeping;
control measures for the use of the products at all stages of the production of food;
authorisation procedures for the manufacture, importation and export of plants.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation. It is assisted by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Medicines Agency (EMSA).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of the EU’s Plant Protection Directive 91/414/EEC (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘The Plant Protection of Plants Directive’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Plant protection product: any substance or product intended to protect plants, animals or the environment from harmful organisms.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 90:
Administrative cooperation in the field of administrative cooperation: the use of the Interinstitutional Management Information System (IMI)
Administrative coordination in the fields of administrative coordination and information exchange
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on administrative cooperation for administrative cooperation between Member States and between EU countries and the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the implementation of EU administrative cooperation (i.e. administrative cooperation) through the Interintegrative Management Information Systems (MI) system.
It aims to improve the functioning of the internal market by providing an effective, user-friendly tool for the application of EU acts.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of national IMI coordinators. These coordinators are responsible for:
identifying their counterpart in another EU country;
organising the exchange of information, including personal data, on the basis of simple and unified procedures and to overcome language barriers;
providing pre-defined and pre-translated workflows.
Each national coordinator must appoint one coordinator to carry out the following tasks:
registering or registering IMI users;
assisting the IMI coordinator with the preparation of workflows, question and answer sets, forms and other arrangements relating to administrative cooperation procedures in IMI;
ensuring that the coordinators’ work is carried out in accordance with the rules and procedures set out in the regulation;
coordinating the work of the national coordinators;
designing and implementing IMI workflows;
preparing workflows and working arrangements for the coordination of IMI activities.
National coordinators may be assigned to SOLVIT centres within the remit of their work, so that they can function independently from the national coordinator.
The European Commission may adopt measures to extend the scope of the regulation’s rules to cover administrative cooperation with non-EU countries and with the European Economic Area (EEA).
The Commission has access to the personal data that are strictly necessary for carrying out its tasks within the responsibilities laid down in this regulation.
In addition, the Commission may, in certain cases and at the request of the EU country concerned, consider technical solutions for the direct transfer of data from national systems to IMI, where such national systems have already been developed, notably for notification procedures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The IMI system is one of the most important tools in the European Union (EU). It is the main tool used by EU countries to coordinate administrative cooperation and to exchange information.
For more information, see:
IMI (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Administrative cooperation: cooperation between the administrative authorities of EU countries, between the Commission and the EU countries.
* Interinstitution: a system for the exchange and management of information between the competent authorities of an EU country and the Commission.

Summary 91:
Conservation, management, exploitation, monitoring, marketing and enforcement measures for fishery and aquaculture products established by the General Fisheries Commission for the Mediterranean (GFCM)
Convention on the application of the EU’s fisheries policy to the Mediterranean and the Black Sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 laying down the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by EU countries of the European Commission's exercise of implementing powers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the rules for the application by the EU of the conservation, management and exploitation of the common fisheries policy (CFP) to the general fisheries commission for the European Mediterranean and Black Sea.
The GFCM Agreement provides an appropriate framework for multilateral cooperation to promote the development, conservation, rational management and best utilisation of living marine resources in the Mediterranean.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the Mediterranean, that is, the Mediterranean Sea and its adjoining waters, as defined in the preamble to the agreement;
the Black Sea and connecting waters, defined in Article 4 of the agreement.
It does not apply to certain fisheries in the Gulf of Lions.
EU countries must inform the Commission, the Member States in whose waters the research is carried out and the Scientific, Technical and Economic Committee for Fisheries of all catches from such fishing operations.
Member States must submit to the Commission in electronic format a report on the fishing activities carried out in the area covered by the agreement every year. This report must include the number of vessels involved and the total number of days fished in the closed season for dolphinfish fisheries and the report on such carrying over.
Before 1 February of each year, Member States must send the Commission an electronic report on fishing activities in their waters.
Each year, the Commission must send a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee for fisheries of the number and number of fishing vessels involved in fishing operations in its waters. The report must also include:
total number of fished days for the year;
number of vessels in the EU fleet register;
EU fleet register number;
fishing authorisations issued by Member States to vessels authorised to fish in the areas covered by this regulation;
information in respect of the use of minimum mesh size for nets used for trawling activities exploiting demersal stocks in Black Sea;
data on statistical matrices;
cooperation and exchange of information with the Executive Secretary of the Commission.
Authorisation of vessels
Vessels are issued with a fishing authorisation by their Member State in accordance with Article 7 of Council Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 of 20 November 2009 establishing a Community control system for ensuring compliance with the rules of the fisheries policy.
Report
Member countries must submit the Commission a report every year on the number, number and type of vessels used in their fishing activities.
Fisheries restricted areas
The Commission must submit a report each year to the Council, the European Environment Agency and the Committee of the Regions on the results of scientific investigations carried out there. The Commission must also send the Council a report.
Dolphinfishes
The European Commission must provide the Commission with:
a list of vessels operating in the restricted areas;
a report on total number fished and lost days;
an annual list of the vessels involved.

Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection: European patent with unitary effect (Regulation 2011/167/EU)
Unitary Patent protection: EU unitary patent protection (Council Regulation (EU) 2021/2021)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1169/2011 on the creation of unitary patents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted in the European Patent Office (EPO) have the same set of claims in all EU countries.
It sets out the rules for granting and renewing patents.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to patents granted by the EPO to:
patents that are registered in the Register for unitary Patent Protection in EU countries;
patent applications that have been submitted by the European patent applicant in the EU countries and which have been registered in that register;
pending patent applications which have not yet been granted.
EU countries must ensure that the patent is deemed not to have taken effect on their territory as a national patent, so as to avoid any duplication of patent protection.
The EPO publishes the list of EU countries that have granted a patent with a unitary protection in its European Patent Bulletin.
A patent granted with the same sets of claims is granted in all the participating EU countries, provided that it has been registered and that its unitary effects have been recognised in the register.
Patent proprietors must pay a single annual renewal fee for a European patent. The renewal fee is progressive throughout the patent protection and, together with the fees to be paid by the patent applicant during the pre-grant stage. The EPO covers all the costs associated with the grant of the patent and the administration of the protection. The fee may be reduced if the patent has been granted in a country with a disproportionately low level of patenting activity and/or having acquired membership of the European Intellectual Property Organisation relatively recently.
Compulsory licences for European patents with unitARY effect are governed by the national laws of the participating countries as regards their respective territories.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
For more information, see:
European patent protection — unitary* (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent granted in one EU country and granted in another.
* Patent applicant: any person or organisation with a patent on a product or service which is patented in the territory of a participating EU country.

Summary 93:
European Observatory on Infringements of Intellectual Property Rights (EOSI)
European Observatory of Infringement of Intellectual Product Rights (ESI) (European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 207/2009 establishing a European Observatory on Counterfeiting and Piracy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal basis for the involvement of the Office for Harmonization in the Internal Market (Trade Marks and Designs) (OHSI) in enforcement-related activities, including the fight against counterfeiting, in particular through fostering its cooperation with the national trade mark offices and the Observatory.
KEY POINTS
The OHSI’s tasks are to:
collect, analyse and disseminate relevant objective, comparable and reliable data regarding the value of intellectual property rights and infringements of those rights;
identify and promote best practices and strategies to enforce these rights, as well as raising public awareness of the impact of infringements;
organise, administer and support the gathering of experts, authorities and stakeholders assembled under the name ‘European Observatory’;
provide technical assistance to non-EU countries involved in intellectual property protection, including developing and delivering specific training programmes and events for officials from third countries;
make recommendations to the European Commission on issues falling within the scope of this regulation, on the basis of a request from the Commission;
carry out similar activities necessary in order to enable the Office to fulfil the tasks set out in paragraph 1.
In order to carry out its tasks, the Office invites to meetings representatives from public administrations, bodies and organisations in the EU countries dealing with intellectual property, and representatives from the private sector, for the purpose of their participation in the Office’�s work under this regulation.
The Office also organises meetings of experts from the public and the private sectors.

Summary 94:
Safeguarding the EU’s economic interests under international trade agreements
Securing the EU's economic interests in the field of international trade: implementing acts
Safer measures
The European Union (EU) has adopted implementing acts to protect its economic interests when third countries impose trade restrictive measures that affect its interests.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 552/2011 of 22 December 2011 on the effective exercise of the Union's rights under International Trade Agreements
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a legal instrument to protect the European Union's (EU's) economic interests against any breach by a non-EU country of its international trade rules. This is particularly the case in situations where third countries enact restrictive measures* that diminish the benefits accruing to the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following situations:
the application of a safeguard measure* by a third country* which affects the EU;
the imposition of a mandatory price penalty* on tenders of suppliers of goods or services originating in and operating from the third country concerned and/or on that part of the tender consisting of goods originating in that country.
It does not apply to:
public procurement* of goods and services;
contractual agreements between contracting authorities or entities conducting public procurement* between EU countries;
public contracts for the supply of services and goods originating from the EU or the EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that the origin of the natural or legal persons providing the services or goods is determined by the competent contracting authorities and entities in accordance with EU law.
Each EU country must submit a list of appropriate contracting authorities, entities or categories of contracting authorities to the European Commission.
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the implementing acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The implementing acts apply from 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts, including:
Regulation No 553/2011 on the protection of the EU economic interests (see summary);
Council Implementing Act No 558/2012 on the enforcement of the rights and obligations of EU countries under the WTO Agreement on Safeguards;
Commission Implementing Regulation No 559/2012 laying down the procedure for the application of the safeguard measures.
For more information, see:
EU trade protection measures (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2011/552/EU of 22 November 2011 on implementing acts protecting the Union economic interests pursuant to the provisions of the International Trade Agreement (ITA) on safeguards (OJ L 347, 23.12.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 529/2011 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Council Delegated Regulation (EC) 2015/852 of 23 December 2015 laying down implementing acts for the enforcement by the EU of its rights under the Trade Agreement with the European Economic Area (TEA) and its annexes to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Atomic Energy Community (TFEC) and the Protocol on Trade and Investment between the EU and the European Free Trade Association (TFTA).
Commission implementing act No 557/2012 of 23 November 2012 laying down a procedure for applying the EU safeguard measures pursuant to Article 8(2) of the TFEU to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) and implementing acts under Article XXVIII of the GATT.

Summary 95:
Agreement between the European Union and Central America on the stabilisation of the market for bananas (Agreement on Banana Stabilisation Mechanism)
Agreement with the Central American countries on the establishment of a stabilisation mechanism for bananas
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — Implementing Regulation on the implementation of the Agreement on the Establishment of a Stabilising Mechanism for the Market for Bananas between the EU and the Member States of Central America
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE REGULATION?
The agreement establishes a mechanism to prevent serious harm to the EU banana growing sector, which is of great importance to the agricultural producers of many of the outermost regions of the EU.
The regulation repeals Regulation (EEC) No 852/2004.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EC) NO 853/2003.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The annexes to the agreement are as follows:
the agreement covers:
bananas imports from Central America of bananas and those sectors to which monitoring has been extended;
monitoring of imports of bananas from the countries concerned;
the monitoring of the social and environmental standards of the countries of the agreement;
an annual monitoring report to the European Parliament and to the Council on updated statistics on imports from these countries of bananas;
measures to be adopted by the European Commission to monitor the observance of the standards laid down in the agreement.
Implementing acts
the Commission adopts implementing acts for the temporary suspension of the preferential customs duty for bananas and the temporary extension of the customs duty.
In the event of a surge in imports, the Commission must adopt immediately applicable implementing acts.
An investigation must precede the application of any safeguard measure, subject to the Commission being allowed to apply provisional safeguard measures in critical circumstances. The Commission must consult interested parties, in particular where they have made a written application within 1 month of receipt of a request or information, showing that they are actually likely to be affected by the outcome of the investigation and that there are special reasons for them to be heard orally.
Prior surveillance measures
The Commission may adopt prior surveillance measures in accordance with the advisory procedure referred to in Article 14(2) and 14(3) of the annexes. These measures are designed to:
ensure that the Commission has all the information it considers necessary to make a determination with regard to the conditions set out in Article 2(1), and, where appropriate, endeavour to verify that information;
allow the Commission to adopt definitive safeguard measures if it considers that there is a threat of serious injury to the Union’s banana sector;
provide the Commission with all the necessary information to determine whether the importation of bananas is likely to cause serious damage to the sector.
If the Commission considers that the import of bananas would cause damage which would be difficult to repair, it must adopt a provisional safeguard measure. This safeguard measure must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. It must be in line with the principles of the Economic and Financial Instruments Directive (Euratom) and with the principle of the precautionary principle.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISION AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Decision No 752/2013/EU has applied since 11 December 2012.
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/856 has applied from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Agreement on Agricultural Cooperation with Central America was signed on 29 June 2012, received the consent of the European Council on 11 December 2013 and is to apply as provided for in Article 353 of the Agreements.
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Successive amendments and corrections to Decision No 770/2011 have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 96:
Bank accounts and debtors’ assets: preservation of bank accounts and accounts held in another EU country
Bank Accounts and debts in another European Union (EU) country
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2009 on the preservation of accounts and debts held in other EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a European Union procedure for the preservation and attachment of the bank accounts of debtors.
It aims to prevent the disappearance of assets before the enforcement of a judgment.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
bank accounts* held in a non-EU country;
accounts held in an EU country other than that country’s jurisdiction;
debts that have already fallen due.
Creditors’ liability
The creditor is responsible for the damage caused to the debtor by the order. The creditor is also liable for the liability of the debtor where the damage is due to fault on their part.
In addition, the creditor is not responsible for:
the non-payment or contesting of the claim or the mere fact that the debtor has more than one creditor;
the use of a credit card, from using the funds.
The court dealing with the application for the order is the court of the EU country where the bank account is located.
Where the debtor is a consumer domiciled in a EU country, jurisdiction to issue the order lies only with the courts of that EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMESPECTS APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEVEL
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2010.
EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a series of measures designed to improve judicial cooperation in civil matters having cross-border implications, particularly when necessary for the proper functioning of the internal market.
For more information, see:
‘Bank accounts’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Eec) No 550/2009 of 24 October 2009 on the Preservation of Bank Accounts and Debtors' Assets in the European Union and repealing Council Regulation No 595/2009 (OJ L 127, 1.1.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 550/09 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 97:
Border surveillance at sea: operational cooperation between EU countries and non-EU countries and the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the European Union (EMO-EU)
Border surveillance operations at sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on border surveillance at the external borders of the Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a framework for operational cooperation at the EU’s external sea borders with non-European Union (EU)* countries.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of border surveillance is to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those who have crossed the border in an irregular manner. To this end, border surveillance includes:
intercepting vessels suspected of trying to gain entry to the EU without submitting to border checks;
operational cooperation between Member States’ border surveillance units;
coordination of search and rescue operations;
assistance to victims of trafficking in human beings.
Cooperation between the EU countries’ competent authorities and the competent authorities of third countries
The regulation sets out the rules and procedures for the coordination of operational cooperation in the field of maritime border surveillance.
The Agency is responsible for:
coordinating border surveillance operations in the area covered by Regulation (EC) No 2007/2004 (see summary);
organising and coordinating maritime surveillance operations with the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
operating in the maritime area of the Schengen acquis;
cooperating with the national authorities of the participating Member States;
providing risk analysis and training;
planning and coordinating sea operations.
In addition, the Agency may cooperate with the competent national authorities in the areas covered by the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurojust).
Coordination of operations
EU countries must ensure that:
the operational plan for a joint operation, pilot project or rapid intervention takes place, including references to EU and international law regarding interception, rescue at sea and disembarkation;
the plan must include specific information on the application of the relevant jurisdiction and legislation in the geographical area where the joint operation takes place;
a description of the operational area of a sea operation.
When coordinating maritime operations, the operational plan must also include references to:
EU law, including the protection of personal data, the right to asylum and to protection against removal and expulsion, the principles of non-refoulement, non-discrimination, the rights of the child and the rights to an effective remedy;
international law, in particular that of the United Nations Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination (UN Convention against the Trafficking in Persons and the Protocol on the Protection of the Rights of the Child);
the rights of persons who have been rescued and who have not been returned to their country of origin;
measures to ensure that persons rescued can be delivered to a place of safety;
rules on the repatriation of persons rescued and those who are being returned to the country of their origin.
Rights of migrants
Member States and the Agency must respect the fundamental rights of migrants, including:
their right to information and to be informed of their rights;
their obligation to ensure the safety of the persons rescued;
protection of their personal data; and
the right to protection of their children.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
 			
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve cooperation between the European Commission and Member States at their external maritime borders in the context of the implementation, application and development of the common border management policy.
For more information, see:
External border surveillance (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2014/552/EU of 21 May 2014 on the implementation of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 752/2013 on the coordination at the European External Border of the Union of the measures and procedures relating to border surveillance in the framework of the Common Border Management Policy (COM(2014) 752 final)
Successive amendments to Decision (EU), Euratom and Euratom have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 99:
The Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund (AFMIF)
The asylum, Migration & Integration Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/852 establishing the Asylum, Migratory and Integration fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s first asylum, migration and integration fund.
It aims to contribute to the development of the common EU policy on asylum and immigration and to the strengthening of the area of freedom, security and justice in the light of the application of the principles of solidarity and responsibility-sharing between the EU countries and cooperation with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The fund supports the efforts by the EU and the Member States relating to the enhancement of their asylum policies in light of their obligations under existing EU law. It also supports measures to combat illegal immigration or the circumventing of existing legal migration rules, thereby safeguarding the integrity of EU countries’ immigration systems.
The main objectives of the fund are to:
increase the capacity of Member States to develop, implement, monitor and evaluate their immigration and integration strategies, policies and measures for third-country nationals, including EU legal instruments.
To enhance the solidarity and better share the responsibility between Member States, in particular towards those most affected by asylum flows, the fund also supports the implementation of a Union Resettlement Programme and the transfer of beneficiaries of international protection from one Member State to another.
In addition, the Fund provides financial support for new approaches concerning access to asylum procedures in a safer manner, by targeting main countries of transit such as protection programmes for particular groups or certain procedures for examination of applications for asylum.
Asylum and migration policy
The Fund also contributes to the effective functioning of the Common European Asylum System, which encompasses measures relating to policy, legislation, and capacity-building, while acting in cooperation with other EU countries, EU agencies and third countries. It is also responsible for:
facilitating the return of persons who have been illegally crossing the external borders of the EU;
providing financial support to Member States in the field of border management;
contributing to the establishment of the European Asylum and Migration Agency (EASA);
supporting the creation of a European Asylum Network (EAN).
The EAN is responsible for the coordination of the EASA and the management of the return process. The EAN has the responsibility of:
monitoring and evaluating the asylum and migration situation;
coordinating with other Member States;
ensuring that asylum procedures are implemented in a safe and effective manner;
collecting and analysing data on the number of people who have applied for international protection;
assisting the EAN in its work.
Funding
Part of the available resources under the Fund are to be used for the implementing of specific actions which require cooperative effort amongst Member States and generate significant added value for the Union, as well as for the implementation, under the EU Resettlement programme, of an EU resettlement programme. The Fund also provides financial incentives for Member States’ implementation of this programme.
Member States must ensure that the financial incentives are not disproportionate to the actual costs of implementing the programme. In addition, Member States must take into account the specific needs of the beneficiaries of protection.
Transfer of beneficiaries
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing measures, including:
a mechanism based on the nationality of the recipients of protection, which allows for the transfer to another Member State of the same number of persons;
a system for the allocation of financial incentives to the countries involved in the resettlement programme;
the establishment of a system for assessing the eligibility of beneficiaries for resettlement.
A number of other implementing measures are also included in the Fund. These include:
assistance for the most vulnerable persons in the European Union;
emergency assistance for those most in need of humanitarian assistance;
measures for the protection of children and women;
reintegration measures for vulnerable persons.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘The Asylum Fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Delegated Resolution of 1 December 2015 on the establishment, on the basis of Article 2 of the Treaty on European Union, of the Asylum Fund (OJ L 127, 1.1.2015, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2015 /852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

The Council therefore adopted Regulation (EC) No 852



Summary 100:
Fund for EU financial support in the field of internal security
Fund for European security
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 515/2014 establishing the European Union Fund for Internal Security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union fund to support the work carried out by Member States’ competent authorities in order to combat transnational threats to the Union’s internal security.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument is designed to provide financial support for actions of particular interest to the EU (‘EU actions’), emergency assistance and technical assistance, and for shared management in respect of national programmes and actions requiring administrative flexibility.
It covers:
measures in and in relation to third countries supported through the instrument, both geographic and thematic, with a view to engaging in drug trafficking activities;
measures to combat organised crime, terrorism, organised crime and the financing of terrorism and organised crime financing;
cooperation between judicial authorities and law enforcement authorities.
EU actions
The fund is funded by direct and indirect management. It is managed by the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the EU.
The Commission monitors the implementation of the Instrument, with the aid of key indicators for evaluating results and impacts.
Funding
The EU budget allocated to the Instrument is divided into 3 categories:
1.
Emergency assistance: the EU budget is divided between the EU countries’ national programmes;
2.
3.
emergency assistance: it is split between the European Police and Border and Coast Guard.
4.
5.
Shared management: the fund is divided up between the Commission, the European Asylum and Migration Agency and the Directorate-General for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Instrument entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The Internal Security Strategy for the EU adopted by the Council on 8-9 November 2010 calls for more cooperation between Member States in the law enforcement field, as tackling organised crime effectively is fundamental to protecting the lawful economy from typical criminal activities such as the laundering of the proceeds of crime.
For more information, see:
‘Internal Security Strategy’ (European Commission).

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing of stocks of common interest (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — measures against countries allowing non-sustainability of fish stocks of interest (see summary)
Non-Sustainable Fishing of stock of interest
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1005/2008 — measures to combat non-compliance with the rules of the EU fisheries management organisation (RFMO)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the type of measures that may be taken with regard to countries that allow or participate in fishing activities that jeopardise the sustainability of a stock of common interests.
It aims to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fisheries involving a fishery involving a common interest to the EU and to non-EU countries;
the management of the stock in question in the context of an RFMO or a similar body;
fishing on the territory of a third country, including territories enjoying self-governing status and endowed with competencies in the area of conservation and management of living marine resources.
In addition, it establishes general conditions for the adoption of specific measures to encourage countries to contribute to the conservation of that stock. These measures are based on objective criteria and are equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO) and the Convention establishing the International Maritime Organization for the Conservation of Living Resources (Cotonou).
The European Commission is assisted by a committee.
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCELEVELS
   
It entered into force on 25 September 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of fisheries management measures in the framework of regional fisheries management organisations.
For more information, see:
Fisheries management (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Common interest: a stock within which the stock can replenish itself with high probability while allowing high yield fisheries on it;
country: a non-member of the European Economic Area (EEA).
* Non-EU country: a country that does not participate in the EU’s fisheries management system.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation 2013/13/EU of 25 September 2013 laying down measures for the prevention, determent and elimination of non- sustainable fishing in the field of fisheries of the common interest.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Commission Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2015/852 of 25 May 2015 laying down specific measures for combating illegal fishing and for preventing and deterring the illegal trade in fish products of fish species caught by fishing vessels operating in the waters of the Common Fisheries Area.
Regulation 2013/15/EU laying down general rules for the coordination of measures to be adopted by the Commission for the protection of the marine environment and the sustainable management of marine ecosystems.
Delegated regulation (EU), Euratom and Euratom of 25 April 2015 laying out specific measures that the Commission may adopt in relation to countries which allow, without due regard to existing fishing patterns or the rights, duties and interests of other countries and the EU, fisheries activities that threaten the sustainable development of the fishery or which fail to cooperate with the EU in its management, and which are not cooperating with other countries or the Union in its conservation.
Directive 2013/11/EU lays down the general rules on the coordination between the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the Economic and Social Committee for the Protection of the Marine Environment and the Committee of the Regions for the Cooperation and Coordination of the Fisheries of the Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 25 September 2011. It had to become law in the Member States by 25 September 2014.

Summary 102:
Investment agreements with non-EU countries
Investments with third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and general principles concerning the exercise by EU countries of the European Commission’s exercise of its investment powers.
It aims to facilitate the negotiation and conclusion of investment agreements between the EU and non-European Union countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation covers only those provisions of bilateral agreements dealing with investment protection. It does not apply to:
bilateral investment agreements concerning the movement of capital between EU countries, including in respect of capital movements involving investments in defence;
investment agreements concerning investment protection;
contractual agreements concerning trade in services.
EU countries must take any appropriate measures to ensure that the provisions of these agreements do not constitute a serious obstacle to the negotiation or conclusion by the EU of bilateral investments agreements with third country.
Authorisation to amend or conclude a bilateral investment agreement
Subject to the conditions laid down in Articles 8 to 11, a Member State may be authorised to enter into negotiations with a third country to amend an existing or to conclude a new investment agreement.
The Member State concerned must notify the Commission of the conclusion and entry into force of the agreement, and of any subsequent changes to the status of that agreement. The Commission must take its decision within 90 days of receipt of the notification.
If the Commission finds that the negotiations have resulted in a bilateral agreement which fulfils the requirements of the regulation, it may authorise the Member State to sign and conclude such an agreement. However, if the agreement does not comply with the requirements, it must inform the other Member States of the reasons for this decision.
Member States must also inform the Commission within 30 days of receiving the notification of any amendments to the agreement. If the information transmitted by the Member States is not sufficient for the purposes of authorising the opening of formal negotiations in accordance with Article 9, the Commission may request additional information.
In addition, Member States must:
ensure that the agreement complies with EU law;
make any necessary amendments to ensure consistency with the EU's investment policy or compatibility with the relevant EU law.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Investment and trade policy (European Commission).

Summary 103:
Tariff preferences for sustainable development and good governance: general arrangement (GSP)
Tariffs preferences for Sustainable development and Good governance: General arrangement (SDP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 2913/92 establishing a general arrangement for the promotion of sustainable development in the field of development cooperation with developing countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a general tariff preferences scheme for developing countries.
It aims to promote the eradication of poverty and to promote sustainable development.
The general arrangement applies to all those developing countries which share a common developing need and are in a similar stage of economic development. It does not apply to countries which are benefiting from a preferential market access arrangement with the EU, which provides at least the same level of tariff preferences as the scheme for substantially all trade.
KEY POINTS
Eligible countries
A list of eligible countries is established in the regulation’s annexes.
Beneficiaries are those countries which:
have a bilateral trade agreement with the European Union (EU);
have an agreement with a non-EU country;
have signed an association agreement with an EU country.
A beneficiary country is eligible for tariff preferences if:
it meets the eligibility criteria for the general arrangement;
it fulfils the conditions laid down in the annexes to the regulation, i.e. it meets the criteria set out in Article 290 TFEU of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU); and
it is in a position to benefit from the additional tariff preferences granted under the special incentive arrangement.
Graduation of a beneficiary country
The graduated tariff preferences are granted when a section of a tariff preference is graduated over 3 consecutive years, in order to increase predictability and fairness of the scheme by eliminating the effect of large and exceptional variations in the import statistics. The beneficiary country may be excluded from the scheme if it does not fulfil the criteria for graduation.
Suspension from the tariff preferences of certain beneficiary countries
The regulation establishes an advisory procedure for the implementation of implementing acts on suspension from certain GSP sections in respect of beneficiary countries and on the initiation of a temporary withdrawal procedure, taking into account the nature and impact of those acts. The regulation also sets out a procedure for repealing a decision on temporary withdrawal under the urgency procedure before that decision to temporarily withdraw tariff preferences takes effect, where the reasons justifying temporary withdrawal no longer apply.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 21 November 2012 for countries which by 20 November 2012 had initiated bilateral trade agreements with the Union.
BACKGROUND
The General arrangement is one of a number of EU policies designed to help developing countries to diversify their economy and to reduce their dependence on the EU.
For more information, see:
‘The General Economic Partnership’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* General arrangement: a system of preferential trade preferences granted to developing countries under the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) in 1979, under which WTO Members may accord differential and more favourable treatment than developing countries (see summary).
* Special incentive arrangement: an arrangement designed to encourage the sustainable development of developing countries and promote good governance.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 192913/1992 of 21 October 1992 establishing a General arrangement for sustainable devolution and promoting good governance in the Field of Development Cooperation with the countries of the Western Balkans (OJ L 127, 23.10.1992, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Eec) No 29913/91 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
