Summary 104:
Export authorisation and import and transit measures for firearms, their parts and essential components and ammunition
Exchange of information between EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 428/2009 on export authorisation, and import/transit measures relating to firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the export of firearms and their parts to and from the European Union (EU) for the purpose of implementing Article 10 of the United Nations Protocol against the Illicit Manufacturing of and Trafficking in Firearms, their Parts and Components and Ammunition, supplementing the UN Convention against Transnational Organised Crime (the ‘UN Firearms Protocol’).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
exports of firearms to or from the EU to or through non-EU countries;
imports of these items to and through EU countries.
Exports are subject to authorisation in the EU country in which they are being exported, except for:
firearms manufactured after 1899;
deactivated firearms;
antique firearms, and their replicas as defined in accordance with national legislation, provided that antique firearms do not include firearms manufactured before 1899.
EU countries must apply for authorisation from the competent national authority.
The authorisation must be valid for at least 3 years and may be renewed for a further period of 5 years. The authorisation is valid for a maximum period of 10 years. It must be renewed at least once every 3 years.
When an EU country considers that an application for an authorisation has not been fulfilled, it must inform the European Commission and the European Parliament. The Commission may adopt delegated acts to carry out the obligations imposed on it by this regulation and by that Common Position.
Imports of the items covered by the regulation must be accompanied by:
a certificate of authorisation issued by the competent EU country;
a declaration of the country of origin of the item;
an export contract between the exporter and the importer.
If no export contract has been concluded or if the holder of the contract does not act on its own behalf, the person who has the power for sending the item out of the customs territory of the EU is responsible for determining the sending of it.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that:
the consignments are accompanied by the necessary documents;
the documents contain the necessary information for the authorisation to be granted; and
the authorisation holder has the right to raise any objections it or they may have to the granting of such authorisation.
Export authorisations are valid for up to 5 years and can be renewed up to a maximum of 3 times. The exporter must notify the competent authority of the relevant EU country of the reasons for granting or revoking such authorisations. The competent authority must also inform the Commission of any objections that it may have regarding the granting or revocation of authorisations, and of the measures it may take to deal with such objections.
Where an export is subject to an import or transit measure, the competent authorities must ensure that the relevant documents are:
accompanied by the required information;
transported within the EU by a consignment of goods;
exported to, or through, a third country by means of an authorised consignment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 3 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
The UN Firearms Protocol, which aims to promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among EU countries in order to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms.
For more information, see:
‘The United Nations Firearms Protocol: Implementing Articles 10 and 11’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom, 6.5.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Council Regulation (EEC, EEC, OJ L 127, 7.6.2009 and pp. 9-11) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 105:
EUROSUR: situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the EU countries (external borders)
EurosUR: surveillance and response capability at external borders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council on operational cooperation coordinated by the European Union Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) external border surveillance system.
It aims to improve the operational and technical ability of EU countries’ border control authorities to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration and cross-border crime and to save the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the surveillance of external borders and to border crossing points;
the interception of illegal immigration activities and unauthorised crossings by persons of the internal borders.
National coordination centres
Each EU country must designate a national coordination centre responsible for coordinating the exchange of information and for cooperation with the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurojust).
The national coordination centres must provide Eurojust with information from their national situational pictures which is required for the establishment and maintenance of the common European situational picture and the common pre-frontier intelligence picture.
Eurojust must provide the Agency, via the communication network, with information that is necessary for the development of the picture and for the maintenance and updating of the image.
The Agency must store non-classified and classified information in a non-EU country’s national situational picture collection centres.
EU countries must store and analyse the non-sensitive information in their national picture collection networks.
In addition, the Agency must:
ensure that the information it receives is not classified;
cooperate with other EU countries in the field of border control;
coordinate operational cooperation with other countries;
provide the Agency with information on:
illegal immigration;
illegal border crossings;
cross-border criminal activities;
unauthorised crossing of the borders;
border crossing points.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve its border control capability. These include:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 652/2006 on the surveillance of sea external borders in the context of operational cooperation (see summary);
Implementation of Regulation (EC, EFTA) No 461/2006 establishing the European border control system (see overview);
Regulating (EU), Euratom and Eurojust on border control.
For more information, see:
External borders (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External borders: the area of the Schengen acquis in which the United Kingdom does not take part, in accordance with Council Decision 2000/365/EC.
* Seaworthy vessel: a small vessel, such as a fishing boat or a rubber dinghy, used by persons in need of international protection.

Summary 106:
Eurodac: access to the European Union’s central database for fingerprint data (2013-2020)
EurodAC: access and sharing of fingerprint data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 604/2013 — access to Eurodac for terrorist offences and other serious criminal offences
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the access of the designated authorities of the EU countries’ national authorities to the central database, EurodAC, for the purpose of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious crimes.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following categories of persons:
third-country nationals or stateless persons who have stayed in the EU for several years and have obtained a settled status or even citizenship of an EU country after that period;
non-EU nationals who have been resident in an EU Member State for many years and who have applied for international protection.
It does not apply to non-EU citizens who have not applied for refugee status.
Requests for comparison with data stored in the Central System should be made by the operating units within the designated authority to the national access point, through the national verifying authority.
The designated authority and the verifying authority may not be part of the same organisation, if the requesting authority is part of another organisation.
EU countries must ensure that the competent national authority has the necessary legal basis to verify the validity of the request.
In addition, the competent authority of the country in which the request is made must be able to verify that the requested data is authentic and that it has been entered into the database in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 259/68/68 of the European Parliament and of the Council on matters pertaining to the establishment of the Central Information Infrastructure (the ‘Communication Infrastructure’).
The Commission outlines in its Communication on improved effectiveness, enhanced interoperability and synergies among European databases in the area of Justice and Home Affairs that authorities responsible for internal security could have access to this database in well defined cases, when there is a substantiated suspicion that the perpetrator of a terrorist or serious criminal offence has applied for asylum or has been convicted of a criminal offence in another Member State.
Context
The purpose of the regulation is to improve the access to existing data filing systems in the field of justice and home affairs.
For more information, see:
European Asylum and Migration Agency (European External Action Service).
KEY TERMS
* Latent fingerprint: a fingerprint which is the dactyloscopic trace which may be found at a crime scene.
* Stateless person: a person who has not been granted refugee status in the Member State in which he or she is resident for more than 2 years.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC, Euratom, ECSC) No 608/2013 of 23 November 2013 laying down rules for access to European Union's Central System for the processing of requests for comparison of latent fingerprint data with the database stored in Eurodatac (Recast) (OJ L 183, 24.11.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 107:
Protection of designations of origin and traditional specialities guaranteed
Protecting designs of origin
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 882/2004 on the protection of certain designations and geographical indications of agricultural products and foodstuffs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s agricultural product quality policy (APQP) is implemented.
It sets out the rules for protecting designations* of origin* and ‘traditional specialities* guaranteed*’.
KEY POINTS
Protection
Protected designations or geographical indications (DOSs)
The regulation applies to products for which there is an intrinsic link between a product or foodstuff characteristics and geographical origin.
The protection of DOSs is extended to the misuse, imitation and evocation of the registered names on goods as well as on services in order to ensure a high level of protection and to align that protection with that which applies to the wine sector.
Traditional specialities are protected under the same rules as protected designations. The regulation also applies to wine.
To be protected, the protected designation of origin must be indicated on the labelling and it must be possible to associate it with the indication ‘Traditional speciality guaranteed’ (see summary).
The European Union symbol (EU symbol) must be used on the packaging of products bearing the protected DOS. The EU symbol must be accompanied by the protected geographical indication (GGI) on the same packaging.
In addition, the European Commission may adopt guidelines on optional quality terms, such as ‘mountain products’ or ‘tourist products,’, which may give rise to consumer confusion if they are used in combination with a protected designation or GGI. These guidelines should be adopted by the Commission within 3 years of the adoption of the regulation.
National authorities must ensure that:
the registered names are used fairly;
the protection of the protected names is enforced;
producers organised into groups must themselves define the product in a specification.
A register of registered names and GIs is established. The registered names must be included in the register and they are automatically included in a register. The register also includes the names of producers who have been excluded from the register.
Procedures for the registration of protected names
The registered names of origin are examined by the competent authorities of the EU country concerned. The competent authorities may refuse or, if registered, invalidate a trade mark on grounds that it conflicts with a prior designation or geographical indication. They may also refuse to grant transitional protection at national level without affecting intra-EU or international trade.
An application for a protected name must be submitted to the competent authority of an EU country. If the application is refused, the applicant must submit a statement of the reasons for the refusal and the reasons why. This statement must include the name of the producer, the geographical origin of the product and a description of the characteristics of the products concerned.
EU countries may also decide to apply for transitional protection if they consider that the protected name does not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation or that it is likely to mislead or deceive consumers.
Exemptions
The Commission may exclude from the scope of this regulation certain protected names which have been granted transitional protection under the APQP. These include:
tourists’ names;
traditional products;
wine;
vegetative products.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
European Agricultural Product Quality Policy (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ег  ъларскабрана гарка
Commission Implementing Decision (EU) 2016/852 of 20 December 2016 on the implementation of Regulation (EC, No 883/2004 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 March 2004 on the Protection of Designations of Origin and of Protected Geographical Indications of Agricultural Products and Foodstuffs (OJ L 183, 21.12.2016, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, No 802/2004 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 108:
Aggressive phasing-in scheme for the application of the double-hull or equivalent design requirements of MARPOL 73/78 to single-Hull oil tankers (recast)
Aggressive phase-in mechanism for the implementation of the MARPOL 1973/78 requirements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2004 — accelerated phasing in of the application to single hull oil tanker design requirements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes an accelerated phase-out scheme to apply the requirements of the Marine Environment Protection Committee (MEPC) Regulation (MARPOL) 73/1978 on the construction of double hulls for the transport of heavy grade oil.
It aims to reduce the risk of accidental oil pollution in European waters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all ships of 600 tonnes deadweight and above. It does not apply to vessels of Category 2 or Category 3 which are equipped only with double bottoms or double sides not used for transport of oil and extending for the whole length of the cargo tank, or with double sides which are used only for the transportation of crude oil, and which are not equipped with a double bottom or a double side.
Exemptions from the regulation are granted to vessels flying the flag of an EU country.
EU countries have the right to deny entry into the ports or offshore terminals under their jurisdiction to oil tanker allowed to operate under this exception.
The EU countries may also ban the transport to or from ports of the EU countries of heavy-grade oil in single hulled ships.
Compliance with the double hull requirements
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure the smooth operation of the regulation, including:
the continued operation, in accordance with Regulation 20.5.5 of Annex I to MARPOL, of Category 1 and Category 2 and Category 3 heavy-gauge tankers flying the flags of a EU country;
the entry into ports and offshore terminals of other Category 2, Category 3 and Category 1 oil tanker ships flying a flag of a non-EU country, irrespective of the fact that they continue to be registered in the EU.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
any oil tanker above 15 years of age, which is under their flag, complies with the condition assessment scheme referred to in Article 6.2 of the annex to the regulation;
that the vessel meets the requirements laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation is one of a series of measures adopted by the European Union (EU) in order to ensure that oil tanker operators comply with the rules of the International Maritime Organization (IMO) and the European Convention on the Law of the Sea.
For more information, see:
Maritime safety and security (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 109:
European system of financial supervision (ESFS) (2010) - implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
European System of Financial Supervision (EFSF) (2009)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 establishing the European system of Financial supervision
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU system of European financial supervision.
It aims to improve the quality and consistency of national financial supervision, strengthening oversight of cross-border groups and establishing a European single rule book applicable to all financial institutions in the internal market.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the role of the European Banking Authority (EBA).
The EBA’s main tasks are to:
assist competent national supervisory authorities in the consistent interpretation and application of EU rules;
contribute to financial stability necessary for financial integration;
ensure the level playing field for financial institutions and other stakeholders, including consumers and employees.
In addition, the EBA is responsible for the elaboration of draft regulatory technical standards, which are specific to the financial services area.
EBA is also responsible for:
monitoring the financial stability of financial institutions;
assisting the European Stability Mechanism (ESM) and the European Financial Stability Facility (ESF);
ensuring the soundness of the financial system;
exercising delegated power.
The delegated power is exercised in the following ways:
to investigate alleged incorrect or insufficient application of Union law obligations by national authorities in their supervisory practice, concluded by a recommendation.
to take specific action to remedy an emergency situation.
To ensure that the result of delegated powers is actually achieved, and that the efficiency of the decision-making process is maintained.
When delegated powers are exercised in relation to cross-sectoral issues that have been coordinated through the Joint Committee, the ESAs must be accountable, through the joint committee, for such coordination.
Delegated power is also exercised in respect of:
the establishment of a European financial stability board;
the harmonisation of the rules applicable to financial institutions across the EU;
regulatory technical standards for the financial sector; and
the coordination of the supervisory activities of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Driving European Recovery’: the European financial crisis and the ESFS (2010).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Е хранска гараца
Commission Regulation (EC, No 553/2009) of 4 March 2009 establishing the new European system for the supervision of financial services (OJ L 127, 7.4.2009, pp. 1–8)

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement for workers, employment and vocational training: Regulation (EC) No 596/2006
Freedom of mobility for workers, employment and Vocational Training: Regulation(EC) no 596 / 2006
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2006 on freedom of movement of workers, labour market and employment in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which workers from one EU country may be employed in another EU country.
It aims to improve the mobility of workers in the EU and to promote their social and economic advancement.
KEY POINTS
 
Eligibility for employment
Any national of an EU country and any employer pursuing an activity in the territory of a Member State may exchange their applications for and offers of employment, and may conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action, without any discrimination resulting therefrom.
The Member States must designate specialist services which are entrusted with organising work in the fields covered by the regulation, cooperating with each other and with the departments of the European Commission. The Member States concerned forward to the Commission the list, drawn up by common accord, of services referred to in paragraph 1 and the Commission publishes such list for information, and any amendment thereto, in the Official Journal of the EU.
Employment services must grant workers who are nationals of the Member States the same priority as the relevant measures grant to nationals vis-à-vis workers from non-EU countries.
Applicants for employment must provide information concerning living and working conditions and the state of the labour market as likely to be of guidance to workers from the other Member States. This information must be sent to the specialist services of the other EU countries and to the European Coordination Office.
Applications for employment should be responded to by the relevant services within a reasonable period, not exceeding 1 month.
National workers must enjoy all the rights and benefits accorded to national workers in matters of housing, including ownership of the housing he needs. They must enjoy the same social and tax advantages as national workers.
Children of a national who is or has been employed in an EU Member State must be admitted to that State’s general educational, apprenticeship and vocational education courses under the same conditions as the nationals of that State, if such children are residing in its territory.
An Advisory Committee composed of 6 members of each Member State is responsible for all the technical duties in this field, and especially for assisting the national employment services. It is composed of representatives of the trade unions and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom).
The Advisory Committee is not bound by any special agreements with non-Member States. It may, however, adopt decisions which may affect the eligibility of workers or employers.
In addition, the Advisory Committee may adopt decisions that may have a significant impact on the implementation of the regulation.

Summary 111:
Limiting the use of ozone-depleting substances
Limitting the uses of ozone depleting substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on hydrochlorofluorocarbons
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) implements the Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer (Ozone Layer).
It recasts and repeals Council Regulation (EEC) 88/540/EEC.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
products and equipment containing or relying on substances classified as ODS;
the import and export of such products and equipment in the EU.
It does not apply to the following:
electrical and electronic waste;
construction and demolition waste and equipment;

The annexes to the regulation provide for the placing on the market of substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses;
processes in which controlled substances may be used as process agents.
In addition, the annexes provide for a list of substances that are subject to a ban on their use.

Summary 112:
The European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment (Euratom) (EURatom)
The European Environment Agency (EPA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1605/2002 on the European Union agency for the protection of the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU) agency to collect, analyse and disseminate information on the state of the EU’s environment.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1049/2001.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the Agency.
The Agency is responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on:
the quality of the European environment;
the pressures on the environment; and
the sensitivity of the environmental;including placing these in the context of sustainable development;
providing the necessary technical and scientific support.
EU countries must keep the Agency informed of the main component elements of their national environment information networks, especially in the priority areas referred to in Article 3(2), including any institution which in their judgment could contribute to the work of the agency, taking into account the need to ensure the fullest possible geographical coverage of their territory.
Agency staff
The agency has a Management Board consisting of one representative of each EU country and two representatives of the Commission. It is chaired by the Executive Director. The Executive Director is assisted by a Scientific Committee. The Committee of the Management Board is composed of representatives from the Member States and the European Parliament and the Council.
EURACHIEVEMENTS
Each year, the Agency adopts its work programme on the basis of a draft submitted by the executive director after consulting the scientific committee and receiving the Commission's opinion. The management board adopts the Agency's work programme, which must be approved by the management board. The annual work programme must include all the elements enabling it to gather the information making it possible to describe the present and foreseeable state of Europe's environment from the following points of view:
The quality of our environment
The pressures on our environment and the pressures on it is experiencing
the sensitivities and the importance of environmental protection.
In addition, the work programme includes:
a multiannual work programme for the period 2014-2020;
a report on the results of this work programme;
an annual report for the year ending 31 March 2021.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU‘s environmental policy is based on a number of principles.
For more information, see:
EU environmental policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Environmental Agency: the Agency is the body responsible for collecting and analysing environmental information for the implementation of EU environmental policy.
* National Environment Information Network: the network of national environmental information networks in the EU. It consists of the following institutions:
European Commission;
European Parliament and Council of Europe;
Council of Europe.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2002/852/EC of 21 December 2002 on the establishment of the Environmental Agency (Eureatom) [Official Journal L 136 of 21.12.2002].
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 1605 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 113:
Eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS)
Eeco-management & audit scheme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the eco-management* scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to encourage European Union (EU) organisations to participate in the European Union’s (EU)-wide eco-Management and Audit Scheme (EAS) and to publish rigorous and independently verified environmental or sustainable development performance reports.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all organisations, in and outside the EU, whose activities have an environmental impact.
Organisations must:
ensure that their environmental management and environmental management systems are in place;
provide the public and other interested parties with information on their compliance with applicable legal requirements relating to the environment and their environmental performance;
make publicly available periodic environmental statements and annual reports;
publish and make publicly available their environmental programme, objectives and targets;
report to the European Commission every 3 years on how they are achieving their environmental objectives, targets and targets.
The Commission must produce sectoral reference documents in the area covered by this regulation following a priority programme. These must include best environmental management practice and environmental performance indicators for specific sectors.
Registration of organisations
Registered organisations must provide the Commission with:
a copy of their environmental statement and an updated environmental statement;
an annual report;
a list of the organisations that have been accredited by a national accreditation body;
the number of organisations registered in the EU and those that have not been accredited.
a description of the organisation and the nature and scope of their activities covered by the regulation.
An annual report must be made available to the Commission and to the public.
EU countries must ensure that registered organisations are able to:
participate in EMAS on a voluntary basis and may gain added value in terms of regulatory control, cost savings and public image, provided that they can demonstrate an improvement of the environmental performance of their organisation;
participation in the scheme is open to employees and workers of the company;
have an open dialogue with the public, and the active involvement of employees in organisations and provide appropriate training.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â  Â
 х 
The European Commission adopted the regulation on behalf of the European Economic Area (EEA).
For more information, see:
Environmental management and audit schemes (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* EMAS: a scheme designed to encourage the participation of EU organisations in the Community's eco-managing and audit system.
* Verification: the conformity assessment process carried out by an environmental verifier to demonstrate whether an organisation's environmental review, environmental policy, environmental management system and internal environmental audit and its implementation fulfils the requirements of this regulation;

Summary 114:
Production, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks
Production and presentation of spirits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1576/89 — rules on the production, description and presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain spirits
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition, description*, presentation*, labelling*, production*, presentation* and protection* of certain spirit drinks in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks produced in the EU for export. It does not apply to drinks produced on the territory of non-EU countries.
Producers must comply with the following rules:
the ethyl alcohol used in the production of spirits must not be of any origin other than agricultural origin;
the spirit drinks must not contain natural or nature-identical flavouring substances and preparations;
they must be sweetened to correspond to particular product characteristics and according to the particular legislation of the EU Member States.
The rules also apply to the geographical indications (GIs) registered in Annex III of the regulation. These are those which are recognised by the World Trade Organisation (WTO) and the European Commission and which are not recognised by national legislation.
In applying a quality policy for spirit drinks which are produced on their own territory and in particular for geographical indications registered in annex III or for the establishment of new geographical indications, Member States may lay down rules stricter than those in Annex II on production, describing, presentation*, production, presentation & labelling in so far as they are compatible with EU law. These rules must be adopted in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny provided for in Article 5a of Decision 1999/468/EC.
Quality policy
Member States must ensure that the rules laid down in the regulation apply to spirits produced in their territory. They must also take into account traditional production methods.
Distribution and marketing
Distributors and importers must:
ensure that the spirit drinks they sell in the Member States conform with the rules set out in Annexes I and II to the regulation;
notify the Commission of the names of the importers, importers and distributors of the spirits they sell;
use only the names and geographical indications listed in the annexes;
inform the Commission, the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and national authorities of the quality of the products they sell.
Labelling
The regulations set out the labelling requirements for each category of spirit drink. They include the following:
description of the spirit drink;
description and presentation;
presentation;
labelling;
protection of the geographical indication of certain geographical indications.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 February 1989.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to ensure the high level of quality of European spirit drinks and diversity in the sector.
For more information, see:
European spirit drinks (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Spirit drink: a spirit drink is defined as a spirit which is produced in a spirit-making process and which is intended to be consumed by the consumer.
* GIs: a geographical indication is a designation of a type of spirit which has been registered in the Official Journal of the European Economic Area (OJ L 347, 29.12.1989, pp. 1-2).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Егаланска грабарка   глака
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1508/89 of 20 February 1989 on the rules of the production and description of spirits and repealing Regulation (EEC, EEC, EFSA, EEA, EC, EFTA, EC and EEA countries’ national legislation on the presentation, description of spirit beverages and on the protection of their geographical indications and repealing Council Regulation (EU) No 552/2004 (OJR) No 1/2003).

Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation against acts of unlawful interference that jeopardise the security of civil aviation (Regulation (EC) No 2320/2002) (see summary)
Common rules to safeguard civil aviation
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — common rules for the protection of civil aircraft
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common rules to protect persons and goods within the European Union (EU).
It seeks to improve the level of security in the EU’s aviation sector.
It aims to:
ensure uniform rules are applied to all airports or parts of airports located in the territory of an EU Member State that are not exclusively used for military purposes;
improve the levels of security for passengers and cargo;
reduce the risk of terrorist attacks on civil aircraft.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following:
airports or parts thereof located in an EU country;
all operators, including air carriers, providing services at airports referred to in point (a);
all entities applying aviation security standards that operate from premises located inside or outside airport premises and provide goods and/or services to or through airports;
airport security screening and screening procedures.
Airport security search: an inspection of the interior and accessible exterior of the aircraft in order to detect prohibited articles and unlawful interferences that could endanger the safety of the flight.
Security restricted area: an area of airside where, in addition to access being restricted, other security standards are applied.
security restricted area is a restricted area.
In-flight security officers: these are government personnel who are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board an aircraft. They are responsible for the provision of security on board the aircraft and for ensuring the safety and security of the passengers and crew.
EU Member States may decide whether to deploy in flight security officers on aircraft registered in that Member State and on flights of air carriers licensed by it as well as to ensure, in accordance with Article 4.7 of Annex 17 to the Chicago Convention on International Civil Aviation and under the terms of that Convention, that such officers are trained in the security aspects of aviation.
The Commission may use the urgency procedure for the adoption of common rules on aviation security.
Member States may designate one or more Member States as the single point of contact with third countries for the implementation of the common basic standards. The Member States must ensure that the security measures adopted by the Member States are equivalent to the common standards and that these measures are monitored by the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
The European Commission must monitor the application by Member States of this regulation, and also to make recommendations to improve aviation security, including inspections, including unannounced inspections. The Commission may draw up a report on the application of the regulation and make recommendations on how it should be improved. It may also draw up recommendations to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (Euratom) on measures to be taken to improve civil aviation security and to improve cooperation between Member States.
If Member States consider that the measures adopted under this regulation are not equivalent to those adopted under the common rules, they must inform the Commission and the EASA of this decision. If the Commission considers that Member States have not complied with the requirements of this Regulation, it may adopt a new regulation. If Member States do not comply with the regulation, the Commission may adopt an amendment to the regulation. The regulation may also be amended in the light of the experience gained, and the regulation itself should be repealed and replaced by Regulation (EC, ECSC, Euratom) No 552/2014.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Civil aviation: any air operation carried out by civil aircraft, excluding operations carried out in State aircraft. It includes:
passenger transport;
cargo transport.
* Air transport: the transport of dangerous goods, such as explosive substances and explosive devices, by air.
MAIN DOCUMENT

any person, entity or body acting on behalf of a natural or legal person or body;

any individual or entity that has a financial interest in funds or financial resources;
an interest in these funds or resources.
the European Union territory.
EU countries and the European Commission must inform each other EU countries of the measures taken pursuant to this regulation within 2 weeks of the authorisation.
The EU countries must inform the other countries of any authorisation granted under this regulation.

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE OF THE EUROPEAN UNMISSIONARY OPERATION IN THE MEDIARY
FORCE IN THE MIDMARY
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 of 31 March 2020 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to launch a new EU military operation to combat human smuggling and trafficking by using aerial, satellite and maritime assets.
KEY POINTS
The decision authorises EU naval operation ‘EU NAVFOR MED IRINI’, which is based in Rome, Italy, to:
contribute to the implementation of the UN arms embargo on Libya;
ensure that Libyan vessels and their crews are seized and disposed of in accordance with UN Security Council Resolution (UNSCR) No 2509 (2020);
combat the smuggling of arms and related illicit cargo from Libya.
It also aims to prevent the illicit export of petroleum from Libya, to capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy, and to the disruption of the business model of human smuggling.
Objectives
The operation’s main tasks are:
to:
implement the United Nations (UN) arms embargo;
combat human smuggling, including by seizing, seizing and disposing of illicit cargo and related cargo;
disrupting the business models of human trafficking;
to prevent the export of oil and other illicit goods from Libya; and
to disrupt the activities of smuggling networks.
To achieve these objectives, the operation is based on:
an aerial surveillance mission;
a satellite surveillance mission in close coordination with other relevant EU countries;
an operational coordination with the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
a monitoring mechanism in close cooperation with other EU countries, including where necessary in Libya, and
a training mission.
The EU Operation Commander is responsible for the organisation of the operation and the deployment of assets. The operation commander reports to the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR) and the Political and Security Committee (PSCSC) of the EU. The High Representative may at any time, upon request from the HR or a Member State, give direction to the Operation Commander on the deployment and use of assets, including:
the deployment of aircraft and naval vessels;
the use of satellite surveillance and maritime surveillance assets;
operational planning and coordination of operations;
implementation of the naval and air surveillance missions.
In addition, the decision establishes a monitoring mechanism, which:
is responsible for:
monitoring and analysing human smuggling;
collecting and storing data on human smuggling* and trafficking, as well as data related to the vessels and equipment used by such persons, and the relevant information acquired while carrying out this core task, to the relevant law enforcement authorities of Member States and to competent EU bodies;
providing and storing necessary medical information and biometric data on trainees, provided that they have given their consent in writing.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Decision has applied since 1 March 2020 and had to become law in the Member States by 31 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Council authorised the EU to launch the operation on 17 February 2020.
For more information, see:
‘EU naval mission in Libya’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU/472/2020)

Authenticity, identity and residence documents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the establishment of the FADO system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Agency for the Protection of the Internal Border and Airspace of the European Union (EASA).
It sets up a system for the exchange of information on authentic documents and on known methods of falsification between EU countries’ authorities.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a system to contain information on all types of authentic travel documents, including driving licences and vehicle licences and on other related official documents, in particular those used in support of applications for official documents issued by third parties, such as third countries, territorial entities, international organisations and other entities subject to international law, and on false versions thereof.
EU countries are obliged to provide the EASA with information on the authentic documents which they issue, including on the security features of such documents, as well as on the false versions in their possession.
The Agency is responsible for the proper and reliable functioning of the system and for providing support to the competent EU country authorities in the detection of false documents. It is assisted by the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBSA). The EBSA has the following tasks:
ensuring that the system is up-to-date and reliable;
providing information on valid travel documents and their recognition by EU countries;
recommending effective ways of detecting specific methods of false document falsification;
assisting EU countries in the development of effective methods of detecting and combating document fraud;
coordinating with the European Commission, the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF, Eurojust and Eurojust.

Summary 119:
Physicians’ access to the market: proportionality assessment
Physicians' access to market:portionality assessmentSUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/36/EU on the proportionality of the requirements restricting access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the regulation of the regulated professions* in the European Union (EU) is proportionate.
It sets out the criteria for assessing the proportionability of these requirements and the additional criteria which are relevant for the regulated profession being analysed.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all regulated professions. It does not apply to professions which are not regulated directly by the EU.
Proportionality
The regulation of regulated professions must be proportionate to the importance of the objectives pursued and the expected gains. It must be justified by public interest objectives and not go beyond what is necessary in order to attain that objective.
Exceptions
Procedures which do not restrict access to regulated professions, such as editorial amendments, technical adaptations to the content of training courses or the modernisation of training regulations, should not fall within the scope of the directive.
In addition, any unjustified restriction resulting from national law restricting the freedom of establishment or the freedom to provide services is prohibited, including any discrimination on grounds of nationality or residence.
Requests for professional organisation or body membership
Member States must take into account the objective of a high level of human health protection, including accessibility and quality of healthcare services, and the need to promote the exchange of information between professionals and consumers.
The European Commission has launched a ‘mutual evaluation process’ to assess the measures of the Member States.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Access to a regulated profession’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егандарскацка гарака  баркакова   Пантананка, ‘Regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council on the approximation of the principles of non-discrimination and proportionality’.
Successive amendments to the directive have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 120:
Multiannual multiannual plan for the management of fish stocks in the North Sea (2014-2023) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 — multiannal plan for fish stocks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the implementation of the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP).
It sets out the objectives, quantifiable targets, conservation reference points, safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to demersal stocks and their fisheries in the north sea.
It does not apply to stocks of common interest, such as:
cod;
fishing opportunities;
Norway lobster;
seaweed.
The plan must be adopted by 6 August 2023 and every 5 years thereafter. It must include:
the objectives and targets set out in the plan;
the timeframes and targets for achieving the objectives;
safeguards linked to biomass;
measures to prevent and reduce the impact of overfishing on the marine environment and the sustainable management of all commercially exploited species and the achievement of good environmental status by 2020.
Safeguards
The Commission may adopt delegated acts adjusting the geographical distribution of stocks if the scientific advice provided by ICES indicates a change in the distribution of the relevant stocks.
In addition, the Commission must assess the adequacy and effectiveness of the application of the regulation based on scientific advice.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLAN APPLY?
From 6 August 2014
It applies from 6 August 2024.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted the CFP since 1 January 2014.
For more information, see:
Fisheries management in the EU (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Multi-annual plan (MFP): an annual plan, adopted by the Commission, for a period of 5 years starting from 1 August 2014 and ending on 31 December 2023.
* Multiannual management plan: an annual management plan that aims to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce fish stocks above the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) levels, as determined by their biological characteristics.

Summary 121:
Management, conservation and control of fishing for straddling fish stocks in the SPRFMO Convention Area (Regulation (EU) 2017/2403) (OJ L 183, 29.10.2017, pp. 1-2)
Management, Conservation and control provisions relating to fishing for fishing for fish stocks within the Strategic Marine Pollution Management Area (SPRFMO) Convention Area
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 2403/2017 laying down management, conservation, control and enforcement provisions for fishing in the strategic marine pollution management area (SPFMO)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the management, control, inspection and enforcement of fishing activities in the SPFMO.
It aims to ensure the long-term conservation and sustainable use of fishery resources, and, in so doing, to safeguard the marine ecosystems in which those resources occur.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
deepwater gillnets* (e.g. trammel nets, set nets, anchored nets, sink nets, means strings of single, double or triple netting walls, held vertically, on or near the bottom, in which fish will gill, entangle or enmesh);
jack mackerel fisheries;
seabird mitigation measures.
EU countries must report to the European Commission the following data regarding:
the number of vessels flying their flag engaged in transhipment in the preceding month;
the total number of fish caught in the previous year.
The Commission must submit a report every 3 years to the Commission on the implementation of the regulation.
Fishing vessels must not engage in bottom fishing outside the bottom fishing footprint or exceeding reference catch levels without prior authorisation from the SPRFMO. They must also not use bottom fishing gears that are known or are likely to occur in areas where VMEs are known to occur. Where possible, EU fishing vessels must be prohibited from discharging offal during shooting and hauling.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that fishing vessels using longlines comply with the seabird management measures set out in this regulation;
not use any fishing gear that is likely to endanger seabirds.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the management of the fisheries under its purview.
For more information, see:
Fisheries and aquaculture (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1924/2017 of 29 October 2017 laying down Management, Conservation, Control and Enforcement Provisions relating to Fishing for Straddling Fish Stocks in the Strategic Maritime Pollution Monitoring Area (‘Regulation’)
Successive amendments to Regulation (E24/2017 have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 122:
Cross-border movements of cash
Cross-Border movements of money
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1939 on the movement of cash in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and deter the circumvention of the obligation to declare or disclose cash.
It lays down the essential components of the definition of ‘cash’, while at the same time enabling the Commission to amend the non-essential components in response to the attempts by criminals and their associates to circumvent a measure which controls only one type of highly-liquid store of value by bringing another type across the external borders.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all persons, their luggage, the means of transport used to cross the EU’s external borders and any unaccompanied consignment or receptacle crossing that border which may contain cash. It does not apply to persons entering or leaving the EU.
The definition of cash includes:
prepaid cards, in particular if they can be bought without customer due diligence procedures;
cash couriers;
bills, cheques and other forms of payment.
EU countries must:
ensure that the competent authorities collect and perform controls on cash movements;
notify the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and Europol of any non-compliance with the rules of the regulation;
identify and inform the OLAF and the Europol if there are indications of criminal activity;
share information relating to sub-threshold amounts with the authorities in other EU countries if there is indications of serious criminal activity.
In addition, the regulation sets out a number of standardised data elements, such as the personal details of the declarant, the owner or the recipient, the economic provenance and the intended use of the cash, which must be recorded in order to achieve the objectives of this regulation. These data must be exchanged between EU countries and with the Commission, in accordance with standards to be set out in implementing acts adopted by the European Parliament and of the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 April 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 April 2018.
BACKGROUND
The introduction of Directive 91/308/EEC (see summary) has led to an increase in cash movements for illicit purposes, which could pose a threat to the financial system and the internal market.
For more information, see:
‘Movement of cash’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 123:
Integrated national energy and climate plans for the energy union
Integated national energy & climate plans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 517/2014 on the implementation of the Energy Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the framework for the implementation by European Union (EU) Member States of their integrated national energy* and climate* plans.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s energy policy is aligned with the Paris Agreement on climate change.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers five dimensions:
energy security;
the internal energy market;
energy efficiency;
decarbonisation;
research, innovation and competitiveness.
EU Member States’ national energy plans must cover 10-year periods and should provide an overview of the current energy system and policy situation. They must also cover the following:
the EU-level target for improvements in energy efficiency in 2030 to at least 32 %, including a review by 2020 with a view to increasing the level to 30 %;
a share of renewable energy consumed in the EU of at least 27 %, and electricity interconnection of 15 %.
The European Commission may, in addition to the national plans, identify further opportunities for cooperation covering one or more of the following areas:
research and innovation;
combating climate change;
smart grids and smart grids;
transmission of energy information and communication technologies;
climate change; and
energy poverty.
In addition, Member States must:
identify the number of households in energy poverty, taking into account the necessary domestic energy services needed to guarantee basic standards of living in the relevant national context, existing social policy and other relevant policies, as well as Commission indicative guidance on relevant indicators, including geographical dispersion, that are based on a common approach for energy poverty;
ensure the participation of social partners in the preparation of the integrated national plans;
draw up a permanent multi-level energy dialogue, bringing together local authorities, civil society organisations, the business community, investors and other interested parties to discuss the different options envisaged for energy policies.
National plans must also include a description of the energy efficiency targets for the period 2020-2030 and the long-term objectives for the EU as a whole. They should also provide a summary of the progress made by Member States in achieving these objectives.
Member States must also report on actions that they undertake to phase out energy subsidies, in particular for fossil fuels.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 March 2014. Member States had to incorporate it into their national plans by 1 March 2016.
BACKGROUND
The Energy Union is the cornerstone of the EU's climate and energy policy. It aims to give EU consumers, including households and businesses, secure, sustainable, competitive and affordable energy, and to foster research and innovation by means of attracting investment.
For more information, see:
Energy Union (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: the energy policy of the European Union. It covers the energy security, energy efficiency, decarbonisation, research, innovation, competitiveness and research and development (R&D) areas.
* Smart grids: a network of interconnected grids that allow the transmission and distribution of electricity, gas and renewable energy across the EU.

Summary 124:
EU action against persistent organic pollutants (POPs) (Regulation (EC) No 1907/2006) and Regulation (EU) No 528/2012 on the control of substances listed in Annex II of the Convention on the Elimination of Persistent Organic Pollutants (OJ L 183, 16.7.2006, pp. 1-8)
EU action on persistent organic pollutants (PAPs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EC) no 1907/06/2006 — the European Union’s common legal framework for combating the release of POPs into the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to eliminate the production, placing on the market and use of intentionally manufactured POPs.
It repeals and repeals Council Regulation (EEC) 2004/98/EC.
KEY POINTS
The regulation:
sets out the list of substances to be banned in the EU and the EU countries;
lists the substances to which the European Commission and the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW) are responsible;
requires EU countries to report regularly to the EASW on the implementation of the regulation.
The European Commission is responsible for the administrative, technical and scientific aspects of implementing the regulation and the exchange of information between EU countries. It is assisted by the European Waste Management Agency (EWMA).
EU countries must:
report regularly, in standardised form, to the Agency, in particular as regards:
release inventories, notified stockpiles and the manufacturing and placing of restricted substances;
monitoring data on the presence of substances in the environment;
ensure that the Agency has access to the information it receives;
identify and reduce the quantities of substances that are being manufactured and used;
take appropriate measures to control existing substances and prevent the manufacturing, placing of new substances.
Eliminating POPs
In line with the Protocol and Convention on Pollution Prevention and Control of Pollution from Chemicals, the EU is committed to:
ensuring that POPs which are unintentional by-products of industrial processes are identified and reduced as soon as possible, with the ultimate aim of elimination, where feasible;
implementing measures to prevent new substances from being added to the list;
establishing programmes and mechanisms to provide adequate monitoring data on POPs and to monitor their presence.
In addition, the regulation sets out:
measures to be taken by EU countries in the field of waste management, including:
the setting up of a waste management system;
the establishment of a European waste management network;
measures for the management of waste;
rules on the disposal of waste.
EU Member States must also:
draw up, implement and update their implementation plans;
provide the Agency with regular reports on their implementation of this regulation;
assist EWMA with the monitoring of the substances on the list.
Waste management
The Commission is assisted in matters relating to waste by the committee set up by Directive 2008/96/EC with a view to ensuring a consistent approach concerning waste legislation of the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2007/36/EC has applied since 16 July 2006 and had to become law in the Member States by 16 July 2007.
Regulations (EC, EC) No 1906/2006, 2007/2008, 2008/2009 and 2008/10/EC have applied since 1 July 2008.
Amendments to Regulation (EC), EC, EC and EC have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
BACKGROUND
The EU is seriously concerned by the continuous release of persistent organic compounds (‘pOPs’). It has adopted a number of measures to combat the problem. These include:
Protocol on Pollutant Release from Industrial Combustion (Protocol of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 1997 on the elimination of persistent pollutants (Protection of the Environment from Pollution) and the Convention of the International Atomic Energy Community on the Emissions of Chemicals from Industrial Processes (Convention of the Hague);
the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) on the protection of the environment against the risks posed by the release and degradation of organic pollutants;
EU Action Plan for the Protection of the Earth (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘Persistent organic pollutants’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Persistent organic pollutant: a substance which is a by-product of an industrial process and which has the potential to be harmful to human health and the environment.
* Permitted substance: any substance, whether on their own, in mixtures or in articles, which exhibits characteristics of a POP.

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products: reducing their impact on the environment (Directive 2013/13/EU)
Single-usable plastic products : reducing its impact on society (DirectIVE 2013/14/EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) No 1313/2014 on the reduction of plastic pollution in the marine environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the amount of plastic litter in the EU’s marine environment.
It applies to all plastic products, including food containers, fast-food containers and meal, sandwich, wrap and salad boxes with cold or hot food, or food containers of fresh or processed food that does not need further preparation, such as fruits, vegetables or desserts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
all plastic products that are found the most on beaches in the European Union (EU);
food and beverage containers and cups for beverages made of expanded polystyrene;
fishing gear containing plastic and products made from oxo-degradable plastic;
plastics manufactured with modified natural polymers, or plastics manufactured from bio-based, fossil or synthetic starting substances, which are not naturally occurring and should therefore be addressed by this directive.
Producers of plastic products are responsible for the costs of cleaning up litter. They must also ensure that their products are regularly disposed of through the sewer system or otherwise inappropriately disposed of.
In addition, the directive sets out rules on the labelling and labelling of these products.
Labelling requirements
Labels must be clearly visible and clearly distinguishable from those of other plastic products. The labelling must also include the following information:
the number of trips or rotations by which the product is intended to be used;
the purpose for which it is intended;
how it is to be refilled or re-used for the same purpose.
The labelling requirements apply to all single-use products, irrespective of whether they are:
food containers;
cups for beverages;
fast food containers and containers for beverages that do not require further preparation;
lays of fishing gear;
coffee and tea bags containing plastic.
Exemptions from the directive
EU countries may exclude certain products from the scope of the directive if they consider that they do not pose a risk to human health, the environment or the economy.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Strategy for Plastics is a step towards establishing a circular economy in which the design and production of plastics and plastic products fully respect re-use, repair and recycling needs and in which more sustainable materials are developed and promoted.
For more information, see:
‘Plastics and plastic’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 126:
EU trade marks — protection of goods and services (Regulation (EU) No 608/2013 — protecting trade marks (codified version)
EU Trade marks — protecting trades and services
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2015/852 — protection and administration of EU trade marks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down rules for the designation and classification of goods* and services covered by the EU trade mark.
It aims to ensure that trade marks are recognised and protected throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
goods and services for which trade mark protection is sought;
trade marks for which protection is granted;
the goods for which the application for protection is made.
Applicants may apply for protection as a national trade mark in one or more EU countries, or only as an EU-wide trade mark, or both.
The application must be submitted to the European Office for Harmonisation and Harmonisation of Trade Marks (Euratom).
The applicant must provide the competent authorities and economic operators with sufficient clarity and precision to enable them to determine the extent of the protection applied for. The applicant must also be able to demonstrate that:
the trade mark is intended for a specific type of goods or services;
it is not intended to be used as an indication of origin;
there is no link between the trade mark and the goods or service.
the applicant is entitled to exclude from the scope of protection any other trade mark which is not covered by this regulation.
a trade name which is identical to that of the applicant.
A trade mark may not be used to draw the consumer’s attention to the resale of genuine goods that were originally sold by or with the consent of the proprietor of the mark in the EU.
Use of a trade mark by third parties
The proprietor is not entitled to prohibit the use of signs or indications which are used fairly and thus in accordance with honest practices in industrial and commercial matters.
Exclusive rights of the owner
The exclusive rights conferred by the owner of an EU Trade mark should not entitle the owner to prohibit third parties from using a sign in comparative advertising where such comparative advertising is contrary to Directive 2006/114/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary).
In addition, the owner may not prohibit the following from being used:
in the context of comparative advertising
in relation to the sale of goods originating in another EU country.
In relation to trade marks which are not protected at EU level.

Summary 127:
Access to published works for persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled: Marrakesh Treaty
Assistance to persons with disabilities: Access to Published Works for Persons who are Blind, Visually Impaired or otherwise Print Disabled
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the implementation, on behalf of the European Union, of the Marrakh Treaty
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to implement the obligations of the Treaty on European Union (Marrakesh) with respect to the export and import arrangements for accessible format copies for non-commercial purposes for the benefit of beneficiary persons between the EU and non-EU countries that are parties to the treaty.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
An authorised entity established in an EU country may distribute, communicate or make available to beneficiary persons or to an its primary activities, institutional obligations or as part of its public-interest missions. It also includes a public institution or non-profit organisation that provides the same services to beneficiaries persons as one of its primary, institutional or public-service missions.
It does not apply to the processing of personal data.
EU countries must establish and follow the practices referred to in the first subparagraph of the regulation in full respect of the rules applicable to processing personal data of beneficiaries.
Exports and imports
An EU country must allow for the importation of, and access to, from a third country, by beneficiary persons in the EU, and authorised entities established in a EU country, for the purpose of the exchange of these accessible formats.
This regulation does not affect the conditions under which the authorisation is granted for the export of, or the access to these copies, under the same conditions as those laid down in Directive 2001/29/EC on the harmonisation of certain aspects of copyright and related rights in the information society (see summary).
Exceptions and limitations to copyright
In addition, the regulation requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations in relation to copyright, related rights and the protection of the intellectual property rights of the authorising entities for the making, dissemination or access to certain works.
Export and import of accessible formats to third countries
The Commission must carry out an evaluation of this regulation and present the main findings in a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee, accompanied, where appropriate, by proposals for amending this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 17 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to promote the objectives of the treaty, including:
exchange of information on the accessibility of cultural materials;
access to cultural materials for the purposes of education and training;
exchanges of information and training on the conditions for access to cultural material;
the exchange of information about cultural materials.
For more information, see:
Accessible formats (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Accessible format: a format that allows a person with a disability to read, write or listen to a written or electronic document.
* Printed works: works that are intended for the reading, writing or listening of a person who is blind, visually impaired or print disabled.

Summary 128:
Enforcement of EU consumer protection legislation
En enforcement of EU consumers’ rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 on the enforcement of consumer protection
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities cooperate with each other more quickly and more efficiently and to deter traders from committing infringements covered by this regulation.
It also aims to improve cooperation with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not cover cross-border infringements.
EU countries must ensure that competent authorities have a minimum set of investigation and enforcement powers in order to apply the regulation. These powers are based on:
the right to purchase goods or services as test purchases, where necessary, under a cover identity;
refusals to implement the consumer right of withdrawal in the case of distance contracts, and to obtain evidence.
They must also have the right to impose penalties for infringements, such as fines, for failing to comply with the regulation’s requirements.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide the European Commission with information on suspected infringements;
ensure that the competent authorities of the relevant EU countries and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) exchange information on infringements with the aim of preventing them from being repeated;
use the mutual assistance mechanism to respond to requests for assistance.
Coordinated action
If there is a reasonable suspicion of a widespread infringement, the competent authority concerned by that infringement should, by agreement, launch a coordinated action.
The competent authorities should:
notify the Commission and the OLAF of any infringements they suspect;
send alerts to the relevant national authorities and OLAFs of suspected infringement;
make use of other powers or measures granted to them at the national level, including the power to initiate or refer matters for criminal prosecution.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a communication on the implementation of the regulation in the context of the ‘Upgrading the Single Market Strategy: more opportunities for people and business’.
For more information, see:
EU consumer protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cross-border infringement: an infringement that takes place in more than one EU country.
* Distance contract: a contract between a seller and a user.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU) 2015/852 of 28 October 2015 laying down the rules for the implementation and enforcement of Regulation (EC, No 6/2004) No 2111/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the Enforcement of Union Consumer Protection (OJ L 127, 29.10.2015, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, no 611/2006 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices: safety and performance requirements
In vitro diagnostics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on the safety, performance and quality of in vitro medical devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the placing on the market and putting into service in the EU the safety and quality requirements for in vitro diagnostic (i.e. in vitro) medical devices.
It aims to ensure the smooth functioning of the internal market as regards these devices, taking into account the small and medium-sized enterprises that are active in this sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
medical devices, such as medical devices, general laboratory products and products for research use, and their accessories;
in vitro diagnostic devices, manufactured and used within a single health institution only;
products intended for use in a single healthcare institution only.
The scope of the regulation does not cover establishments primarily claiming to pursue health interests or healthy lifestyles (e.g. gyms, spas, wellness and fitness centres).
It does not apply to the following categories of products:
biomarker or biomarker* tests;
diagnostic tests that provide information on the predisposition to a medical condition or a disease;
categories of tests that predict treatment response or reactions;
companion diagnostics.
Manufacturers’ responsibilities
The manufacturer is responsible for the manufacture and control of the devices, as well as post-market surveillance and vigilance activities concerning them.
the authorised representatives of the manufacturer’s organisation are responsible for ensuring that the devices comply with the regulation.
Notified bodies’ assessments of manufacturers’ technical documentation, in particular documentation on performance evaluation, must be critically evaluated by the authority responsible for notified bodies.
EU countries must establish a system of notified bodies to monitor the compliance of manufacturers and their authorised representatives with the regulations.
Importers must inform the competent authorities of any serious adverse events, device deficiencies and related updates.
Monitoring and monitoring of the manufacture of devices must be carried out by a person responsible for regulatory compliance who fulfils minimum conditions of qualification.
If a device is found to be defective, the authorised representative must be held liable for defective devices in the event that a manufacturer established outside the EU has not complied with its general obligations.
In addition, the regulation sets out the rules on EU market surveillance and controls of products entering the EU market.
Quality and risk management
The European Commission must ensure that manufacturers:
have a quality assurance system in place to ensure that their devices meet the requirements laid down in the regulation;
have an authorised representative responsible for monitoring and controlling the devices’ manufacture;
ensure that the quality and safety of their devices are monitored and monitored by the competent national authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    дарганскабранка гака  
It has applied since 1 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  баркаковарна 
For more information, see:
‘In vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* In vitro diagnostic: a medical device that can be used to diagnose, monitor and treat a disease or condition. It can be placed in the body, in vitro or in a lab, and is designed to be used in combination with other medical devices to diagnose and treat the disease. It is a medical tool for the diagnosis, monitoring and treatment of diseases and conditions.
