Summary 158:
Cross-border, transnational and interregional cooperation with the EU’s neighbouring countries (Regulation (EU) No 1301/2013)
Cross-boundary, cross-border and intra-regional coordination with non-EU countries
The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) is the main instrument of the European cohesion policy (EU cohesion policy). It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1303/2013 of 25 November 2013 establishing the European Regional development Fund.
SUMMARY
This regulation establishes the European territorial cooperation goal for the European Structural and Investment Funds (ESIF). The ESIF is one of the main instruments of the EU cohesion policy.
The ESIF aims to:
support and facilitate, through specific arrangements, effective trans-border cooperation with countries outside the EU;
promote high employment that results in social and territorial cohesion, including through activities supporting sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage as part of a territorial strategy aimed at achieving employment-friendly growth;
develop administrative capacity.
This Regulation sets out specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the ERDF, and defines the goals for those activities. It also sets out the specific character of cooperation programmes, which are set out in an annex to the regulation.
Crossborder cooperation
The ERDF is to support or, as appropriate, to establish cross-bilateral cooperation with EU countries which border the EU. This cooperation should aim to strengthen cooperation by means of actions conducive to integrated territorial development linked to the EU's cohesion policy priorities, and should also include maritime cooperation not covered by cross-based cooperation programmes.
Transnational cooperation
This cooperation should support and facilitate effective cooperation between EU countries’ neighbouring regions. It should be focused on:
developing administrative capacity;
strengthening cooperation in the areas of transport, information and communication technology;
improving the quality of life of people in the regions of the participating EU countries;
encouraging the exchange of information and skills between the participating regions;
enhancing the capacity of the administrative authorities of participating regions to manage and implement cooperation programmes;
ensuring the effective implementation of the cooperation programme.
In addition, the European Territorial Cooperation goal should be concentrated on a limited number of thematic objectives in order to maximise the impact of cohesion policy across the Union.
Interregional Cooperation programmes
The Commission is to define transnational cooperation areas having regard to actions needed to promote integration territorial development. These areas are to be developed in line with the objectives of the InterACT and ESPON programmes. In addition, these cooperation programmes should be adapted, especially as regards the definition of the beneficiary or beneficiaries under the current INTERACT (Inter-European Coordination for the Development of the North-East of Europe) programmes. These programmes should also be adapted to the needs of the regions which participate in these programmes, and to the specific requirements of the ESIF.
Cooperation programmes must be approved by the participating Member States and the European Commission. The Commission approves only the core elements of the programmes, while the other elements are approved by each participating Member State or Member States.
Financing
The main sources of ERDF funding are:
the ESIF's investment for growth and jobs goal;
the investment for jobs goal. The ERDF also provides for:
transnational cooperation;
economic and social cohesion;
environmental protection;
transport;
agriculture and forestry;
science and technology; and
municipalities and rural areas.
EU Member States are to set out the resources allocated to each of the different components of the ETSIF, while maintaining a significant concentration on cross- border cooperation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
European territorial cooperation goals (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Resolution of 25 December 2013 laying down the European regional development fund and implementing the European structural and investment funds for the period 2007-2013 (OJ L 127, 1.2.2014, pp. 1-3)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1303 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 159:
Energy infrastructure priorities for 2020 and beyond: strategic energy infrastructure
Energy infrastructure prioritisation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 714/2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 July 2009 on conditions for access to the network for cross-border exchanges in electricity and gas.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the implementation of the strategic trans-European energy infrastructure priorities, which by 2020 are essential for the achievement of the EU’s energy and climate policy objectives.
It aims to modernise and expand the European energy infrastructure and to interconnect networks across borders, in order to make solidarity between EU countries operational, to provide for alternative supply or transit routes and sources of energy and to develop renewable energy sources in competition with traditional sources.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all energy infrastructure projects of common interest in the areas of energy, transport and telecommunication. The number of such projects is estimated at some 100 in the fields of electricity and 50 in the field of gas. It covers:
trans-European electricity transmission and distribution networks;
interconnections of gas and oil pipelines;
transport infrastructure;
railways;
telecommunications infrastructure.
EU countries must:
coordinate the planning and implementation of these projects;
cooperate with the European Commission to identify synergies whenever to do so makes sense from an overall economic, technical, environmental or spatial planning point of view and with due regard to the relevant safety aspects;
security of supply and security of supply;
ensure transparency and public participation in the decision-making procedures;
take the necessary measures to ensure that projects are approved on time.
In addition, EU countries should:
identify and designate European critical infrastructures as set out in Council Directive 2008/114/EC of 8 December 2008 on the identification and designation of European critical infrastructure and the assessment of the need to improve their protection;
provide the public with the necessary information and information on the status of the projects; and
take steps to ensure the transparency and participation of the public in the granting of permits, regulatory issues and financing.
The European Commission has adopted a communication on the priorities identified by the Commission for the energy infrastructure by 2020. It calls on EU countries to:
promote investment in energy infrastructure in line with the objectives of the Europe 2020 strategy;
improve the efficiency of the energy network;
increase the share of renewable energy in final energy consumption to 20 % by 2020;
reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 20 % and achieve a 20 % increase in energy efficiency by 2020 whereby energy efficiency gains may contribute to reducing the need for construction of new infrastructure. The Commission also calls on the Commission to adopt a new energy infrastructure policy to optimise network development at European level for the period up to 2020 to meet its core energy policy objectives of competitiveness, sustainability and security.
Background
The Commission has identified a number of strategic objectives for energy infrastructure. These include:
modernising and expanding the European electricity network and the interconnecting of gas pipelines and transmission networks across the EU;
improving the security of the supply of supply of electricity;
increasing the efficiency and reliability of the electricity transmission system;
contributing to the transition to a low-carbon economy.
For more information, see:
‘Energy Infrastructure Priority 2020 and Beyond’ on the website of the Commission.

Summary 160:
Alternative dispute resolution (ADR) for online sales and service contracts: online out-of-court resolution of disputes between consumers and traders (ODR)
Alternative dispute Resolution (ODR)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — European Online Dispute Resolution (ORA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European online dispute resolution platform for online disputes between traders and consumers.
It aims to improve the transparency of the EU’s alternative dispute resolution* (ADRs) market and to improve consumer protection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all online sales or service contracts where the trader, or the trader‘s intermediary, has offered goods or services through a website or by other electronic means and the consumer has ordered them on that website.
The ODR platform is open to all ADR entities listed in accordance with Article 20(2) of Directive 2013/11/EU. It is free of charge.
ADR entities must:
register with the platform;
provide an electronic case management tool;
conduct the dispute resolution procedure with the parties through the platform.
EU Member States must ensure that all traders established within the EU engaging in online sales* must provide, on their websites, an electronic link to the platform, and that traders are informed about the ADR procedures by which those traders are covered and about whether or not they commit to use them to resolve disputes with consumers;
ensure that the platform is accessible to the public and that are registered with the European Commission.
Advertising of ADRights and services on the ODRP is prohibited. ADRates are fixed at €10 per ADR entity.
Disputes between ADR e-commerce traders are not covered by Regulation (EC) No 45/2001 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 18 December 2000 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data by the European institutions and bodies and on the free movement of such data (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve online consumer protection, in particular:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2013 on alternative dispute resolutions for online commerce (Ora)
Directive 2013/12/EU on alternative disputes for online transactions (Otra)
Regulations (EC, EFTA and the European Central Bank)
Decision 2013/13/EU establishing the European Online Dispute Resolution Platform (OLDR) (Ostra)
Commission Implementing Decision 2013/23/EU setting up a European ODR Platform for Online Disputes (OLR) (OLRA).
KEY TERMS
* Online sale or service contract: a contract between a trader and a consumer for the provision of goods and services.
* ADR: an ADR procedure which allows traders to submit complaints against consumers for the purpose of resolving disputes over contractual obligations.

Summary 161:
Payments to farmers: Regulation (EC) No 73/2009 on the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) and Regulation (EU) 2019/2014 on the payment of EU support to farmers
Payments: Regulation(EC) no 73/2010 on the application of the Common Agricultural Policy (CAP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the basic elements of the EU’s common agricultural policy (CAP).
It aims to ensure that farmers receive the maximum amount of direct support from the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies from 1 January 2014.
Direct payments
The Regulation applies to payments to farmers in excess of €2,000 per hectare. It does not apply to:
direct payments to small farmers;
payments to medium-sized and large farmers.
Eligible area
EU Member States are allowed to apply certain limitations for the purpose of establishing the number of payment entitlements. These include:
the number of hectares of permanent grassland that are eligible for direct payments;
the maximum number of hectare of grazing land that are not covered by grazing land;
a number of legal persons who are not entitled to direct payments.
In addition to these limitations, Member States may decide to take into account historical factors when calculating the value of the payments which farmers should have in 2019, provided that no payments in 2019 have a value lower than 60 % of the average.
Payment entitlements
Member States have the right to decide how many payments they will grant to farmers. They may also decide not to grant direct payments to certain natural and legal persons, such as:
farmers who have not been granted direct payments in the previous year;
legal persons whose agricultural activity is not more than marginal;
contractors who do not have a contract with the European Commission.
The European Commission has the power to adopt certain acts in respect of the amendment of the list of eligible persons.
A number of derogations from the regulation apply to Croatia. These derogations are:
a reduction in the amount of payments to Croatia for the period 2018-2023;
an increase in the total number of direct payments granted to Croatia in the period 2019-2024.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The Commission adopted a Communication in 2009 on the implementation of the common Agricultural policy (COP) on the basis of a proposal from the Commission. The proposal was adopted in the light of the debate on that Communication.
For more information, see:
‘The European Agricultural Policy’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC) 2014/73/2009 of 21 December 2014 on the allocation of Union support for farmers (recast) (OJ L 304, 28.12.2014, pp. 1-8)

Summary 162:
Support for the smaller Aegean islands
Supporting the smaller islands
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1405/2006 establishing a support programme for the small and medium-sized islands of the eastern Mediterranean
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the EU’s support programme (SAP) for the islands of eastern and southern Greece.
KEY POINTS
EU policy to assist local production in the smaller Mediterranean islands has involved a multitude of products and measures for their production, marketing or processing. These have been implemented by means of the support programme, which constitutes an essential tool for supplying those islands with agricultural products and for supporting local agricultural production.
The SAP covers:
the supply of agricultural products for human consumption, for the manufacture of other products or as agricultural inputs;
the preservation and development of local agricultural activities, including the production, processing, marketing and transport of local raw and processed products.
In addition, the SAP is designed to:
encourage the production and marketing of quality products;
promote trade between the islands;
support the development of the local agri-food sector;
improve cooperation between the authorities of the islands and the European Commission;
ensure the protection of the intellectual property rights of the operators involved in the programme.
Support programme
The programme comprises:
a specific supply programme comprising:
specific supply arrangements for the EU's agricultural products listed in Annex I to the Treaty (‘the agricultural products’) which are essential in the small islands for the production of human consumption;
specific measures to assist the local production as provided for in Chapter IV;
local agricultural production measures, including measures to preserve and develop the local agricultural sector.
EU countries may supplement or modify certain non-essential elements of the regulation in respect of supplementing or modifying certain essential elements of this regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 23 April 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a long-term strategy to promote the development and sustainable use of the smaller eastern Mediterranean islands. The SAP was established in 2006.
For more information, see:
European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Support programme: a programme designed to provide financial support to Greece for the provision of specific agricultural products to the smaller, medium and small-sized Mediterranean islands (SAAIs).
* Specific supply arrangements: measures to guarantee supply to the SAAIs and compensate for the additional costs arising from their insularity, small size and distance from markets.

Summary 163:
Road transport statistics: EU-wide statistics
Road transport Statistics: EU countries’ statistical data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — statistical data on road transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, processing and transmission of statistics on the transport of goods by road in the European Union (EU).
It aims to provide a link between the carriage of goods and vehicle journeys by measuring the degree of utilisation of vehicles carrying out this transport.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries except Malta, so long as the number of Maltese-registered goods road transport vehicles licensed to engage in the international carriage of good by road does not exceed 400 vehicles.
EU countries must forward to Eurostat, on or before the date on which the first quarterly information is forwarded, a report on the methods used in compiling statistical data. The report must include:
the number of vehicles used for the survey;
the place of loading and unloading of the preceding laden and unladen journeys (notion of ‘place where unladen journey begins’);
the type of road transport vehicle (lorry or road tractor) in years (from its first registration);
load capacity*: maximum weight of goods declared permissible by the competent authority of the country of registration of the vehicle.
The European Commission is responsible for transmitting the statistical results. It must ensure that the methods for collecting and processing the data are sufficiently precise so as to enable the Commission to have at its disposal comparable, reliable, synchronised, regular and comprehensive statistical data, whilst taking into account the structural characteristics of the road transport in the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?
   
It entered into force on 12 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of the statistical data it collects.
For more information, see:
EU road transport statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Road transport: any road vehicle used to transport goods.
* Load capacity: maximum permissible weight, in 100 kg, of goods transported by road.

Summary 164:
Transmission and service of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil and commercial matters between EU countries
Transmitting and service in civil matters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000 on the service in the Member States of judicial or extrajudicial document transmission and service
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) system for the transmission and the service of legal documents between the EU countries.
It aims to facilitate the exchange of information, documents and information between the judicial authorities of EU countries and the postal services of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the transmission of judicial, extrajudicial and non-judicial documents to be served in another EU country;
the service of these documents to the addressee of a request for service.
Each EU country designates the public officers, authorities or other persons, hereinafter referred to as ‘receiving agencies’, competent for the receipt of judicial documents from another country.
The transmitting agencies are designated as:
transmitting agencies, which are the agencies designated pursuant to Article 2.1 of Decision 1999/468/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 June 1999 on the communication of judicial information to the European Judicial Network in Civil and Commercial Matters (EJN).
The European Commission must draw up a manual containing information relevant for the proper application of this regulation, which should be made available through the EJN.
Requests for service must be made using the standard forms set out in the annexes to the regulation. The applicant must be advised by the transmitting agency to which he forwards the document for transmission to the agency which forwards it to the applicant.
If the applicant refuses to accept a document, the applicant may refuse to accept it if it is not in one of the official languages of the place of service. If the applicant has refused to accept the document, it can be remedied through the service on the applicant in accordance with the provisions of the regulation, accompanied by a translation into a language provided for in paragraph 1 of the annex.
When a document is refused, it must be sent to those agents or persons who are competent to handle it.
In the event of non-compliance with the formal conditions required, the request and the documents transmitted must be returned, on receipt, to the sending agency, together with the notice of return, using a standard form.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 29 May 2000 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 29 May 2001.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures relating to judicial cooperation in civil, commercial and administrative matters.
For more information, see:

Summary 165:
Advanced therapy medicinal products — Regulation (EC) No 596/2006
Advanced therapy medicines — Regulation(EC) no 596(2006)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 550/2006 on the regulation of advanced therapy medicines
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the authorisation of the manufacturing and importation of advanced therapies* for human use, as well as the requirements for authorising the manufacturing or importing of such products.
It aims to ensure that the regulation does not interfere with decisions made by EU countries on whether to allow the use of any specific type of human cells, such as embryonic stem cells, or animal cells.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all advanced therapy medicinal* products. It does not apply to:
genetic therapies*;
somatic cell therapy* products;
tissue engineered products* for the purpose of regenerating, repairing or replacing a human tissue;
immuno-therapeutic products* intended for use in or administered to human beings.
Genetic therapies are defined in Annex I to Directive 2001/83/EC, while somatic cell therapies are in Annex II.
Tissue-engineered products are defined as a product that:
contains or consists of engineered cells or tissues, and
is presented as having properties for, or is used in or is administered to humans with a view to regenerate, repair or replace a human body.
is not intended to be used for the same essential function or functions in the recipient as in the donor.
Products containing or consisting exclusively of non-viable human cells and/or tissues, which do not contain any viable cells and tissues and do not act principally by pharmacological, immunological or metabolic action, are excluded from the scope of the regulation.
Authorisation
The European Medicines Agency’s Committee for Medicinal Products for Human Use is responsible for preparing a draft opinion on the quality, safety and efficacy of each advanced therapy product for final approval by the European Agency for the Safety and Health of Medicines (EMA).
The Committee for Advanced Therapies, with its unique expertise in this field, is consulted for the evaluation of any other medicinal product which requires specific expertise falling within its area of competence.
EU countries are required to report to the European Commission on the implementation of this regulation after experience has been gained, with a particular attention to the different types of products authorised.
Imports
The Commission must adopt a decision on the application for authorisation in the Member States of the European Economic Area (EEA) by 31 December 2018. The decision must be adopted in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny provided for in Article 5a of Decision 1999/468/EC.
Manufacturers and importers must ensure that their products comply with the rules laid down in the regulation and that they have the necessary technical and organisational know-how to ensure the safety and quality of the products they manufacture and import. They must also ensure that they comply with other EU rules on the manufacture, importation and labelling of medical devices.
The competent authority must also establish a risk management system to address risks related to advanced therapy products. This system must include:
a risk assessment of the risks associated with the manufacturing, import and use of the product;
the risk assessment and risk management of the marketing authorisation;
measures to be taken in the event of adverse reactions to the product.
In addition, the Commission must establish guidelines to be drawn up either by the Agency or by the Commission on good manufacturing practice in respect of medicinal products for human and investigational medicinal products, and adapted, where necessary, to reflect the specific nature of those products. These guidelines must be based on the principles and guidelines for good clinical practice as set out in Directive 2005/28/EC laying down principles and detailed guidelines for clinical practice in the field of investigational medicines.

Deadline for transposition in the Members States

Summary 166:
Clean air for Europe: type approval of motor vehicles and their trailers (2006-2020)
Clean air: type acceptance of motor cars and trailers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — type approval requirements for motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  
It lays down rules for the type approval* of vehicles and trailers in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the quality of the EU’s internal market and to reduce air pollution.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to vehicles of categories M1, M2, N1 and N2 as defined in Annex II to Directive 70/156/EEC with a reference mass not exceeding 2 610 kg.
It repeals Directive 98/69/EC (see summary) and its subsequent amendments.
Type approval procedures
Manufacturers must ensure that type approval procedures for verifying conformity, durability of pollution control devices, on-board diagnostic (OBD) systems, measurement of fuel consumption and accessibility of vehicle repair and maintenance information are met. They must also demonstrate that all new replacement pollution control device requiring type approval which are sold or put into service in the EU are type approved in accordance with this regulation and its implementing measures.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules for in-service conformity (i.e. that a vehicle meets all the requirements for type approval), durability and availability of the pollution control system in the vehicle, on board diagnostic (ODD) and in-vehicle emission control devices.
EU Member States must establish a standardised method of measuring fuel consumption, and carbon dioxide emissions of vehicles to ensure that no technical barriers to trade arise between Member States.
Member States must also ensure that:
vehicles are registered in a Member State with a minimum fuel consumption of less than 0.5 l/100 km/l;
the vehicle is registered in an EU Member State that does not have a type approval system;
there are no restrictions on the number of vehicles that can be registered in that Member State.
The Commission must keep under review emissions which are as yet unregulated and which arise as a consequence of the wider use of new fuel formulations, engine technologies and emission control systems and, where necessary, submit a proposal to the European Parliament and to the Council with a view to regulating such emissions.
Exceptions
Certain exceptions for vehicles designed to fulfil specific social needs should be foreseen in the Euro 5 stage. The regulation does not apply to certain types of light commercial vehicles, such as buses, coaches, trucks and ambulances, which are not covered by this regulation.

Summary 167:
Food safety — common procedures for substances
Food safety – common procedures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1334/2008 on food safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common procedure for the authorisation of substances* to be placed on the market or used in foodstuffs for human consumption.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to food additives, food enzymes and food flavourings.
It does not apply to smoke flavourings falling within the scope of Regulation (EU) No 2065/2003 on flavourings used or intended for use in or on foods (see summary).
Authorisation procedure
The European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) is responsible for assessing and authorising substances to be used in and on foods. It is assisted by the European Medicines Agency (EMA) and the European Commission.
Authorisations are granted on the basis of an independent scientific assessment of the risks that they pose to human health.
The application for authorisation must be made available to the EU countries by the EFSA and the Commission within 6 months of the application being received.
EU countries must consult the authority before authorising a new substance. The Commission may refuse to authorise a substance if it considers that it is likely to have an adverse effect on human health or the environment.
In addition, the Commission may adopt measures to remove a substance from the EU list of authorised substances. These measures must be adopted in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny provided for in Article 5a of Decision 1999/468/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 January 2002 laying down the general principles and requirements of food law.
Implementing measures
The Commission adopts implementing measures to amend non-essential elements of each sectoral food law relating to the removal of a substances from the Community list. The measures must comply with the procedures laid down in Article 4 of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND

For more information, see:

* KEY TERMS
* Food safety: a high level of protection of human life and health, including the protection of consumer interests, in order to ensure the free movement of food within the European Union (EU).
* Flavouring: a substance with flavouring properties, such as flavourings and certain food ingredients, which is intended to enhance the flavour of food.
Food additives: substances that have a flavouring effect on food and which are added to food or intended to be added to it.

Summary 168:
Accreditation and market surveillance of products from non-EU countries and the CE marking
Accreditation, market surveillance and controls of non-European Union (EU) products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1605/2002 establishing a framework of rules and principles in relation to accreditation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the accreditation of bodies carrying out conformity assessments in both the regulated and the non-regulated areas of the EU.
It aims to ensure that these bodies are recognised by the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to:
ensure that EU countries’ national conformity assessment bodies (NCBs) recognise each other’s accreditation;
establish a system of mutual recognition between national accreditation bodies;
provide for the exchange of information between the national authorities and the Commission;
improve the effectiveness of market surveillance authorities’ checks on dangerous non-conforming products.
Scope
The regulation applies to the following categories of products:
products with a CE marking (e.g. medical devices);
medical devices;
personal protective equipment;
electrical products;
industrial products; and
electromagnetic devices.
In addition, it applies to products which are not covered by the regulation and which are imported into the EU under the same conditions as those covered by it.
Rules on accreditation:
National accreditation body
The European Commission recognises one or more than one body as the competent national body for each category of product.
the body recognised under this regulation must:
apply for accreditation by the EU country in which issued the conformity assessment certificate;
request accreditation from the national body in which they are established;
apply to the Commission for the recognition of its accreditation.
The Commission may grant or refuse accreditation to a body if it considers that the body does not fulfil its responsibilities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 July 2002.
BACKGROUND

Summary 169:
Agreement between the EU and the Nordic countries on the surrender of suspects to the authorities of the EU countries
Agreement with the Nordic Countries on the cooperation of the judicial authorities in extradition matters
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2008/852/EC on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European Union and the Kingdom of Norway and the Republic of Iceland on the extradition of suspected criminals
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve the extradition system between, on the one hand, the Member States and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries: Norway, Iceland and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (1).
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Community (now the EU).
KEY POINTS
The extradition system is based on a mechanism of surrender pursuant to an arrest warrant.
The arrest warrant is issued by the judicial authority of the country which is competent to issue it by virtue of the law of that country. The issuing judicial authority may refuse to execute the warrant if:
the offence has been committed in whole or in part in the territory of the executing country or in a place treated as such;
the act was committed outside the country of the issuing country and the law does not allow prosecution for the same offences when committed outside its territory.
When the executing judicial authority refuses to execute a warrant, it must provide a description of the circumstances in which the offence was committed, including the time, place and degree of participation in the offence by the requested person, the penalty imposed, if there is a final judgment, or the prescribed scale of penalties for the offence.
If a person does not comply with the conditions of the arrest warrant, the country that issued the warrant may request that the person be handed over to the country where the custodial sentence or detention order has been passed against him or her.
Where a person refuses to comply with a request for extradition, the EU country which has issued the request may make a declaration to the effect that he or she will not be surrendered or that surrender will be authorised only under certain specified conditions.
In addition, the countries which have signed the agreement may request the European Security and Intelligence Service (SIS) to transmit all the information it receives to the European Judicial Network (EJN). The SIS is capable of transmitting all of the information described in the agreement to the EJN.
An arrest warrant may not be executed if the person concerned:
is a national of one of the countries covered by the agreement;
has been convicted of a criminal offence in another country;
is considered to be a threat to public order or public security.
EU countries can establish an obligation or an option to refuse to act on an execution warrant in the following cases:
if, in one of these cases, the act on which the warrant has been issued is regarded as having been committed:
in part or in whole in the country covered by it;
in a place which is not treated as a place of public security;
on the grounds of double criminality;
if the offence is punishable by a maximum sentence of at least 5 years’ imprisonment.
Other grounds for non-execution of the warrant include:
a lack of evidence of the offence being committed;
a breach of the secrecy of the person being sought;
an absence of a written record of the arrested person.
A person who refuses to cooperate with the authorities may be refused the right to appeal against the refusal.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
They have applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU and its EU countries have adopted a common extradition policy.
For more information, see:
Expedition cooperation (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision 2008/884/EC of 21 December 2008 on the signature, on behalf, of the agreement between the Member State of the one part, and the State of Norway, the Union of Iceland, and of the State responsible for the administration of justice in the field of extradition (OJ L 31, 1.1.2008, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to the Decision have been incorporated in to the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 25.02.2021



Summary 170:
Passenger rights on rail transport — Regulation (EC) No 552/2006
Passenger relations on rail transportation — Regulation(EC) no 552’06
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 550/2006 on passenger rights on railway transport
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that passengers’ rights are respected when travelling by rail in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the obligations of railway undertakings (i.e. railway companies) to passengers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all rail journeys and services throughout the EU provided by one or more railway companies. It does not apply to urban, suburban and regional rail passenger services.
Passengers are the weaker party to the transport contract.
Railway companies must ensure that:
all trains are accessible to persons with reduced mobility;
all buildings and rolling stock are made accessible through the progressive elimination of physical obstacles and functional hindrances when acquiring new material or carrying out construction or major renovation work;
there are adequate security measures in railway stations and on trains and to manage risk;
passengers’ personal security and personal security are guaranteed;
they have the right to be adequately insured and to have their luggage adequately insured;
the rights of passengers and their luggage are guaranteed.
EU countries may grant temporary exemptions from the application of the regulation to certain services or journeys because a significant part of the rail passenger service, including at least one scheduled station stop, is operated outside the EU.
In addition, EU countries may decide to grant a temporary exemption from the regulation if:
railway undertakings’ liability in the event of a major disruption to a rail passenger rail service;
railways, bridges, tunnels, bridges and tunnels are closed;
traffic management is carried out in accordance with the rules of the European Commission’s ‘Consumer Policy Strategy 2002-2006’.
The European Commission has adopted a communication on consumer protection in the field of transport. It calls on EU countries to encourage railway companies to put in place arrangements for compensation and assistance for passengers in the case of major disruption of a rail service.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Railways (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 555/2006 of 21 December 2006 on the passenger rights of railway companies (OJ L 183, 29.12.2006, pp. 1-8)

Summary 171:
Statistics in the field of education and lifelong learning: European statistical information system
Studies in education and learning
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1882/2003 establishing a common framework for the systematic production of European statistics in the area of education
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common statistical framework for European statistics on education, training, lifelong learning.
It aims to contribute to the Lisbon strategy and to establish comprehensive lifelong learning strategies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the European Union (EU) and its Member States’ (Eurostat’s) statistical information systems on education;
the United Nations International Institute for Statistics (UIS) and the OECD;
participation of the European Union in the OECD and the European Economic and Social Committee (Euratom);
the results of the pilot studies on education and training conducted between 2005 and 2007.
Data collection and transmission
Data and metadata must be supplied annually, if not otherwise specified, within the deadlines agreed between Eurostat and the national authorities taking into account the latest agreements between the UIS, OECD and Eurostat on concepts, definitions, data collection format, data processing, periodicity and deadlines for transmission of results.
EU countries’ capacities for data collection and the processing and the development of concepts and methods must be taken into account by the Commission.
The European Commission must consult the Statistical Programme Committee established by Council Decision 89/382/EEC, Euratom on the implementation of this regulation.
Quality and reliability
The Commission must ensure that the data collected and the results are reliable and comparable.
When implementing measures are adopted, they must be based on a cost-benefit analysis as part of a comprehensive analysis of the effects and implications, taking into consideration the benefit of the measures, the costs for the Member States and the burden on respondents. If these measures lead to a requirement for a significant enlargement of existing data collections or for new data collection or surveys, implementing decisions must be made in accordance with the principle of proportionality.

Summary 172:
Energy statistics
energy statistics
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 establishing a common framework for the production, transmission, evaluation and dissemination of comparable energy statistics in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for producing, transmitting, evaluating and disseminating energy statistics.
It aims to improve the comparability, transparency, flexibility and comparability of energy statistics across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out detailed rules concerning the reporting of the data needed for the national statistics by undertakings and other sources.
EU countries must compile all data specified in the regulation from the beginning of the calendar year following the adoption of this regulation, and transmit them from then onwards with the frequencies laid down in Article 4(1) of the regulation.
The European Commission may grant exemptions or derogations to EU countries from those aspects of the energy data collection that would lead to an excessive burden on respondents.
Every 5 years, EU countries must provide the Commission (Eurostat) with a report on the quality of data transmitted as well as on any methodological changes that have been made. The Commission may adopt measures, in accordance with the principle of subsidiarity as set out in Article 5 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU, to ensure that the data transmitted are reliable and up-to-date.
Each year, the Commission publishes a set of final energy consumption statistics. These include:
the annual nuclear statistics;
the renewable energy statistics, once incorporated, and
the set of the final consumption statistics, which are updated every 5 years.
the nuclear statistics which are based on the average annual nuclear generation capacity of EU countries;
energy consumption statistics which cover the total consumption of electricity and any other form of energy, such as gas, oil, electricity, water, heating, transport and heating.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
Energy statistics have traditionally been focused on energy supply and on fossil energies. However, in order to achieve the EU’s objectives of sustainable, competitive and secure energy, the European Commission has adopted a number of measures, including:
Decision No 280/2004/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 February 2004 concerning a mechanism for monitoring Community greenhouse gas emissions and for implementing the Kyoto Protocol (see summary);
Directive 2009/34/EC establishing a mechanism to monitor and monitor the greenhouse gas emission reductions of the Member States;
Decisions 2009/35/EC and 2009/36/EC concerning the establishment of a public domain energy forecast model.
For more information, see:
EU energy statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy statistics: data on the treatment or use of energy products, namely production, trade, stocks, transformation, consumption, and structural characteristics of energy system such as installed capacities for electricity generation or production capacities for oil products.
* Nuclear statistics: statistics on the annual nuclear capacity of nuclear power plants.

Summary 173:
Passenger rights of disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility in the transport of goods and passengers on buses and coaches (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Passenger relations with the transport sector
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the rights of passengers travelling by bus and coach
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all passengers travelling on public transport, whether due to disability, age or any other factor, should have opportunities for using public transport services that are comparable to those of other citizens.
It sets out the rights and non-discriminatory contract conditions applicable to passengers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to passengers travelling with occasional services where the initial boarding point or the final alighting point of the passenger is situated in the territory of an EU country. It does not apply in case where a package tour is cancelled for reasons other than cancellation of a regular service.
EU countries must ensure compliance with this regulation and designate a competent body or bodies to carry out supervision and enforcement tasks.
Passengers must be fully informed of their rights under this regulation, so that they can effectively exercise those rights.
Carriers must issue a ticket to the passenger, unless other documents give entitlement to transport.
The carriage of passengers must be free of charge.
Travel agents and tour operators must ensure that:
the passenger is accompanied by a travel agent or a tour operator who is able to provide assistance to the disabled person;
the disabled person is informed of the reasons therefor, and informs the person in writing within 5 working days of the request;
there is no discrimination based on nationality or on the place of establishment of the carriers, or ticket vendors within the EU.
Ticket prices must not exceed €220 per passenger;
travel agents and travel agents must provide assistance free to the passengers and terminal managing bodies at all times during the journey;
tour operators, travel agents or tour operators are responsible for ensuring that passengers are provided with assistance free at all stages of the journey, including during the check-in and check-out process.
Rights of passengers
The rights of the passengers must include the receipt of information regarding the service before and during their journey, and the right to be informed of any changes to the service, including the reasons for the cancellation of the service and the possibility of compensation.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules on:
access to the access conditions provided for in Article 11(1) of the annex to the regulation;
access conditions for disabled persons at the ticketing and ticketing terminals;
refugees’ access to the terminal and to the travel agent’s assistance free areas;
disability and reduced mobility assistance at the departure and alighting points.
Transport contract conditions
The contract conditions and tariffs applied by carriers must be offered to the general public without any direct or indirect discrimination, based on the nationality of the final customer or the place where the carriers are established.
These conditions must not be limited or waived, in particular by a derogation or restrictive clause in a transport contract.
Consumers must be informed and informed of these conditions.
Disabled persons must be provided with:
information regarding the conditions of the transport contract;
information on the service;
a description of the conditions relating to the mobility of the person concerned;
an explanation of the charges and charges applicable to the carriage of the persons covered by this regulation.
Exceptions to the above rules are allowed.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to ensure the protection of passengers’ rights, including:
Directive 2009/34/EC on the right of the general population to travel by public transport (see summary);
Regulations (EC) No 552/2004, (EC, Euratom) No 1082/2004 and (EC), No 1083/2004 on the access to public transport for persons with disabilities and for persons who have reduced mobility;
Directives (EC and Euratom).
For more information, see:
‘Passenger Rights’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation on the Rights of Passengers travelling by Bus and Coach (OJ L 157, 1.2.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Eur) 2019 / 852 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the production and transmission of statistics to the European Commission on the European Union (EU)’s fisheries statistics.

Scope

the report must be based on the quality and relevance of the statistical data.

Summary 175:
Control and enforcement of the North-East Atlantic Fisheries Commission (NEAFC) control and enforcement scheme (SES) (PDF)
Control and Enforcement of the NEAFC’s Control and Enforcement Scheme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 — general rules and conditions for the application by the European Union of the scheme adopted by the EU of the Convention on future multilateral cooperation in the north-east Atlantic fisheries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the general rules, conditions and procedures for applying by the Union (EU) of the convention-based scheme adopted in 2006.
It aims to ensure that fishing vessels flying the flag of EU countries and operating in the waters covered by the convention are subject to the control and enforceability of the regulation.
KEY POINTS
The convention provides for an appropriate framework for multilateral fishing cooperation on the rational conservation and management of fishery resources in the area defined by the Convention (the Convention Area).
The Convention provides for the establishment of a scheme of control and enforcing measures applicable to fishing vessels operating in waters beyond the waters under the fisheries jurisdiction of the contracting parties (the Regulatory Area). The Scheme provides for:
rules for inspection at sea, including inspection and surveillance procedures and infringement procedures which must be implemented by the Contracting Parties;
rules to promote compliance with the Convention;
procedures for inspection and monitoring of fishing activities in the Convention Area.
The regulation applies to:
Vessels authorised to fish directly for one or more regulated resources, i.e.
the list of the regulated resources referred to in the annex to the regulation;
the number of vessels and the number of fish on board.
EU countries’ responsibilities
The EU countries must cooperate with one another and with the European Commission and the body designated by it in applying the scheme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 15 November 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission adopted the regulation on behalf of the EU.
For more information, see:
‘North East Atlantic Fisheries Convention’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Convention Area: the area covered by a multilateral agreement between the European Economic Area (EEA) and its Member States, the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) and the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
* Regulated resources: those fishery resource which are subject of recommendations under the Convention and are listed in the Annex to the Regulation.

Summary 176:
Ecolabel: European Union (EU) rules
EU Ecolabel
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 834/2007 on the European Union’s ecolabel scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the setting of ecodesign requirements for energy-related products.
It aims to simplify the administrative burden associated with the use of the EU Ecolabelling scheme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all food and feed products, as well as unprocessed agricultural products that lie within the scope of Regulation (EC).
It does not apply to medicinal products for human use, as defined in Directive 2001/83/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 6 November 2001 on the Community code relating to medicines, or for veterinary use, or to any type of medical device.
The European Commission adopts measures to establish specific criteria for each product group. The Commission consults the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and the European Economic and Business Board (EIB) to determine which group of products are eligible for the ecolabelled status.
Requirements
The criteria must include requirements intended to ensure that the products bearing the EU environmental label function adequately in accordance with their intended use.
EU countries must ensure that competent bodies meet the requirements laid down in Annex V of the regulation.
Products that are certified as organic would not be eligible for award of an EU EColabel, to avoid confusion for consumers.
They must be accompanied by a product identification number (EICN) issued by the European Commission.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that the product meets the requirements set out in Annex VI;
apply for the Ecol label within 6 months of receiving an application from the EU country in which the product is registered;
assess the products’ environmental performance;
check that they comply with the requirements in Annex VII;
provide the competent body with all the relevant documentation;
conduct investigations to verify compliance with the criteria;
identify products that do not meet the criteria.
Ecolabels are awarded to products that fulfil all the requirements of the Regulation (Annex VIII).
The Commission may, no later than 9 months after consulting the EUEFSA and the EUEB, adopt measures to define specific criteria. The European Commission is responsible for the development and revision of the criteria and for any review of the implementation of the scheme. It may also invite any interested party to lead the development or revision of these criteria, provided that common procedural rules are followed and that the process is coordinated by the Commission. It must also consult the European Environment Agency (EEA).
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to modernise and streamline the EU ecolabilisation scheme. These include:
Directive 2009/125/EC establishing a framework for energy related products (see summary);
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2013 establishing a legal framework for ecodeign requirements in the context of Directive 2011/34/EU on energy efficiency;

Summary 177:
Protection of medicinal products — certificate of patent protection in the European Union (EU) (codified version)
Protected medicinal products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1901/2006 on the protection of patents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions for the granting of a certificate to a medicinal product protected by a patent to be placed on the market in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following products:
any medicinal product covered by a valid basic patent and for which the first authorisation to place it on the EU market was obtained after 1 January 2000, provided that the application for a certificate was lodged within 6 months from 1 May 2004;
any pharmaceutical product protected with a valid patent and obtained before 1 May 2000, except for Malta, which may be granted a certificate if it was granted before 1 July 2002.
It does not apply to certificates granted in accordance with their national legislation before 1 January 1995.
The duration of the certificate may not exceed 5 years from the date on which it takes effect.
An application for an extension of the duration may be made when lodging the certificate or when the application is pending and the appropriate requirements of Article 8(1)(d) or 8(2) of the regulation are fulfilled.
Where the applicant does not meet the conditions laid down in Article 8, the regulation, the authority must ask the applicant to rectify the irregularity, or to settle the fee, within a stated time.
If the applicant fails to do so, the applicant must inform the competent authorities within 3 months of the lapse of the valid certificate.
A certificate may be revoked on the request of a third party if it is found to be invalid. The authority may decide on the lapse on its own motion or at the request from a non-EU country.

Summary 178:
Convention on the jurisdiction of the courts of the European Union in civil and commercial matters (OJ L 183, 29.11.1973)
Convention of the Court of Justice of the International Chamber of Commerce on the exclusive jurisdiction of courts in matters relating to the law of the Member States of the Council of Europe
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — conclusion of the Convention on the Exclusive Jurisdiction of the Courts in Civil and Commercial Matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE CONVENTION AND OF THE DECISION?
The convention aims to ensure that the courts in the European Economic Area (EEA) have exclusive jurisdiction in matters of civil law and commercial law.
The decision concludes the convention on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The Convention applies to all EU countries. It does not extend, in particular, to revenue, customs or administrative matters.
Jurisdiction
The parties to a contract may be sued only in the court of the country in which the contract is concluded. This applies to:
contracts for the sale of goods on instalment credit terms;
contract for a loan repayable by instalments, or for any other form of credit, made to finance the purchase of goods;
a contract for the supply of goods to be sold or to be supplied by a person who pursues commercial or professional activities in the country of the consumer's domicile or, by any means, directs such activities to that state or to several States including that state, and the contract falls within the scope of such activities.
any other than those covered by the convention.
the parties may also be sued in the courts where the contract was entered into.
An insurer may bring proceedings only against a defendant, irrespective of whether he or she is the policyholder, the insured or a beneficiary of a contract, if the action may be combined with an action relating to rights in rem in immovable property, in the state where the property is situated.
Defendants may not bring proceedings against a third party in an action on a warranty or guarantee, or in any other third party proceedings, unless these were instituted solely with the object of removing him or her from the jurisdiction or the court which would be competent in his case.
Where the parties have entered into an agreement on jurisdiction on jurisdiction, the agreement may be departed from only by an agreement between the parties to the contract, provided that such an agreement is not contrary to the laws of that country.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The agreement entered into force on 29 November 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Convention for the Exemption of Courts of Justice in Civil And Commercial Matters was signed in Rome on 29 October 1973.
For more information, see:
The Hague Convention (European Commission).
